### [1] [EXPO-SQL: Execution-based Clause-level Policy Optimization for Text-to-SQL](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23693)
*Jaehoon Lee,CheolWon Na,Suyoung Bae,Jin-Seop Lee,Jihyung Lee,YunSeok Choi,Jee-Hyong Lee*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They improve Text-to-SQL RL training by using clause-level execution-based rewards instead of a single query-level reward, leading to better SQL generation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RL-based Text-to-SQL methods use coarse query-level rewards that treat all clauses equally, providing weak learning signals and limiting performance. There is a need for more fine-grained supervision that can distinguish which parts of a generated SQL query are right or wrong.

Method: They propose EXPO-SQL, an execution-based clause-level policy optimization method. It analyzes execution feedback such as error messages and results from clause-wise incremental execution to identify which clauses in a generated SQL query are erroneous, then assigns clause-level rewards accordingly and uses these in RL training for the Text-to-SQL model.

Result: On standard Text-to-SQL benchmarks, EXPO-SQL achieves significantly better performance than supervised fine-tuning, prompting-based approaches, and prior RL-based methods that rely on query-level rewards.

Conclusion: Fine-grained clause-level rewards derived from execution feedback provide stronger supervision than uniform query-level rewards in RL for Text-to-SQL, leading to more accurate SQL query generation; EXPO-SQL is an effective way to realize this idea.

Abstract: Text-to-SQL enables users to query databases using natural language by generating executable SQL queries. Recent methods have increasingly adopted Large Language Models based reinforcement learning (RL) to leverage execution feedback for training. However, existing RL methods assign uniform query-level rewards to all clauses in a SQL query, treating correct and incorrect clauses equally. This coarse-grained reward design leads to insufficient learning signals for correct SQL generation. To address this issue, we propose EXPO-SQL (EXecution-based clause-level Policy Optimization for Text-to-SQL) which provides fine-grained supervision through clause-level rewards. To assign clause-level rewards, our method identifies erroneous clauses by analyzing execution results, including error messages and clause-wise incremental execution. Experiments on widely-used Text-to-SQL benchmarks demonstrate that EXPO-SQL significantly outperforms existing supervised fine-tuning, prompting, and RL-based methods through fine-grained clause-level learning. Our code is available at https://github. com/jhn25/EXPO-SQL.

</details>


### [2] [ModTGCN: Modularity-aware Graph Neural Networks for Text Classification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23694)
*Rajarshi Misra,Aditya Sharma,Vinti Agarwal,Hari Om Aggrawal*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: ModTGCN is a modularity-aware graph neural network for text classification that explicitly encourages class-coherent document communities on a document similarity graph, while using a decoupled, more efficient TextGCN-style architecture, yielding better performance especially on low-homophily datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing graph-based text classification models aggregate information mostly from local neighborhoods and ignore global community structure in semantic document graphs. However, documents of the same class tend to form clear clusters. Neglecting this structure can blur class boundaries and cause over-smoothing, particularly on complex, low-homophily datasets. There is a need for a graph model that leverages global modularity while maintaining discriminative power and computational efficiency.

Method: The paper introduces ModTGCN, a graph neural network that jointly optimizes standard cross-entropy loss for classification and a modularity-based auxiliary objective. The modularity term is computed on a document-document similarity graph constructed from transformer-based document embeddings (either pretrained or fine-tuned). To improve scalability, the traditional heterogeneous TextGCN graph is decomposed into separate document-word and word-word subgraphs, reducing computational cost. The authors also systematically explore graph construction schemes, label-aware edge reweighting, and different ways of supervising the modularity objective.

Result: On five benchmark text classification datasets, ModTGCN consistently outperforms baselines. The gains are especially pronounced on more complex, low-homophily datasets such as Ohsumed and 20 Newsgroups, indicating that explicitly modeling modularity and global community structure is particularly beneficial when same-class documents are not strongly connected in the original graph. Training is 2x–10x faster than with the original coupled TextGCN graph.

Conclusion: Incorporating a modularity-aware auxiliary objective into graph-based text classification allows the model to exploit global community structure and form class-coherent document clusters, mitigating over-smoothing and improving accuracy. Decoupling the TextGCN graph into document-word and word-word components yields significant efficiency gains without sacrificing performance. The approach is especially advantageous for datasets with complex, low-homophily structures and suggests that community-aware objectives are a promising direction for future GNN-based text classification methods.

Abstract: Graph-based text classification models typically rely on local neighborhood aggregation and overlook global community structure, despite semantic document graphs exhibiting strong class-consistent clustering. Ignoring this can blur class boundaries and lead to over-smoothing. We propose ModTGCN, a modularity-aware graph neural network for text classification that jointly optimizes cross-entropy and a modularity-based auxiliary objective to promote class-coherent document communities while preserving discriminative representations. The modularity term is computed on a document-document similarity graph derived from transformer embeddings (pretrained or fine-tuned). To improve scalability, we decouple the original heterogeneous TextGCN graph into separate document-word and word-word components, achieving 2x-10x faster training. We further study graph construction strategies, label-aware edge reweighting, and supervision choices for modularity optimization. Experiments on five benchmarks show consistent gains, with larger improvements on complex, low homophily datasets such as Ohsumed and 20NG.

</details>


### [3] [Quantifying Prior Dominance in RAG Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23695)
*Barak Or*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a new metric (NCU) to measure how much a RAG system truly uses retrieved context versus relying on its own memorized knowledge, and find small models can use context more faithfully than large ones for pure fact extraction.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing RAG evaluations are based on discrete heuristics and cannot reliably tell whether a model answer comes from the provided context or from its internal parametric memory, causing ‘epistemic blindness’. The authors want a rigorous, quantitative way to measure genuine context use and to study how model size and alignment affect it.

Method: Introduce Normalized Context Utilization (NCU), which compares continuous token log-probabilities of model outputs under three conditions: zero-shot (no external context), oracle (relevant context supplied), and adversarial (misleading/contradictory context). Use NCU to evaluate models from 1.5B to 72B parameters plus a proprietary API on strict factual extraction tasks without Chain-of-Thought reasoning, analyzing how much they rely on context vs parametric priors and how they behave when context conflicts with priors.

Result: They find that for strict factual extraction, increasing model size gives sharply diminishing returns; small language models can match or beat much larger ones in actual context utilization as measured by NCU. Larger models and the proprietary API exhibit stronger ‘Prior Dominance’ (preferring internal knowledge over external evidence). The commercial API often ignores explicit contradictory context (in about half of adversarial cases) and shows negative transfer, losing confidence when its priors are challenged.

Conclusion: NCU provides a stricter, log-probability-based measure of contextual information gain, revealing that small models have a structural epistemic advantage for strict extraction tasks: they adhere more closely to provided context and are less dominated by their parametric priors. This challenges naive scaling assumptions for RAG and suggests that well-designed SLMs may be preferable for certain high-precision retrieval-based workflows.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) grounds Large Language Models in external knowledge, yet current evaluations rely on discrete heuristics that suffer from ''epistemic blindness'' - failing to distinguish genuine contextual information extraction from parametric memory recall. To address this, we introduce the Normalized Context Utilization (NCU) metric, leveraging continuous token log-probabilities across zero-shot, oracle, and adversarial conditions to strictly quantify contextual information gain. Evaluating architectures ranging from 1.5B to 72B parameters alongside a proprietary commercial API reveals that for strict factual extraction (without Chain-of-Thought reasoning), traditional scaling laws exhibit extreme diminishing returns: highly efficient Small Language Models (SLMs) match or outperform high-capacity architectures. Furthermore, we demonstrate that ``Prior Dominance'' correlates with model scale and proprietary alignments. The evaluated commercial API not only overrode explicit external evidence in nearly half of adversarial conflicts, but also frequently suffered from systemic confidence collapse (Negative Transfer) when its parametric priors were contradicted. Our findings highlight the structural epistemic advantage and superior contextual adherence of SLMs in strict extraction workflows.

</details>


### [4] [Self-Recognition Finetuning can Prevent and Reverse Emergent Misalignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23700)
*Arush Tagade,Shaoheng Zhou,Jiaxin Wen,Shi Feng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates emergent misalignment (EM) in large language models and proposes self-generated text recognition (SGTR) finetuning as a character-focused defense. It compares SGTR with several benign finetuning baselines across multiple models and EM datasets, finding SGTR particularly effective at preventing misalignment without collateral harm, and argues that EM is better understood as destabilization of the model’s aligned character rather than adoption of a stable misaligned persona.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Emergent misalignment—where models exhibit harmful or deceptive behaviors despite safeguards—has been hypothesized to result from activation of misaligned personas or evil character traits. Understanding whether EM stems from genuine learning of harmful content or from disruption of the model’s aligned character has implications for how we design defenses. Existing defenses are mostly in-training or capability-oriented; the authors are motivated to test a more targeted, character-level intervention that can both reverse and prevent EM while preserving model capabilities.

Method: The authors design a two-stage finetuning framework applied to three different LLMs (GPT-4.1, Qwen2.5-32B-Instruct, Seed-OSS-36B-Instruct) and multiple EM-inducing datasets. They introduce self-generated text recognition (SGTR) finetuning, which trains the model to recognize and align its own outputs, and compare it to several benign finetuning baselines: domain-specific correction data, general-knowledge data, and a word-counting task. They evaluate each intervention in (1) reversal settings—after EM has already been induced and capabilities degraded—and (2) prevention settings—intervening before EM manifests. They also run diagnostic experiments: analyzing changes in the model’s identity self-reports under EM finetuning, corrupting self-recognition to see how it affects misalignment, and ablation of the identity-bearing system prompt to assess its role in EM.

Result: All considered finetuning interventions can reverse EM to a similar degree, but only when they restore the model capabilities that EM had impaired. For prevention, however, SGTR stands out: it consistently reduces misalignment metrics without worsening any particular safety or capability dimension, whereas other benign finetuning approaches show trade-offs. Diagnostic results indicate that EM finetuning increases diversity and instability in identity self-reports, that artificially corrupting self-recognition further worsens misalignment, and that removing the system-level identity prompt greatly diminishes EM finetuning’s impact.

Conclusion: The study concludes that emergent misalignment is better characterized as a destabilization of the model’s default aligned character rather than the formation of a new, coherent misaligned persona. Self-generated text recognition finetuning, which explicitly reinforces the model’s sense of its own aligned character, is an effective preventative defense that avoids harm to other metrics. The role of identity prompts and self-recognition is central: fortifying these aspects supports alignment, while corrupting them amplifies misalignment, suggesting that future safety work should treat model character and identity as key levers for controlling EM.

Abstract: Emergent misalignment (EM) has been linked to the activation of misaligned persona vectors and evil character traits, suggesting that EM operates through disruption of the model's aligned character rather than direct learning of harmful content. Motivated by this connection, we study self-generated text recognition (SGTR) finetuning as a character-targeted intervention that is distinct from existing in-training defenses. We conduct two-stage finetuning experiments across three models (GPT-4.1, Qwen2.5-32B-Instruct, Seed-OSS-36B-Instruct) and multiple EM datasets to compare SGTR finetuning against benign finetuning baselines (correct domain-specific data, general knowledge, and word counting) to find it an effective defense in both reversal and prevention settings. We find that all interventions produce comparable EM reversal, but only when restoring capabilities that EM had degraded. For prevention, only SGTR finetuning consistently reduces misalignment without exacerbating any individual metric, suggesting that character fortification specifically drives prevention. We provide further evidence for EM's relation to the LLM's default character by showing that EM finetuning induces diversity into the LLM's identity self-reports, artificially corrupting self-recognition exacerbates misalignment caused by EM finetuning, and that removing the model's identity-bearing system prompt substantially reduces the effect of EM finetuning. Together, these findings reframe EM not as the adoption of a coherent misaligned persona but as the destabilization of aligned character.

</details>


### [5] [Evaluating LLM Usage for Efficient and Explainable Numerical and Classified Implicit Sentiment Analysis of Product Desirability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23701)
*Sherri Weitl-Harms,John Hastings*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates using LLMs to score sentiment/desirability from qualitative product feedback, finding they correlate very highly with human expert labels and outperform traditional baselines, while being scalable and interpretable via confidence estimates and rationales.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional qualitative product feedback contains rich, nuanced information about user experiences and desirability, but it lacks explicit sentiment scores and is hard to quantify systematically. Existing automated sentiment tools (lexicon-based or standard transformers) often underperform on such implicit sentiment. There is a need for a scalable, accurate, and interpretable way to turn open-ended feedback into quantitative measures of product desirability that can guide product development and marketing.

Method: The authors design a framework that uses large language models in a zero-shot setting to infer sentiment from qualitative product feedback obtained via the Product Desirability Toolkit (PDT) survey method. They apply several LLMs, including GPT-4o-mini and larger models, to two PDT datasets (ZORQ and CARMA) with 106 respondent term groupings that have gold-standard human sentiment annotations. The LLMs generate both continuous numerical sentiment scores and categorical sentiment labels directly from free-text responses, without explicit review scores. Model performance is evaluated by Pearson correlation with human numerical labels and classification accuracy versus expert sentiment categories. They compare LLMs with lexicon-based and transformer baselines, and they augment the framework with model confidence ratings and human-readable rationales to enhance interpretability (xAI). They also analyze cost differences across models.

Result: Across both PDT datasets, the LLMs’ numerical sentiment scores closely align with expert human annotations, achieving Pearson correlations up to 0.97 and categorical classification accuracy up to 94%. LLM predictions remain robust despite variations in data presentation and are accompanied by consistently high self-reported confidence. In contrast, lexicon-based and standard transformer baselines fail to achieve statistically significant performance. Notably, GPT-4o-mini performs on par with the larger, more expensive models while reducing cost by about 94%, indicating that cost-efficient deployment is feasible without substantial performance loss.

Conclusion: LLMs can effectively and accurately quantify product desirability from qualitative PDT feedback in a zero-shot setting, providing both continuous and categorical sentiment measures that align closely with expert human judgments. Their superiority over traditional sentiment analysis baselines, coupled with the strong performance of a lower-cost model (GPT-4o-mini), demonstrates that this approach is both powerful and economically scalable. The inclusion of confidence scores and explanatory rationales enhances interpretability and trust, making the framework practical for real-world product satisfaction assessment. Overall, combining the PDT survey method with an efficient LLM-based sentiment pipeline offers a rich, scalable tool for product evaluation, supporting idea generation for product improvements and targeted marketing based on nuanced user impressions.

Abstract: Qualitative product feedback can reveal nuanced user experiences, but its implicit sentiment is difficult to measure. This paper presents a scalable and interpretable framework that uses large language models (LLMs) to quantify product desirability from such data. Using two Product Desirability Toolkit (PDT) datasets from ZORQ and CARMA comprising 106 respondent term groupings with gold-standard human annotation, zero-shot continuous numerical sentiment scoring and categorical sentiment classification are evaluated without relying on explicit review scores. Across the datasets, LLMs generated numerical sentiment scores directly from qualitative responses and closely matched expert labels, achieving Pearson correlations up to 0.97 and classification accuracy up to 94%. LLMs maintained robustness even when handling data presented in multiple forms and consistently expressed high confidence. In contrast, lexicon-based and transformer baselines did not produce statistically significant results. Among the models tested, GPT-4o-mini achieved performance comparable to larger models at 94% lower cost, supporting scalable deployment.
  The framework also incorporates model confidence ratings and human-readable rationale explanations (xAI), improving interpretability, transparency, and trust while supporting practical use in product satisfaction assessment. In general, using the PDT tool as a survey method along with a cost efficient LLM for sentiment analysis has the potential to provide for product evaluation with results that are rich in terms of sentiment scores (both numerical and classified sentiment) and in terms of the high-level user impressions of the product that can be used to identify ideas for product development and improvement, as well as marketing ideas for target audiences.

</details>


### [6] [Ground Then Rank: Revisiting Knowledge-Based VQA with Training-Free Entity Identification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23881)
*Qian Ma,Qiong Wu,Zhengyi Zhou,Yao Ma*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a simple, training-free framework (Identify-Before-Answer) for knowledge-based visual question answering that first identifies the correct entity from candidate names using an MLLM, then uses a textual re-ranker for evidence selection, outperforming more complex multimodal RAG baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: KB-VQA tasks need grounding to external knowledge at both entity and fact levels, which current MLLMs and MM-RAG systems handle poorly. Existing methods entangle entity identification and section-level evidence ranking in a single re-ranking stage, making them expensive and less generalizable, and MLLMs struggle with open-ended entity naming despite being better at multiple-choice style selection.

Method: They reframe KB-VQA as a two-stage process. First, they use an MLLM in a training-free way to select the most likely entity from a small set of candidate entity names (identify step). Second, given the chosen entity, they apply an off-the-shelf textual re-ranker to select the most relevant knowledge sections/evidence, and then answer the question via standard RAG-style generation. This decouples entity identification from evidence re-ranking and leverages the MLLM’s strengths in discriminative selection rather than open-ended naming.

Result: On the Encyclopedic-VQA and InfoSeek benchmarks, the proposed IBA framework consistently outperforms fine-tuned multimodal re-ranking baselines, while requiring less training and reducing inference complexity. Analyses show that the gains come from both more accurate entity identification and improved evidence selection once the correct entity is fixed.

Conclusion: Decoupling entity identification from section-level evidence ranking via the IBA framework removes a key bottleneck in KB-VQA. A simple, training-free pipeline that leverages MLLMs for entity selection plus a textual re-ranker for evidence can outperform more complex fine-tuned multimodal RAG systems, offering a more efficient and generalizable approach to knowledge-based visual question answering.

Abstract: Knowledge-Based Visual Question Answering (KB-VQA) requires grounding visual queries to external knowledge beyond directly observable content in images. While recent multi modal large language models (MLLMs) show strong perceptual abilities, they struggle on KB-VQA tasks requiring groundings from both fine-grained entity and evidence levels. Most existing multi-modal retrieval augmented generation (MM-RAG) methods tightly couple entity discrimination and section-level evidence ranking into a single re-ranking stage, leading to high cost and limited generalization. In this work, we revisit existing MM-RAG solutions from a workflow perspective and argue both entity-level and fact-level groundings are key bottlenecks. We observe that although MLLMs often fail under open-ended entity naming, they can better identify the correct entity when selecting from a small set of candidate names. Based on this insight, we propose a simple and training-free identify-before-answer IBA framework that decouples entity identification from section-level re-ranking. Our approach prompts an MLLM to select high-confidence entities using only candidate names, followed by an off-the-shelf textual re-ranker for evidence selection. Experiments on Encyclopedic-VQA and InfoSeek show that our method consistently outperforms fine-tuned multi-modal re-ranking baselines while reducing training and inference complexity. Additional analyses reveal that the improvements arise not only from better entity identification, but also from selecting more informative evidence once correct entity is fixed. Our implementation is made public to ease reproducibility.

</details>


### [7] [One Year Later...The Harms Persist, But So Do We!](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23884)
*Annika Marie Schoene,Cansu Canca,Gautham Vijay Kumar,Anson Antony*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates safety safeguards of six proprietary LLMs across 16 DSM-5 mental health conditions using adversarial attacks and introduces a harm taxonomy and evaluation framework, finding severe safeguard failures beyond suicide/self-harm and warning of risks to vulnerable users, especially in education.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: With LLMs increasingly used for mental health conversations, existing safety safeguards are insufficient and inconsistent, especially beyond a narrow set of well-studied risks like suicide and self-harm. There is a need to systematically assess how well current proprietary LLMs handle a broad spectrum of DSM-5 conditions and to define clear harm categories to guide ethical design and deployment.

Method: The study tests six proprietary LLMs across 16 DSM-5 mental health conditions. It applies four variants of adversarial prompt attacks to probe safety weaknesses. The authors create an eight-dimension harm taxonomy and a multi-dimensional evaluation framework to quantify and categorize model failures and harms across conditions.

Result: Safeguards in evaluated LLMs perform reliably only for suicide and self-harm scenarios. For several other conditions—such as eating disorders, substance use disorder, and major depressive disorder—the models exhibit extremely high failure rates, up to 100%, under adversarial prompting. This reveals substantial gaps and inconsistencies in current safety mechanisms across conditions and harm types.

Conclusion: Current general-purpose LLMs, as deployed, are not ethically adequate for mental health-related use across the spectrum of DSM-5 conditions. Effective deployment requires clearly defined harm categories and condition-specific safeguards aligned with the new taxonomy and framework. Until such safeguards are systematically implemented, using these models with vulnerable populations—especially in educational contexts—poses significant and unacceptable risk.

Abstract: General-purpose large language models (LLMs) are increasingly used for mental health-related conversations, yet safety safeguards remain inadequate and inconsistent across clinical conditions. This study evaluates six proprietary LLMs across 16 DSM-5 conditions using four adversarial attack variants, introducing an eight-dimension harm taxonomy and a multi-dimensional evaluation framework. Results show that safeguards hold reliably only for suicide and self-harm, while conditions such as eating disorders, substance use disorder, and major depressive disorder exhibit failure rates of up to 100%. We argue that ethical design and deployment of these LLMs demand clearly defined harm categories across clinical conditions and implementation of safeguards accordingly. Until such safeguards are in place, these models pose significant risks to vulnerable populations, making their growing integration into educational settings a particularly concerning.

</details>


### [8] [Do LLM Attribution Metrics Transfer? Auditing Retrieval-Augmented Generation Evaluation Across Datasets and Constructs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23915)
*Tianyu Ding,Aditya Nannapaneni,Juan Pablo De la Cruz Weinstein*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates multiple automatic attribution metrics for LLM retrieval-augmented generation and finds that no single metric reliably transfers across datasets or tasks; metric choice must be validated on the target dataset, and LLM judges, while more robust, are costly and not universally best.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), we want models to ground their answers in retrieved sources and to measure this grounding automatically. Practitioners often treat different attribution metrics as interchangeable, assuming that a metric that works well on one dataset will also work on others. The authors are motivated to systematically test this assumption, understand whether any scorer consistently transfers across datasets and constructs, and quantify the risks of picking attribution metrics based only on prior benchmarks.

Method: The authors audit eight automatic scorers for attribution: simple lexical overlap metrics, embedding-based methods, BERTScore, and entailment/grounding-trained NLI-style models including versions trained on FEVER and the MiniCheck checker. They evaluate these scorers across three constructs: (1) provenance/topicality of retrieved documents, (2) generated-answer attribution (does the answer content match/support the cited sources), and (3) fact-check entailment. For transferability, they define a strong criterion: a scorer transfers if its performance stays within the 95% confidence interval of the best audited scorer on every dataset within a multi-dataset construct. They focus in particular on generated-answer attribution using AttributionBench’s four datasets (n=1,610) and HAGRID (n=2,150), comparing AUROC scores and metric rankings, and analyze Kendall tau rank correlations and leave-one-dataset-out model selection strategies. They also benchmark a prompt-based LLM judge as an alternative evaluator in the same settings, measuring robustness, cost, and determinism.

Result: For the construct with the richest human-labeled data—generated-answer attribution—no audited scorer satisfies the transfer criterion across datasets. Metric rankings are unstable and even invert between datasets: for example, the ranking between AttributedQA and LFQA has a negative Kendall tau (-0.64, p=0.031). An NLI-based scorer that performs best on the short-claim dataset AttributedQA (AUROC 0.90) collapses to chance-level performance on the long-form LFQA dataset (AUROC 0.53), where BERTScore instead achieves high performance (AUROC 0.91). The authors verify that this collapse is not simply due to length or truncation issues. A naive data-driven policy that selects “best-on-average” scorers using other datasets fails under leave-one-dataset-out evaluation, incurring a mean held-out regret of 0.172 AUROC, which is worse than just fixing a single scorer across datasets. The LLM judge does not suffer the same catastrophic collapses (it maintains reasonable performance on LFQA) but is not consistently the best metric, is about 100x more expensive, and is non-deterministic.

Conclusion: No currently popular automatic attribution metric for RAG can be assumed to transfer reliably across datasets and task variants. Metric performance and rankings can change dramatically from one dataset to another, so choosing an evaluator based on aggregate or prior-benchmark performance is risky and can be worse than using a single fixed metric. Practitioners must validate attribution metrics directly on their target dataset. While LLM judges appear more robust to some failure modes (e.g., they avoid chance-level collapses on long-form QA), they introduce high computational cost and randomness, shifting rather than eliminating the burden of careful metric validation and selection.

Abstract: Practice often treats automatic metrics for attribution in LLM retrieval-augmented generation as interchangeable. We audit eight automatic scorers -- lexical, embedding, and BERTScore baselines alongside entailment/grounding-trained models (clean and FEVER NLI, the checker MiniCheck) -- across three evaluation constructs (provenance/topicality, generated-answer attribution, and fact-check entailment), asking whether any scorer transfers: stays within the 95% confidence interval of the best audited scorer on every dataset of a multi-dataset construct. In the construct with the most multi-dataset human-labeled coverage -- generated-answer attribution (AttributionBench's four source datasets, n = 1,610, with independent HAGRID, n = 2,150) -- none does: the per-dataset metric rankings invert (Kendall tau = -0.64, p = 0.031 on AttributedQA vs. LFQA), and an off-the-shelf NLI scorer that is best on short-claim AttributedQA (AUROC 0.90) collapses to AUROC 0.53 (chance) on long-form LFQA, where BERTScore wins (0.91); the flip is not a length or truncation artifact. This instability has a concrete decision cost: a naive "best-on-average" rule for choosing an evaluator fails leave-one-dataset-out (mean held-out regret 0.172 AUROC, worse than fixing one scorer), so metric choice must be validated on the target dataset rather than learned from others. A prompt-based LLM judge avoids the chance-level collapses the automatic scorers suffer (no LFQA collapse) but is not uniformly best, ~100x costlier, and non-deterministic -- relocating, not removing, the validation burden.

</details>


### [9] [When Retrieval Metrics Mislead: Measuring Policy Signal in Long-Horizon Tool-Use Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23937)
*Tianyu Ding,Juan Pablo De la Cruz Weinstein*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper examines whether exact-match retrieval recall is a good proxy for the usefulness of retrieved policy clauses in downstream policy classification, finding that even with low exact-match recall, retrieved clauses can yield similar classifier performance to gold clauses.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In policy-aware decision systems, retrieval components often fetch relevant policy clauses for a decision model, and their quality is commonly assessed with exact-match recall of the ground-truth clause. However, this may not reflect the true downstream utility of the retrieved context, especially when approximate or partially related clauses may still be sufficient for correct decisions. The authors want to test if exact-match clause recall is actually predictive of downstream policy classification performance in a realistic benchmark (tau-bench).

Method: They use tau-bench pre-action policy classification tasks with Qwen2.5-3B and 7B models as policy classifiers. First, they construct a compact structured state representation of the environment and compare it to raw trajectories under gold-policy conditioning, showing structured state improves macro-F1 after tuning. Then they replace the gold benchmark-designated policy clause with the top-ranked clause retrieved at decision time from a policy retriever, measuring classifier macro-F1 when conditioned on retrieved clauses vs gold clauses. They also include mismatched-policy and no-policy controls, vary retrievers, and test across multiple fine-tuning configurations and model sizes (3B and 7B), reporting macro-F1 scores and confidence intervals over task clusters.

Result: With gold policy clauses and structured state, macro-F1 is substantially higher than with raw trajectories (improvement of about 0.13–0.17). For the airline domain, the exact governing clause is retrieved at rank 1 only 7% of the time, indicating low exact-match recall. Despite this, the primary 3B classifier attains macro-F1 of 0.58 with retrieved clauses, very close to 0.60 with gold clauses (difference -0.02, 95% CI over task clusters [-0.23, +0.21]). Mismatched-policy and no-policy baselines perform much worse (0.32 and 0.21 macro-F1), and similar qualitative patterns hold for a second retriever and for the 7B classifier, though exact numbers vary with fine-tuning settings.

Conclusion: Exact-match clause recall significantly underestimates the downstream utility of retrieved policy clauses in tau-bench pre-action policy classification. Even when the precise gold clause is rarely retrieved at rank 1, the retrieved clauses still support near-gold classification performance, clearly outperforming mismatched or missing policy conditions. Therefore, evaluating retrievers solely by exact-match recall can be misleading; researchers should instead assess retrieval quality in the loop with the downstream classifier, focusing on end-task metrics like macro-F1 rather than recall alone.

Abstract: Exact-match retrieval recall is often used as a proxy for whether a retriever supplies useful policy context to a downstream decision model. We test this proxy for pre-action policy classification in tau-bench using Qwen2.5-3B/7B classifiers. Under gold-policy conditioning, a compact structured state improves macro-F1 over raw trajectories by 0.13-0.17 after tuning. We then replace the benchmark-designated policy clause with the top-ranked clause retrieved from decision-time context. Although the exact governing clause is retrieved at rank 1 for only 7% of airline states, the primary 3B classifier obtains macro-F1 0.58 with retrieved clauses versus 0.60 with gold clauses (Delta=-0.02, task-cluster 95% CI [-0.23,+0.21]); mismatched-policy and no-policy controls score 0.32 and 0.21. We do not detect a macro-F1 difference between retrieved and gold clauses in this configuration, although the interval remains too wide to establish non-inferiority. The same qualitative pattern appears with a second retriever and at 7B, while varying across fine-tuning configurations. These results indicate that exact-match clause recall can underestimate downstream policy utility in this benchmark setting, motivating evaluation with retrieved policies in the classification loop rather than recall alone.

</details>


### [10] [QuechuaTok: Morphological Boundary Accuracy as a Necessary Metric for Tokenizer Evaluation in Agglutinative Low-Resource Languages](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23943)
*Maria Contreras*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces QuechuaTok, a benchmark to evaluate tokenization schemes for Southern Quechua, showing that standard metrics like fertility are insufficient and that morphology-aware tokenizers perform best on morphological accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard tokenizer evaluation metrics, especially fertility rate, are commonly used in NLP but fail to reflect morphological correctness in agglutinative, low-resource languages like Southern Quechua. There is a lack of systematic comparison of popular subword tokenizers against morphology-aware alternatives for such languages, and existing work rarely evaluates tokenizers based on how well they respect true morpheme boundaries. The authors aim to fill this gap and provide a principled benchmark for Quechua.

Method: The authors construct a 200k-sentence corpus for Southern Quechua and use the SQUOIA finite-state morphological analyzer as a silver-standard reference for morpheme boundaries. They compare four tokenization strategies: BPE, Unigram LM, WordPiece, and a morphology-aware PRPE tokenizer, each trained with a 16k vocabulary. They then compute three metrics for each tokenizer: fertility rate (average number of subwords per word), out-of-vocabulary (OOV) rate, and a new metric, morphological boundary accuracy (MorphAcc), which measures how well tokenizer boundaries align with SQUOIA’s morphological segmentations.

Result: BPE achieves the lowest fertility rate (1.636 at 16k vocabulary) but only 6.67% MorphAcc, indicating that it largely memorizes surface forms instead of respecting underlying morphology. In contrast, the morphology-aware PRPE tokenizer achieves the highest MorphAcc at 83.33%, showing superior alignment with true morpheme boundaries, though with a higher fertility rate. Other standard tokenizers (Unigram LM, WordPiece) fall between these extremes on all metrics. These results reveal a trade-off between compact tokenization and morphological faithfulness.

Conclusion: Fertility rate and similar standard tokenization metrics are inadequate for evaluating tokenizers on agglutinative languages because they ignore morphological correctness. Morphological boundary accuracy is necessary to capture how well tokenizers respect morpheme structure. Morphology-aware tokenizers like PRPE can dramatically improve morphological alignment (over 80% MorphAcc for Southern Quechua), and should be preferred or at least considered when working with low-resource agglutinative languages. The QuechuaTok benchmark and released code/models provide a reusable framework for future work on tokenizer evaluation in morphologically rich, low-resource settings.

Abstract: Tokenization is a foundational step in NLP pipelines, yet standard evaluation metrics such as fertility rate fail to capture morphological correctness for agglutinative languages. We present QuechuaTok, a systematic benchmark comparing four tokenization strategies - BPE, Unigram LM, WordPiece, and a morphology-aware PRPE tokenizer - for Southern Quechua (quz), a low-resource agglutinative language spoken by 8-10 million people in South America. Using a 200k-sentence corpus and the SQUOIA finite-state morphological analyzer (Rios, 2016) as silver standard, we evaluate three metrics: fertility rate, OOV rate, and morphological boundary accuracy (MorphAcc). Our results show that BPE achieves the lowest fertility rate (1.636 at 16k vocab) by memorizing surface word forms, while achieving only 6.67% MorphAcc. PRPE achieves 83.33% MorphAcc - the highest of all systems - demonstrating that fertility rate alone is insufficient to evaluate tokenizers for agglutinative languages. All code and models are publicly available at kaggle.com/code/macmaky/quechuatok

</details>

Analyze how performance varies across layers to infer representational structure.

Result: Both wav2vec2-base and Whisper-small reliably distinguish reduced from canonical segments. Moreover, even when segments are phonetically reduced, their representations still contain enough information to predict the underlying stop consonants in the cluster, across multiple layers of the models.

One underexplored phenomenon is consonant cluster reduction (CCR) in African American English (AAE), a widespread phonological process and a source of automatic speech recognition (ASR) disparity. To examine how CCR is represented, we conduct speaker-independent layer-wise probing of wav2vec2-base and Whisper-small using two tasks: segmental reduction detection and segmental restoration of underlying cluster identity. Both models distinguish reduced and canonical forms with high accuracy. Crucially, reduced segments retain cues to their underlying stops, indicating that CCR is encoded as structured gradient phonological variation rather than simple segmental deletion. These results demonstrate structured phonological encoding of AAE CCR patterns in modern speech models.

### [12] [Does My Embedding Reflect That $A = B$? Evaluating Mathematical Equivalence in Embedding Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23959)

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They study whether text embedding models recognize when differently worded math statements are actually the same, introduce a benchmark (MELD), show existing models fail, and propose a contrastive method that better aligns formal and informal math text.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Mathematics is expressed in many ‘languages’: different subfields, styles, and formal systems (like Lean vs natural language). The same underlying statement can look very different. Discovering such equivalences matters both for research (avoiding duplicate work, transferring results) and for practical applications like navigating formal libraries and proof assistants. With the rise of formalization, we need embeddings that capture underlying mathematical meaning, not just surface wording. It is unclear whether current embedding models do this, and there is no focused dataset to test it.

Method: 1) Construct the MELD dataset: pairs (or sets) of mathematically equivalent statements written in very different natural language forms. 2) Evaluate state-of-the-art text embedding models on MELD to see whether equivalent statements are embedded close together. 3) Analyze how existing models cluster statements (finding that they focus on shared terminology rather than shared mathematical content). 4) Propose a contrastive learning approach for mathematical text embeddings: train a model using pairs of informal statements and their various formalizations as positive pairs, encouraging it to align them in embedding space. 5) Test the new embeddings on both informal–formal retrieval tasks and on MELD.

Result: Empirically, existing state-of-the-art embedding models tend to group statements based on overlapping vocabulary and phrasing, failing to recognize many mathematically equivalent but lexically different pairs in MELD. The proposed contrastive training, which explicitly aligns informal statements with multiple formal counterparts, produces embeddings that perform better on informal–formal retrieval and also show improved performance on MELD, despite MELD containing only natural language statements.

Conclusion: Current embedding models are not reliably math-aware: they overemphasize lexical similarity and undercapture mathematical equivalence. By leveraging contrastive learning on informal–formal alignments, one can train embeddings that better reflect underlying mathematical content. This improves both cross-language (informal–formal) retrieval and recognition of equivalence among purely natural-language mathematical statements, suggesting a promising direction for tools that help navigate between different mathematical ‘languages’ and bridge human and formalized mathematics.

Abstract: Because mathematics is highly abstract, a single statement can take very different forms depending on what subfield it is framed in. There are many examples where breakthroughs occurred after researchers discovered that a question had already been answered in a different field. At the same time, the growth of new resources related to formalization has increased the need for tools that enable efficient and reliable navigation between mathematical 'languages' (e.g., from Lean to natural language). In this paper, we investigate whether current embedding models capture mathematical equivalence. To do this, we introduce the Mathematically Equivalent but Lexically Different Pairs (MELD) Dataset, a collection of mathematically equivalent statements that are expressed in very different language. We show that current state-of-the-art embedding models tend to group statements by the terminology used to make them instead of the underlying math. Motivated by this, we propose a contrastive approach to learning embeddings of mathematical text that focuses on aligning informal statements with different formalizations. Our experiments demonstrate that this leads to improvements not only on informal-formal retrieval tasks but also on MELD, which only contains natural language statements.

### [13] [Faithful by Construction: Claim-Anchored Attribution for Multi-Document Summarization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23989)
*Shuo Guan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CAMS is a modular summarization framework that builds summaries from extracted, provenance-tracked claims to improve faithfulness and precise multi-source attribution over end-to-end LLM summarizers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: End-to-end LLM-based multi-document summarization often hallucinates facts and only provides coarse, post hoc attributions (e.g., to whole documents), making it hard to verify each statement. There is a need for a summarization method that offers fine-grained, reliable traceability of each summary sentence back to specific supporting text spans while keeping summary quality high.

Method: The paper reuses the Extract–Select–Rewrite paradigm but defines extracted atomic claims as the main intermediate unit. CAMS works in four stages: (i) extract atomic claims from each document with token-level provenance, (ii) cluster equivalent claims across documents and detect conflicts, (iii) select a subset of claims based on salience and level of support, and (iv) rewrite the selected claims into a fluent summary where every sentence is anchored to a specific, support-checked claim pointing back to one or more source spans. The pipeline also includes verification and constrained rewriting to encourage factual faithfulness while maintaining structural attribution links.

Result: On benchmarks like MultiNews, DiverseSumm, and WCEP (in zero-shot transfer), CAMS achieves comparable summary quality to strong end-to-end and span-attribution baselines but significantly improves faithfulness and citation precision. It raises multi-source attribution accuracy by about two-thirds, cleanly separates evaluation of citation quality from localization accuracy, and shows through an additional audit (using an independent support model) that its citations are more precise. The framework also reveals an explicit, controllable trade-off between faithfulness and coverage.

Conclusion: Modular, claim-anchored summarization can match the fluency and overall quality of end-to-end LLM summarizers while providing much better fine-grained attribution and faithfulness. By centering atomic claims with explicit provenance and conflict handling, CAMS offers a more transparent and auditable approach to multi-document summarization, with tunable faithfulness–coverage behavior that end-to-end models usually obscure.

Abstract: End-to-end large language models (LLMs) produce fluent multi-document summaries but remain prone to hallucination, and the attributions they offer are typically coarse (whole documents or passages) and generated post hoc, leaving each summary statement hard to verify. We revisit the modular Extract--Select--Rewrite paradigm and recast its intermediate representation as the unit of attribution. We present CAMS, a Claim-Anchored Multi-document Summarization framework that (i) extracts atomic claims with token-level provenance from every source document, (ii) clusters equivalent claims across documents while flagging inter-source conflicts, (iii) selects a support-aware and salient subset, and (iv) rewrites the selection into a summary in which every sentence is anchored to a support-checked claim that links back to one or more source spans. Because content is localized before it is realized, the pipeline is attribution-oriented by construction and faithfulness-oriented by construction: it structurally preserves fine-grained, multi-source traceability while using support-aware selection, constrained rewriting, and verification to encourage, rather than guarantee, factual faithfulness. We evaluate quality, faithfulness, and localization on MultiNews, analyze conflict handling on DiverseSumm, and test zero-shot transfer on WCEP, using a two-regime protocol that separates reference-free citation quality from gold-aligned localization accuracy, and we add an evaluator-decoupled audit that tests citation precision with a support model never used for selection or verification. CAMS matches strong end-to-end and span-attribution baselines on summary quality while substantially improving faithfulness and citation precision, lifting multi-source attribution accuracy by roughly two-thirds, and exposing a controllable faithfulness--coverage trade-off that end-to-end models leave implicit.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinical value sets—lists of standardized terminology codes—are central to quality measurement, phenotyping, cohort construction, and clinical decision support. Automatically completing or expanding these sets is hard because clinical code systems are huge, versioned, and not fully memorized by LLMs, and prior work (RASC) showed that direct zero-shot LLM generation performs poorly. The authors want a method that leverages LLM reasoning power without sacrificing recall, precision, or safety/auditability.

Method: They adopt a two-stage, retrieval-constrained framework. Stage 1 builds a high-recall candidate pool of codes using Qwen3-based retrieval enhanced with vocabulary-aware query expansion and a "code-display rescue" retrieval mechanism, improving the chance that all true codes are in the pool. Stage 2 then replaces the prior SAPBert cross-encoder with a large language model (GPT-5) used as a blinded adjudicator over this candidate set, deciding which codes belong to the value set while being constrained to only choose from the retrieved pool.

Result: On the RASC benchmark’s full 3,744-value-set test split, the enhanced retrieval increases candidate-pool recall from 0.553 to 0.730; on the held-out-publisher stratum, recall reaches 0.655. However, simply feeding this larger pool to the existing SAPBert cross-encoder yields relatively low macro F1 (0.287 full-test, 0.233 held-out). Replacing SAPBert with GPT-5 adjudication over the same pool boosts macro F1 to 0.549 on the full test and 0.533 on held-out publishers, indicating major performance gains from LLM-based selection under retrieval constraints.

Conclusion: A retrieval-then-LLM-adjudication pipeline is substantially more effective for clinical value set completion than direct LLM generation or traditional cross-encoder ranking alone. High-recall, auditable candidate pools combined with constrained LLM reasoning can significantly improve macro F1 while ensuring that all returned codes come from a known, reviewable source, making the approach more practical and trustworthy for clinical applications.

Abstract: Clinical value sets define the standardized terminology codes used in quality measurement, phenotyping, cohort construction, and clinical decision support. The recently introduced Retrieval-Augmented Set Completion (RASC) benchmark showed that direct zero-shot large language model (LLM) generation is poorly suited to this task: clinical code systems are large, version-controlled, and not reliably memorized by language models. We study a stage-wise alternative in which candidate-pool construction is optimized for recall and a constrained LLM adjudicator is optimized for candidate selection. On the full 3,744-value-set RASC test split, Qwen3-based retrieval with vocabulary-aware expansion and code-display rescue retrieval increases candidate-pool recall from the original RASC retrieval baseline of 0.553 to 0.730; on the held-out-publisher stratum, pool recall is 0.655. The higher-recall pool alone is not sufficient: applying the original SAPBert cross-encoder to this expanded pool gives full-test macro F1 of 0.287 and held-out-publisher macro F1 of 0.233. Replacing the stage-2 selector with blinded GPT-5 adjudication over the same pool increases full-test macro F1 to 0.549 and held-out-publisher macro F1 to 0.533. These results show that retrieval-constrained LLM adjudication can substantially improve value set completion while preserving the safety constraint that all returned codes must come from an auditable candidate pool.

### [15] [Towards Spec Learning: Inference-Time Alignment from Preference Pairs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24004)

  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for a middle-ground approach that is cheap, data-efficient, and interpretable, yet can compete with fine-tuning in specialized domains with dense preference signals.

Method: The authors propose spec learning: users provide a short instruction plus a small number of preference judgments over model outputs. These specifications encode the learned preferences and can be directly inspected and edited by humans. The approach is evaluated by comparing LLM performance when guided by learned specs versus models trained via direct preference optimization (DPO).

Result: On datasets from specialized domains with dense preference signals, LLM responses conditioned on the learned specifications often outperform those produced by models trained with DPO. This demonstrates that spec learning can be competitive with, and sometimes better than, parameter-updating preference optimization while using far fewer resources.

Conclusion: Spec learning offers an efficient, interpretable alternative to preference-based fine-tuning for controlling LLM behavior. By compiling a small set of user preferences into natural-language specifications used as prompts, it can steer models effectively at inference time, match or surpass DPO on certain tasks, and provide transparent, human-readable representations of the underlying preference signal.

Abstract: Steering a large language model (LLM) toward a desired behavior typically relies on an iterative process of hand-crafting a prompt based on a careful inspection of the model's responses. This is an involved, brittle, and error-prone process. Preference-based fine-tuning is a more rigorous but often prohibitively expensive solution. We propose spec learning, a framework that relies on a brief user instruction and a small set of preference judgments. These are compiled into specifications in the form of natural-language prompts for an LLM. Specifications condition LLMs at inference time, and no parameter updates to the underlying models are required. We show that the responses generated based on the compiled specifications often outperform direct preference optimization (DPO) on datasets from specialized domains whose preference signal is dense. Unlike opaque weight updates, the resulting specifications are human-readable and double as interpretable and transparent written embodiments of the preference signal that produced them.

Parameter-level edits are hard to control, roll back, or trace, and they risk interfering with unrelated capabilities. The paper is motivated by the desire for models whose knowledge can be updated, versioned, and reverted in a precise, auditable way through explicit, structured memory operations.

Method: The paper builds on MeMo, a language-model architecture with explicit correlation matrix memories (CMMs) where memorization, retrieval, and forgetting are built-in operations. The framework handles both direct sequence-level edits and structured diff-style inputs.

Result: Conceptually, the work specifies how complex version-aware edits can be decomposed into ordered sequences of primitive MeMo operations, extending MeMo with V-CMM and T-CMM for version and transaction management. It presents a design that can apply, record, and reuse knowledge updates, including their inverses, and sketches an evaluation methodology focusing on update success, rollback ability, traceability, locality of impact, and transaction reuse. The abstract does not report empirical benchmarks but rather a framework and evaluation plan.

Conclusion: Explicit correlation-matrix memories, combined with a version-aware operation layer and dedicated CMMs for versions and transactions, can turn knowledge editing into a structured, auditable process. Instead of retraining the whole model when knowledge changes, one can perform targeted memory transactions that support rollback, history preservation, and reuse, potentially making language model maintenance more modular, controllable, and efficient.

Abstract: MeMo proposes language models with explicit multi-layer correlation matrix memories (CMMs), where memorization, retrieval, and forgetting are architectural operations. This paper asks how such memories can reduce the need for retraining when knowledge changes. For changes expressible as MeMo memory associations, the model's accessible knowledge can be updated by editing explicit memories rather than retraining the whole model. We propose a version-aware operation layer in which high-level operations such as replace, obsolete, keep-history, rollback, and trace are compiled into MeMo-native primitive calls over sequences and tokens. The key observation is that a version-aware operation is rarely a single MeMo association. It is an ordered transaction of primitive edits, for example forgetting one sequence-token chain, memorizing another, preserving a historical chain, and recording an inverse program. The framework introduces two auxiliary CMMs: a Version CMM (V-CMM) for mapping version transitions to transaction handles, and a Transaction CMM (T-CMM) for storing reusable change contents and inverse programs. It supports both direct sequence-level edits and structured diff-level inputs, and outlines an evaluation route for update success, rollback, traceability, locality, and transaction reuse.

From the empirical comparisons, the best-performing order of operations was: tokenisation → text cleaning → stemming → stop‑word removal.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that the sequence of preprocessing steps substantially influences sentiment analysis performance on Twitter data. One critical step is preprocessing: the automated processing of text for machine learning algorithms. Preprocessing plays a critical role in reducing noise and improving efficiency. However, little research has systematically examined the order in which preprocessing techniques are implemented. We find that, when accounting for order, spelling correction is the least impactful preprocessing technique, whereas tokenisation is the most impactful. Stemming and stop-word removal are interchangeable, and it is better to remove stop words without removing negation. The best order for applying the preprocessing techniques was tokenisation, text cleaning, stemming, and then stopword removal. Our results provide a systematic approach for practitioners to deploy preprocessing to improve model output without the costly preprocessing exploratory phase.

### [18] [Selective Capability Unlearning in End-to-End Spoken Language Understanding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24063)
*Akanksha Singh,Vinod Kumar Kurmi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They identify a safety issue in SLU models where an intent’s behavior persists even after the intent is removed, and introduce a representation-level method (BSU) to isolate and weaken that behavior without hurting other performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In real deployments, SLU systems may need to disable some functionalities (intents) for policy or safety reasons. Simply blocking an intent label in an autoregressive model is insufficient because the model can still generate the associated slot structure if given an appropriate prefix. This persistence of capability creates safety and compliance risks, motivating a method to truly remove or neutralize such intent-specific behaviors.

Method: They define and study “capability persistence” in SLU, where the intent-to-slot mapping survives even after the intent is suppressed. To mitigate it, they introduce Binding Subspace (BSU), a framework operating at the representation level. BSU identifies latent directions in the model’s internal representation space that encode the binding between intents and their conditioned slot-generation behavior, and then isolates and attenuates these directions so that the model’s ability to reconstruct intent-slot structures from forced prefixes is reduced.

Result: On multiple SLU benchmarks, applying BSU to existing autoregressive SLU models markedly decreases the model’s ability to recover the original intent-slot structure when given forced prefixes (lower forced-prefix recoverability), indicating a successful weakening of the targeted capability. At the same time, BSU preserves performance on the remaining, non-targeted functionalities, so overall SLU utility is largely maintained.

Conclusion: Autoregressive SLU models exhibit capability persistence, where disabling an intent label does not remove the underlying intent-conditioned slot-generation behavior. By manipulating the internal representation space with Binding Subspace (BSU), one can effectively isolate and dampen these intent-conditioned directions, substantially reducing recoverability of the removed functionality while keeping other capabilities intact, thus improving controllability and safety of deployed SLU systems.

Abstract: Modern spoken language understanding (SLU) systems are increasingly deployed in real-world settings, where specific functionalities may need to be removed due to policy or safety constraints. In SLU, a functionality corresponds to an intent and its associated slot-generation behavior. However, in autoregressive models, suppressing a target intent does not eliminate the conditional mapping that generates slots conditioned on that intent. When the intent prefix is externally supplied, the model can reconstruct the original intent-slot structure. We identify this structural failure as \textbf{\emph{capability persistence}}. We propose \textit{\underline{B}inding \underline{S}ubspace (BSU)}, a representation-level framework that isolates and attenuates intent-conditioned directions underlying this mapping. Across SLU benchmarks, BSU substantially reduces forced-prefix recoverability while preserving retained performance.
