### [19] [Sentence-Level Contextual Entrainment in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24077)
*Yang Liu,Chenhui Chu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies 'contextual entrainment' in LLMs at the sentence level, showing that sentences appearing in the prompt get higher probability during inference, even if counterfactual. It characterizes this effect across many models, shows it weakens with model size, and identifies a small subset of attention heads that largely control it, whose deactivation reduces entrainment without harming performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Previous work showed token-level contextual entrainment: models over-favor tokens seen in the context, which can bias predictions. However, many real tasks involve whole sentences or statements, not isolated tokens. The authors want to know whether this bias extends to entire sentences (including false ones), how prevalent it is across models and tasks, how it scales with model size, and whether it can be controlled mechanistically to improve reliability.

Method: They define a sentence-level measure by taking the mean log-probability per token for full sentences and compare probabilities of sentences that are or are not present in the prompt. They test 26 LLMs from seven model families on two datasets spanning subjective and objective tasks. They quantify how much the presence of a sentence in the prompt raises its probability, analyze how this effect scales with model size, and perform mechanistic interpretability experiments on attention heads to identify which heads are most responsible. They then ablate (turn off) these heads during inference to measure the impact on contextual entrainment and overall task performance.

Result: They empirically confirm robust sentence-level contextual entrainment across models and datasets: sentences that appear in the prompt get significantly increased probability, including counterfactual or incorrect ones. The strength of entrainment decreases as model size grows. Mechanistic analysis shows that only about 2–4% of attention heads account for most of the entrainment effect. Ablating these heads substantially reduces contextual entrainment while leaving general performance largely unchanged.

Conclusion: Contextual entrainment is a real and measurable sentence-level phenomenon in LLMs that can systematically bias model outputs toward sentences seen in the prompt, even when those sentences are false. This bias is weaker in larger models and is implemented by a small subset of attention heads whose targeted ablation can mitigate the effect without degrading capabilities. These findings both illuminate internal mechanisms of LLMs and suggest practical interventions to improve reliability in the presence of misleading or counterfactual context.

Abstract: Contextual entrainment, which is a newly discovered phenomenon in large language models (LLMs), refers to the tendency of a model to assign higher probabilities to tokens that appear in its context. In this work, we extend this phenomenon from the token level to the sentence level by examining the per-token mean log-probability of a sentence instead of the probabilities of individual tokens. We investigate sentence-level contextual entrainment across 26 LLMs from seven families and two datasets, which cover both subjective and objective tasks. We find that sentence-level contextual entrainment exists. This means that the sentences in the prompt (even if they are counterfactual statements) can significantly increase their probability during model inference time. As the model size increases, contextual entrainment gradually decreases. We also find that contextual entrainment is controlled by 2% to 4% of the attention heads. Turning off these attention heads can effectively mitigate contextual entrainment without hurting the model's performance.

</details>


### [20] [CAVEWOMAN: How Large Language Models Behave Under Linguistic Input and Output Compression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24083)
*Morayo Danielle Adeyemi,Ryan A. Rossi,Franck Dernoncourt*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Cavewoman, an evaluation protocol showing that compressing model outputs usually saves cost, but compressing user prompts tends to backfire—cost rises and accuracy falls, while surface text diverges from the model’s own unconstrained answers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Practitioners often shorten prompts (“caveman style”) to save tokens and inference cost, but there is little rigorous, channel-aware evidence about whether this actually reduces cost or harms performance. The authors want a systematic way to quantify the real trade‑offs of compressing either the user’s input, the model’s output, or both, across models and datasets.

Method: They define Cavewoman, a two-channel protocol that evaluates each generation simultaneously on: (1) task accuracy, (2) realized per-item cost under realistic token pricing, and (3) semantic agreement with an unconstrained reference generation from the same model. They apply this to eight models (API and open-weight) on five datasets, at five different compression levels, independently compressing the input channel and the output channel. They also test robustness of the semantic divergence findings via length-controlled re-scoring, multiple-comparisons correction, and alternative semantic similarity metrics.

Result: Compressing outputs significantly reduces realized cost on most API models (about 1.4–2.4× cheaper per model, up to 3× in the best case) and on all four open-weight models under assumed public-tier pricing, with acceptable accuracy. Compressing inputs does the opposite: it increases net cost (~1.15× on average across five benchmarks, up to 1.8× on the worst dataset and 2.7× under stronger compression) because models respond with longer outputs while accuracy degrades. Furthermore, on non-reasoning models, many generations remain factually correct but their surface text no longer entails or closely matches the model’s own unconstrained reference answers, and this divergence is robust under various controls and semantic measures.

Conclusion: Naïve “caveman” prompt shortening is often counterproductive when applied to inputs: it tends to increase cost and reduce accuracy while making model outputs semantically drift from their own unconstrained baselines. By contrast, carefully compressing outputs is a more reliable way to cut costs. Cavewoman offers a standardized, two-channel framework to measure these effects and can guide practitioners toward compression strategies that genuinely save money without hidden performance regressions.

Abstract: "Talk short. Drop grammar. Save token." This caveman style is widely promoted as a way to cut inference cost, but whether it actually saves anything depends on which channel (the user's prompt or the model's response) is being compressed. We present Cavewoman, a two-channel evaluation protocol that scores every generation on task accuracy, realized per-item cost, and reference-text agreement against the model's unconstrained reference. We evaluate eight models on five datasets at five reduction levels, with both channels measured on the same items. Output compression cuts realized cost on most API models (1.4-2.4x per model, up to 3x in the best case) and on all four open-weight models under public-tier pricing. Input compression has the opposite effect, a strict lose-lose: it raises net cost rather than lowering it (~1.15x on the five-benchmark mean, up to 1.8x on the worst dataset and 2.7x under stronger compression), because models compensate with longer responses even as accuracy collapses. Under the same setting, surface text diverges from the unconstrained reference: on the non-reasoning models, roughly half of all generations are correct yet their surface text no longer entails the model's own unconstrained baseline generation. The divergence survives length-controlled re-scoring, multiple-comparisons correction, and replication under complementary semantic measures. Code and data are available at https://github.com/danielle34/cavewoman.

</details>


### [21] [Predicting Poets' Origins from Verse: A Computational Analysis of Regional Linguistic Fingerprints in the Complete Tang Poems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24093)
*Chi-Sheng Chen,Hung-Yun Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether Tang-dynasty poets’ regional origins (North vs. South, and finer circuits) can be detected from their poetic language using machine learning on the Complete Tang Poems, and finds that simple character n-gram features already encode a meaningful geographic signal.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To investigate whether literary style in Classical Chinese poetry carries stable, measurable traces of geographic origin, and to use computational methods to shed light on historical questions about regional linguistic variation, prestige norms, and the evolution of poetic language across the Tang dynasty.

Method: They construct a poet-level corpus by aggregating all poems for each of 357 poets in the Complete Tang Poems and linking each poet to their administrative circuit of origin using the China Biographical Database. They then frame origin prediction as a multi-class text classification problem (South vs. North and ten Tang circuits), using character n-gram TF-IDF features plus interpretable domain features (imagery, season, allusion) as input to classical and neural classifiers. They also compare these models to a hierarchical frozen-encoder representation from a pretrained Classical Chinese transformer model (GuwenBERT). They analyze classification performance, relate linguistic and geographic distances via a Mantel test, and study how regional separability changes over historical subperiods of the Tang. They interpret misclassifications, especially high-confidence errors, for their historical significance.

Result: (1) Models predict South vs. North origin with 0.69 accuracy versus a 0.53 majority baseline, and achieve circuit-level prediction above chance, showing a detectable regional signal. (2) Linguistic distance between circuits positively correlates with geographic distance (Mantel r = 0.40, p ≈ 0.09 over nine circuits), suggesting distance-decay in poetic language. (3) Regional signal varies over time: South/North separability is around chance in the High Tang and highest in the Late Tang, consistent with early homogenization and later divergence. (4) Model errors are historically structured: in the Early Tang, all misclassified poets are southern authors predicted as northern, implying the prestige of the northern court idiom. (5) A hierarchical GuwenBERT representation, even over the full corpus per poet, only matches TF-IDF performance; combining them yields no gains, implying that character n-grams already encode most of the regional signal.

Conclusion: Poetic language in the Tang dynasty encodes measurable traces of poets’ geographic origins, with regional differences that increase with physical distance and shift over historical time. These differences reflect broader sociolinguistic dynamics such as court prestige and later regional divergence. Simple, interpretable features like character n-grams are sufficient to capture this signal, and more complex neural representations do not improve prediction. The work demonstrates that interpretable machine learning can serve as a powerful hypothesis generator for literary history, revealing subtle, historically meaningful patterns in classical texts.

Abstract: We ask whether the geographic origin of Tang-dynasty poets leaves a detectable linguistic trace in their work. Aggregating every poem attributed to each author in the Complete Tang Poems (Quan Tang Shi) and linking poets to their administrative circuit of origin via the China Biographical Database (CBDB), we build a poet-level corpus of 357 poets across the ten Tang circuits and frame origin prediction as multi-class classification. Using character $n$-gram TF-IDF together with interpretable domain features (imagery, season, and allusion), classical and neural models predict a poet's broad region (South vs.\ North) at $0.69$ accuracy, well above the $0.53$ majority baseline, and finer circuit-level origin above chance. Beyond classification, three findings emerge. (i) Linguistic distance between circuits grows with geographic distance (Mantel $r=0.40$, $p\approx0.09$ over nine circuits), evidence of a distance-decay effect in poetic language. (ii) The signal interacts with time: South/North separability is at chance in the High Tang and strongest in the Late Tang, consistent with court-driven homogenization at the empire's height followed by regional divergence. (iii) The model's confident errors are historically meaningful -- in the Early Tang, every misclassification is a southern poet read as northern, reflecting the prestige of the northern court idiom. We further show that, when given the whole corpus through a hierarchical frozen-encoder representation, a classical-Chinese transformer (GuwenBERT) only matches -- not beats -- simple TF-IDF, and that combining them adds nothing, indicating that character $n$-grams already capture the regional signal. Our results position interpretable machine learning as a hypothesis generator for literary history.

</details>


### [22] [PORTER: Language-Grounded Event Representations for Portable Structured EHR Foundation Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24102)
*Lin Lawrence Guo,Adam Paul Yan,Emily Vettese,Lillian Sung*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: PORTER is a vocabulary-independent EHR foundation model that represents clinical events via natural-language descriptions plus a numeric pathway, achieving comparable in-domain performance while greatly improving cross-vocabulary and cross-institution transfer.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing EHR foundation models rely on fixed vocabularies of discrete event tokens (e.g., specific codes or fields). This prevents them from directly handling previously unseen concepts, new combinations of concepts and attributes, or continuous numeric values. As a result, models transfer poorly across hospitals, coding schemes, or even different data pipelines within the same institution, often requiring extensive vocabulary harmonization and retraining. The paper aims to build an EHR model that is robust to these vocabulary changes while retaining strong predictive performance.

Method: The authors propose PORTER, a language-grounded structured EHR foundation model. PORTER: (1) represents each clinical event via a textual description processed by a frozen text encoder, decoupling representation from any fixed event-token vocabulary; (2) incorporates numeric values through a specialized numeric pathway designed to capture magnitudes without altering concept identity; and (3) models temporal clinical dynamics with an autoregressively pretrained backbone over patient timelines. The model is pretrained on EHR event sequences and then evaluated on many downstream prediction tasks, including scenarios where event descriptions differ between pretraining and deployment and when transferred to a different dataset (MIMIC). Mechanistic analyses examine how representation geometry and encoder scale affect cross-vocabulary transfer and how the numeric pathway influences sensitivity to values.

Result: On 74 clinical prediction tasks at a pediatric hospital, PORTER matched the mean AUROC of a strong fixed-vocabulary baseline sharing the same temporal backbone and pretraining objective. When patient timelines were rendered using alternative event descriptions that were unseen during pretraining, PORTER transferred without any retraining or explicit vocabulary mapping, recovering 97.1% of the mean AUROC of a model trained directly on the target vocabulary. On the external MIMIC dataset, PORTER outperformed the fixed-vocabulary model, whose performance suffered because 69% of its events were dropped as unseen tokens. Mechanistic results showed that successful cross-vocabulary transfer aligned with preservation of patient-level representation geometry, not with simply using a larger text encoder, and that the numeric pathway improved sensitivity to numeric magnitude while maintaining concept identity. PORTER also surpassed a task-specific text-serialization baseline in AUROC while using 329x less amortized compute.

Conclusion: PORTER demonstrates that grounding EHR event representations in natural-language descriptions, augmented with a dedicated numeric pathway and temporal backbone, can remove dependence on a fixed event vocabulary without sacrificing in-domain predictive accuracy. This approach enables effective zero-shot transfer across vocabularies and institutions, reduces the need for costly vocabulary harmonization, and allows efficient reuse across many downstream clinical prediction tasks. The work highlights representation-geometry preservation as a key factor for cross-vocabulary generalization and positions language-grounded, vocabulary-independent models as a promising direction for scalable, transferable EHR foundation models.

Abstract: Most electronic health record (EHR) foundation models encode clinical events as discrete event tokens from a fixed vocabulary and therefore cannot directly represent events containing unseen concepts or new combinations of concepts and attributes such as numeric values. This limits transfer across institutions and even across deployment pipelines within the same institution. We introduce PORTER, a language-grounded structured EHR foundation model that decouples event representation from this fixed vocabulary. PORTER represents events through their descriptions using a frozen text encoder, integrates numeric values through a dedicated pathway, and learns clinical dynamics over patient timelines with an autoregressively pretrained temporal backbone. Across 74 clinical prediction tasks at a pediatric hospital, PORTER matched the mean AUROC of a fixed-vocabulary model with the same temporal backbone and pretraining objective. When the same patient timelines were rendered using event descriptions not seen during pretraining, PORTER transferred without retraining or vocabulary mapping, recovering 97.1% of the mean AUROC of a model trained directly on the target vocabulary. When transferred to MIMIC, PORTER outperformed the fixed-vocabulary model, which dropped 69% of events because their tokens were unseen. Mechanistic analyses showed cross-vocabulary transfer tracked preservation of patient-level representation geometry rather than the scale of the text encoder, and the numeric pathway improved sensitivity to magnitude without disrupting clinical concept identity. PORTER also achieved higher AUROC than a task-specific text serialization comparator, at 329-fold lower amortized compute. PORTER is a step toward vocabulary-independent EHR foundation models that reduce the need for vocabulary harmonization while preserving in-domain performance and enabling efficient cross-task reuse.

*Zijie Dai,Siuhin He,Hui Li,Qihui Zhou,Jiajun Li,Mingcong Song,Guoping Long,Hongjie Si,Xin Yao,Lin Zhang,James Cheng,Xiao Yan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Metis, a self-evolving agent with a dual memory representation (text + code) that outperforms prior systems in accuracy and efficiency on interactive tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing self-evolving agents store experience either as text prompts or as code tools, but this design-time choice is not grounded in a clear understanding of the trade-offs. There is a lack of controlled studies comparing these representations on the same experience, and current systems fail to exploit their complementary strengths.

Method: 1) Run the first controlled comparison where identical experience is represented as (a) text memory injected into context and (b) code memory as callable tools, measuring construction cost, execution efficiency, and transferability. 2) From the findings, design Metis, a hierarchical dual-representation memory system. It structures textual memories into execution plans, environment facts, and pitfalls, and selectively converts frequently reused plans into validated tools. 3) Evaluate Metis on AppWorld against baselines like ReAct and other self-evolving agents.

Result: The controlled study shows text and code memories have complementary strengths: text is broadly applicable and more transferable; code is more execution-efficient but costly to construct and narrower in scope. Metis, which combines them hierarchically and selectively, improves task accuracy by up to 20.6% over ReAct and reduces execution cost by up to 22.8%, and outperforms other self-evolving systems on the joint criteria of accuracy, efficiency, and memory-construction cost.

Conclusion: Neither pure text memory nor pure code memory is sufficient for optimal self-evolving agents; a principled combination is needed. Metis demonstrates that a hierarchical dual-representation memory, which promotes heavily reused textual plans into tools, can better balance accuracy, runtime efficiency, and the cost of building and maintaining memory, achieving strong performance on challenging interactive benchmarks like AppWorld.

Abstract: Self-evolving agents improve over time by distilling experience from past executions and reusing it in future tasks. Existing systems represent such experience either as natural-language text injected into the agent context or as code exposed as callable tools. However, the choice between these representations is typically made at design time rather than derived from the characteristics of the experience itself, leaving the trade-offs between them poorly understood. We present the first controlled study that isolates text memory and code memory over an identical set of experiences. Our results show that the two forms exhibit complementary trade-offs in construction cost, execution efficiency, and transferability, such that neither representation alone is sufficient. Guided by these findings, we propose Metis, a self-evolving agent system built on a hierarchical dual-representation memory. Metis organizes textual experience into execution plans, environment facts, and common pitfalls, and selectively crystallizes recurring plans into validated callable tools. This design combines the broad applicability of text memory with the execution efficiency of code memory while incurring tool-generation cost only when justified by repeated reuse. We evaluate Metis on AppWorld, a challenging benchmark for interactive agents. The results show that Metis improves task accuracy by up to 20.6% over ReAct while reducing execution cost by up to 22.8%. Compared with representative self-evolving agent systems, Metis consistently achieves a better balance between accuracy, execution efficiency, and memory-construction cost.

### [24] [MedBench v5: A Dynamic, Process-Oriented, and Hallucination-Aware Benchmark for Clinical Multimodal Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24155)
*Ding Jinru,Jiang Chuchu,Lu Lu,Pang Wenrao,Bian Mouxiao,Gao Zhuangzhi,Chen Jiangyuan,Peng xinwei,Chen Ruiyao,Ren Sijie,Lu Renjie,Han Bin,Liu Meiling,and Xu Jie*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: MedBench v5 is a new medical AI benchmark that evaluates not just final answers but the reasoning process, stress robustness, and hallucination behavior of clinical multimodal models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current medical AI benchmarks mostly use static question-answer formats, which hide how models reason, fail to isolate specific clinical skills, and do not systematically track hallucinations. This makes it hard to understand model weaknesses, safety issues, and reliability under imperfect or conflicting information—conditions common in real clinical practice.

Method: The authors design MedBench v5, a process-oriented benchmark for language models, vision-language models, and agentic systems in the clinical domain. It combines: (1) a dual-dimensional framework that evaluates 14 aspects of clinical cognitive responsiveness and 4 categories of atomic medical skills implemented via agent environments, spanning 63 concrete tasks; (2) controlled information-flow stressors—data omission, contradictions, and delayed evidence—to test how performance degrades; (3) a dynamic process audit protocol with five predefined reasoning checkpoints to trace how models think and where they fail; and (4) a hallucination monitoring scheme that tracks when hallucinations start, how they spread, how they become anchored in reasoning, and how they interact with future contradictory evidence.

Result: When running state-of-the-art models on MedBench v5, the authors find that high overall scores on tasks can coexist with unstable reasoning processes. Under stressors like missing, conflicting, or delayed information, models particularly struggle with detecting contradictions, updating diagnoses appropriately, handling hallucination propagation, and using contradictions to self-correct. In contrast, the final step of grounding answers in evidence can appear relatively unaffected, masking process-level weaknesses.

Conclusion: MedBench v5 offers a unified and fine-grained evaluation infrastructure for clinical AI that exposes not only end-task performance but also reasoning stability, robustness to information stress, and hallucination trajectories. This benchmark enables more precise capability profiling and safety analysis of clinical multimodal models, and can guide the development of more trustworthy, process-aligned medical AI systems.

Abstract: Existing medical AI benchmarks lack process visibility, atomic skill evaluation, and integrated hallucination detection. We introduce MedBench v5, a redesigned benchmark for clinical multimodal models (language, vision-language, and agent systems) that moves from static QA to dynamic, process-oriented evaluation. MedBench v5 features: (1) a dual-dimensional framework combining Clinical Cognitive Responsiveness (14 sub-dimensions) and Medical Atomic Skills (4 agent environments), covering 63 tasks; (2) three switchable information-flow stressors (omission, contradiction, evidence delay) for factorized degradation analysis; (3) a dynamic process audit protocol with five reasoning nodes that produces model-specific failure fingerprints; (4) hallucination propagation monitoring across initiation, propagation, anchoring, and contradiction interaction-capturing silent hallucination. Experiments on frontier models show that strong overall task performance does not guarantee process stability: stressors mainly disrupt contradiction detection, diagnosis updating, hallucination propagation, and contradiction-based self-correction, while final evidence grounding can remain superficially stable. MedBench v5 provides a unified infrastructure for capability profiling, controllable stress testing, process auditing, and hallucination trajectory analysis in clinical AI evaluation.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Behavioral research, however, requires that models capture both individual responses and population-level distributions to be behaviorally valid. There is no comprehensive, standardized benchmark to compare models across key behavioral capabilities or to guide the development of behavior-specialized foundation models.

Method: The authors construct BehaviorBench, a benchmark covering four behavioral capabilities: (1) behavior prediction and simulation, (2) strategic decision-making, (3) subject-trait inference, and (4) behavioral knowledge application. Result: General-purpose proprietary models perform strongly on individual-level prediction and knowledge-intensive tasks but underperform in aligning with true population-level behavioral distributions. In contrast, behavior-focused foundation models, especially the newly developed Be.FM-1.5, show substantially better distributional alignment while remaining competitive on individual-level metrics. Conclusion: Evaluating foundation models for behavioral science requires attention to distributional as well as individual-level performance. BehaviorBench provides a comprehensive, standardized way to assess these capabilities, enabling more behaviorally valid AI systems. The Be.FM-1.5 model demonstrates that fine-tuning on behavioral data can significantly improve population-level alignment without sacrificing much individual-level accuracy, making it a promising tool for a wide range of behavioral science applications. The benchmark and models are publicly available for further research and development.

Abstract: Foundation models have been increasingly applied to behavioral science domains such as psychology, sociology, and economics. While these models show promise in individual tasks such as survey response prediction and human-subject experiment simulation, there remains no systematic understanding of how well they perform across diverse behavioral science tasks, contexts, and populations. We introduce BehaviorBench, a comprehensive benchmark that evaluates foundation models along four core capabilities: (1) behavior prediction and simulation, (2) strategic decision-making, (3) subject-trait inference, and (4) behavioral knowledge application. Crucially, BehaviorBench evaluates model outputs at both the individual and distributional levels, capturing not only per-subject accuracy but also population-level alignment, an essential requirement for behavioral validity. Leveraging the tasks in BehaviorBench, we further develop Be.FM-1.5, extending the Be.FM family of behavioral foundation models fine-tuned on behavioral data. Our results reveal a considerable gap: proprietary general-purpose models excel at individual-level prediction and knowledge-intensive tasks, whereas behavioral foundation models, fine-tuned on behavioral data, achieve substantially stronger distributional alignment. Notably, Be.FM-1.5 leads on distributional metrics and remains competitive on individual-level metrics, suggesting that proper behavioral adaptation can close the gap. Our results highlight the importance of distributional evaluation, establish BehaviorBench as a foundation for developing and assessing behaviorally aligned AI systems, and demonstrate Be.FM-1.5's potential for a broad range of behavioral science studies. Our BehaviorBench and Be.FM-1.5 models can be accessed via https://umich-foreseer.github.io/behaviorbench/.

This overlooks a deep regularity. Through more than two millennia of convergence around Sanskrit, Indic languages came to share a morphosyntactic architecture formalized in Pānini's grammar, the Astādhyāyī. This cuts across genealogical lines, uniting languages through a common framework. The benchmark provides ground-truth data and a reproducible setup for assessing reliability-aware SHM methods under controlled conditions.

Conclusion: The work introduces Digi Turbine as a reliability-aware PINN benchmark tailored to offshore wind turbine monopile SHM, bridging the gap between purely data-driven surrogates and costly high-fidelity simulations. It shows that embedding physics, priors, and reliability analysis into the learning workflow is a promising direction for fast online monitoring, and provides a standardized testbed for future methodological developments.

Abstract: Reliable structural health monitoring (SHM) of offshore wind turbine (OWT) support structures requires fast state estimation from sparse measurements. Repeated high fidelity finite element or aeroelastic analyses are difficult to use directly in online monitoring loops, while purely data-driven surrogates can require large training sets. This paper presents Digi Turbine, a synthetic reliability-aware Physics Informed Neural Network (PINN) benchmark for OWT monopile support structure monitoring. The workflow embeds a simplified Euler Bernoulli beam equation with Winkler soil foundation in the training objective, couples it with Bayesian-prior-informed inverse identification, and adds First Order Reliability Method (FORM) screening.   <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They collect multi-round review comments for 11,063 accepted Nature Communications papers, segment comments, and automatically cluster them into fine-grained review aspects. Result: The LCF-BERT-CDM model achieves the best aspect sentiment classification performance with a Macro-F1 of 82.65%. Conclusion: Fine-grained, aspect-level sentiment in peer review evolves systematically across review rounds: later rounds tend to contain more positive and fewer negative sentiments, yet overall aspect sentiment scores are negatively related to the number of rounds, indicating that manuscripts requiring more rounds tend to face more critical evaluations in key aspects. The study demonstrates that deep learning-based aspect sentiment analysis can effectively model these dynamics, offering nuanced insight into how reviewers’ focus and attitudes change through the review process.

Abstract: Mining sentiment information from the textual content of peer review comments offers valuable insights into the scientific evaluation process. Notably, the dynamic shifts in reviewers' focus and sentiment tendencies throughout multiple review stages remain underexplored. To address this gap, the present study investigates the distribution and evolution of aspect-level sentiments and examines their correlation with the number of review rounds. We begin by segmenting the multi-round review comments of 11,063 accepted papers from Nature Communications and identifying fine-grained review aspect clusters. A manually annotated corpus of approximately 5,000 review sentences is then constructed. Using this dataset, we train a series of deep learning-based aspect sentiment classification models. Among them, the LCF-BERT-CDM model achieves the best performance, with a Macro-F1 score of 82.65%. Subsequent statistical analysis reveals a consistent trend: as the number of review rounds increases, the proportion of positive sentiments rises, while negative sentiments decline. Correlation analysis further indicates that aspect sentiment scores are negatively associated with the total number of review rounds. Key aspects exhibiting stronger correlations include "experiments", "research significance" and "result analysis".

*Junhyeok Lee,Han Jang,Hyeonjin Goh,Kyu Sung Choi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: MMed-Bench-IR is a multilingual medical information retrieval benchmark for RAG that jointly tests cross-lingual alignment, concept discrimination, and evidence retrieval across six languages and three tasks, revealing large performance drops in non-English settings.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinical RAG systems increasingly need to serve users in many languages while retrieving from primarily English medical evidence. Existing benchmarks test cross-lingual retrieval, concept understanding, and evidence retrieval separately, which fails to capture how biomedical expertise and multilingual coverage interact in real-world use. There is a need for an evaluation suite that jointly probes these dimensions and exposes hidden weaknesses of current systems in non-English clinical scenarios.

Method: The authors design MMed-Bench-IR, a benchmark spanning six languages and three structurally different tasks: (1) cross-lingual medical QA retrieval with 6,127 queries grounded in UMLS concepts; (2) concept discrimination with 4,975 confusion sets across three difficulty levels; and (3) multilingual evidence retrieval for RAG with 2,040 carefully curated queries. They ensure zero overlap of concepts and queries across tasks so that aggregate scores reflect broad, generalizable capability rather than memorization or task leakage. Ten retrieval systems from six methodological paradigms are evaluated on this benchmark.

Result: Across the three tasks and six languages, the evaluated systems exhibit pronounced cross-lingual performance degradation. For example, a strong biomedical encoder with an nDCG@10 of 0.818 in English drops to 0.056 in Japanese for comparable retrieval, demonstrating that models performing well on English-only benchmarks may fail dramatically in other languages. This pattern is consistent across systems and tasks, highlighting severe multilingual weaknesses.

Conclusion: MMed-Bench-IR exposes critical gaps in current multilingual medical retrieval systems, especially for non-English languages, which are invisible under English-only evaluation. The benchmark provides a structured way to disentangle and jointly assess cross-lingual alignment, concept discrimination, and evidence retrieval, and should guide the development and tuning of future clinical RAG models toward robust, multilingual performance.

Abstract: Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) in clinical settings increasingly requires multilingual retrieval against predominantly English evidence corpora. Multilingual medical retrieval demands three capabilities: cross-lingual alignment, concept discrimination, and evidence retrieval. However, existing benchmarks evaluate these only in isolation, leaving the interaction between biomedical expertise and multilingual coverage unmeasured. We introduce MMed-Bench-IR, a benchmark designed to disentangle these axes across 6 languages and three structurally heterogeneous tasks: (1) cross-lingual medical QA retrieval with 6,127 queries grounded in the Unified Medical Language System (UMLS), (2) concept discrimination over 4,975 confusion sets at three difficulty tiers, and (3) multilingual evidence retrieval for RAG with 2,040 quality-assured queries. The three tasks share zero concept and query overlap by design, ensuring that aggregate scores reflect genuine capability breadth. Evaluation of ten systems across six paradigm families reveals severe cross-lingual failure: biomedical encoders that score 0.818 nDCG@10 in English drop to 0.056 in Japanese, a gap that English-only benchmarks cannot detect.

</details>


### [30] [Decoherence as Defence and the Magnitude of Noise Regularisation: A Rigorous N -Qubit Theory of Stochastic Quantum Neural Networks for Adversarially Robust Network Intrusion Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24219)
*Gautier-Edouard Edouard Filardo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies stochastic quantum neural networks for intrusion detection, proving how depolarising noise contracts Pauli observables and showing that properly trained noisy SQNNs gain adversarial robustness and modestly better generalisation; it also derives a theory of quantum dropout as curvature-weighted L2 regularisation and demonstrates feasibility on neutral-atom hardware.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work showed that entangled SQNNs can perform collaborative intrusion detection but left key questions open: whether depolarising noise can genuinely regularise like dropout, why robustness bounds were loose, and whether results held beyond toy settings. There was also no clear theory connecting quantum noise, robustness, and generalisation, nor an assessment on real datasets or hardware platforms.

Method: The authors formulate N-qubit stochastic quantum neural networks using the stochastic master equation and Liouvillian formalism, then analytically study the effect of depolarising noise across entangling layers on Pauli read-outs, proving a decoherence-contraction theorem. They connect this to adversarial robustness using noise-as-defence theory, and empirically evaluate SQNNs with and without depolarising noise on the NSL-KDD intrusion detection dataset under strong white-box FGSM and PGD attacks. For generalisation, they perform a perturbative analysis of per-gate stochastic deactivation (quantum dropout) and depolarising noise, deriving equivalent regularisation penalties, and validate these predictions in a 30-seed experimental study. Finally, they outline a neutral-atom hardware implementation and analyse scalability with qubit number.

Result: They prove that depolarising noise of strength γ across L entangling layers contracts any weight-w Pauli read-out by (1-4γ/3)^{wL}, and for weight-1 observables this gives (1-4γ/3)^L. Experiments on the NSL-KDD dataset show that SQNNs trained with depolarising noise achieve significantly higher and more stable adversarial robustness than noiseless SQNNs and gradient-based classical detectors, which can suffer large robustness collapse (from 95% to 47%), while noisy SQNNs do not and exhibit roughly half the robustness variance. They find robustness gains stem from a reshaped decision boundary in training, not simply from gradient masking at attack time. Theoretical analysis shows per-gate dropout induces a curvature-weighted L2 penalty p(1-p)/2 Σ θ^2 ∂^2_θ L, maximised at dropout rate p=1/2, while depolarising noise yields an analogous penalty in output space. A 30-seed study confirms that both mechanisms similarly and modestly reduce the train-test gap (~0.01) with high statistical significance, and excessive dropout above p=1/2 gives no further benefit, matching theory. Prior single-seed claims of a strong dichotomy between dropout and depolarising noise are not supported when replicated.

Conclusion: Depolarising noise in SQNNs does not merely act as output noise but, when incorporated during training, provides meaningful adversarial robustness benefits by reshaping the decision boundary and stabilising performance. True quantum dropout and depolarising noise both function as regularisers, implementing curvature-weighted penalties in parameter or output space, and their generalisation effects are small but reliably positive and statistically indistinguishable. The work thus refines earlier, overly conservative robustness bounds, resolves the question of quantum dropout’s regularisation role, demonstrates robustness and generalisation on a real intrusion detection dataset, and shows that these SQNN architectures are compatible with neutral-atom quantum hardware at practical scales.

Abstract: Stochastic quantum neural networks (SQNNs) encode neuronal activations as qubits, synaptic topology as entanglement, and neural noise through a Lindblad master equation. A recent conference study applied a ring-entangled SQNN to collaborative intrusion detection and reached three conclusions: ring entanglement is \emph{essential} for non-local anomaly detection; an adversarial-resilience bound holds but is \emph{conservative}; and the depolarising channel \emph{fails} to act as a dropout-style regulariser, behaving instead as output noise. It left open whether a per-gate stochastic deactivation (``true quantum dropout'') could regularise where the depolarising channel could not, and whether the loose robustness bound could be replaced by a predictive theory. This paper resolves both and extends the framework to real data and to neutral-atom hardware. We give an $N$-qubit formulation through the stochastic master equation and its vectorised Liouvillian, and prove a \emph{decoherence-contraction theorem}: a depolarising channel of strength $γ$ over $L$ entangling layers contracts every weight-$w$ Pauli read-out by a factor $(1-4γ/3)^{wL}$ (for the weight-$1$ read-out used here, $(1-4γ/3)^{L}$); building on the general noise-as-defence result of Du et al., we make this quantitative and operational for intrusion detection. On the real NSL-KDD dataset under white-box FGSM and PGD attacks, a depolarising SQNN trained with the channel is, over seven seeds under strong $\ell_\infty$/$\ell_2$ attacks, significantly more robust than the noiseless circuit ($\ell_\infty$ PGD-$20$, $p=0.04$, large effect) and, critically, never suffers the catastrophic robustness collapse that the noiseless model and gradient-trained classical detectors (which fall from $95\%$ to $47\%$) do, cutting robustness variance roughly twofold; we show this robustness arises from a noise-reshaped training boundary rather than from attack-time gradient contraction. For generalisation, we derive an adaptive-penalty formula showing that per-gate dropout implements a curvature-weighted $L_2$ penalty $\tfrac{p(1-p)}{2}\sumθ^2\partial^2_θL$ in weight space, maximised at $p=1/2$, whereas depolarising noise implements an output-space penalty. A $30$-seed study confirms the formula's quantitative prediction: both mechanisms reduce the train-test gap by a small but statistically significant margin ($\approx\!0.01$; $p<10^{-4}$ and $p=0.004$), are statistically indistinguishable from each other, and the effect is concentrated where overfitting is largest; increasing the dropout rate past $1/2$ does not help, as the formula predicts. The single-seed dichotomy of prior work does not survive replication. We close with a neutral-atom realisation and a feasibility-by-$N$ analysis.

</details>


### [31] [SURGELLM: Rethinking Multi-Task Evaluation through Task-Aware Feature Gating with Class-Balanced Normalization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24259)
*Noor Islam S. Mohammad,Ulug Bayazit*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Surgellm is a unified transformer framework with lightweight modules to fix inductive-bias mismatch, class-imbalance bias in features, and lack of lexical conditioning, achieving strong macro-F1 gains across heterogeneous NLP tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fine-tuned encoders reused across different NLP tasks suffer from three issues: (1) their inductive biases may not align with the lexical cues that truly matter for a given task, (2) class imbalance corrupts feature and normalization statistics, especially when some label-specific patterns dominate, and (3) standard attention mechanisms lack an explicit way to condition on curated external lexical knowledge. The paper aims to design a unified, modular way to correct these issues without heavy architectural changes, improving performance and interpretability across diverse downstream tasks.

Method: They propose surgellm, a transformer-based framework augmented with three lightweight components. (1) A surgical feature gate: a per-dimension learned sigmoid that uses curated lexical indicators plus the [CLS] token to selectively amplify or suppress encoder features, with a theoretical guarantee that it reduces to the identity mapping when those features are uninformative. (2) Task-conditioned prefix tokens: additional tokens prepended to every input, encoding quantized feature values and the task identity, giving the model explicit task context and lexical priors. (3) Instance-Weighted Normalization (IWN): a modified normalization scheme that reweights instances to remove class-prior bias from the statistics used by the gate, preventing class imbalance from distorting feature gating. They also derive an excess-risk bound connecting performance gains to a measurable notion of surgical feature alignment between lexical indicators and representation dimensions.

Result: On four heterogeneous tasks—SST-2 sentiment classification, multi-hop retrieval, LLM-prompt attribution, and authorship detection—covering 17,830 examples and 11 model variants over three random seeds, the IWN-enhanced version of surgellm reaches a macro-F1 of 0.940. This is an absolute improvement of 0.036 over the strongest non-IWN baseline, and a much larger gain of 0.130 on the authorship detection task. A control experiment where the curated vocabularies are replaced by random vocabularies leads to an average F1 drop of 0.028, indicating that improvements stem from meaningful lexical signals rather than just added parameters or architectural complexity.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that surgellm’s modular additions—surgical feature gates, task-conditioned prefixes, and instance-weighted normalization—provide an effective, theoretically grounded way to adapt transformer encoders across heterogeneous NLP tasks. By explicitly aligning internal features with curated lexical indicators and debiasing normalization statistics from class priors, the framework yields sizable and robust macro-F1 gains, especially in challenging settings like authorship detection. The random-vocabulary ablation supports the claim that performance benefits depend on true lexical knowledge, and the released code, vocabularies, and near-perfect auto-extraction pipeline facilitate reproducibility and extension.

Abstract: Fine-tuned encoders deployed across heterogeneous NLP tasks face three compounding problems: mismatched inductive biases, class-imbalance corruption of feature statistics, and no mechanism to condition attention on external lexical knowledge. We introduce \textbf{\surgellm}, a unified transformer framework that addresses each with a dedicated lightweight module: a \emph{surgical feature gate} (learned per-dimension sigmoid over curated lexical indicators and \texttt{[CLS]}; provably degenerates to identity when features are uninformative), \emph{task-conditioned prefix tokens} (quantized feature values and task identity prepended to every input), and \emph{Instance-Weighted Normalization} (IWN; removes class-prior bias from gate statistics). We prove an excess-risk bound linking gate benefit to \emph{surgical feature alignment}. Across four tasks, SST-2, multi-hop retrieval, LLM-prompt attribution, and authorship detection, covering 17,830 examples and eleven model variants over three seeds, the IWN variant achieves macro-F1 \textbf{0.940} ($+0.036$ over the strongest non-IWN baseline; $+0.130$ on authorship detection). A random-vocabulary control ($-0.028$ avg.\ F1) confirms gains are lexical, not parametric. Code, vocabularies, and a $99.5\%$-recovery auto-extraction recipe are released.

</details>


### [32] [Pigeonholing: Bad prompts hurt models to collapse and make mistakes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24267)
*Hyunji Nam,Keertana Chidambaram,Dorottya Demszky,Natasha Jaques*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how bad conversational contexts in large language models cause performance degradation and proposes a mitigation method.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although in-context learning works well for LLMs, real user interactions often contain mistaken suggestions or previous wrong model outputs, which can trap the model into repeating or reinforcing errors. The authors want to systematically understand and quantify this failure mode and find ways to make models more robust to such bad contexts.

Method: They define and study a phenomenon called "pigeonholing," where LLMs become overly constrained by incorrect or narrow context. They design two scenarios: user-suggested solutions and conversations that include the assistant’s prior incorrect responses. Across 10 verifiable and open-ended tasks and 10 different models, they measure how performance and diversity of answers change as the number of erroneous turns increases. They then propose a mitigation method, RLVR with synthetic errors, where models are trained via reinforcement learning using deliberately corrupted contexts to encourage robustness.

Result: Experiments show substantial performance degradation when models are exposed to bad contexts, including 38–40% drops from repeating incorrect answers, narrow and repetitive outputs in coding and generation, and stance-flipping to match user or prior assistant claims. The degradation grows almost monotonically with more erroneous turns, with at least an additional 14% performance drop when mistakes increase from 1 to 5 turns. Mode collapse can occur even when the given example is correct. The proposed RLVR with synthetic errors improves robustness by 43–60% under bad contexts relative to standard RLVR baselines.

Conclusion: Bad conversational contexts can severely bias LLMs, causing them to repeat errors, narrow their output space, and change stances, a failure mode termed "pigeonholing." This effect intensifies with longer error-laden dialogues and can arise even with correct examples. Training with RLVR augmented by synthetic errors substantially mitigates pigeonholing, improving LLM robustness to adverse or misleading contexts.

Abstract: While in-context learning is generally shown to be effective in Large Language Models (LLMs), bad contexts can cause performance degradation and mode collapse, a phenomenon we call "pigeonholing." **Unintentionally bad** contexts can happen without malicious jailbreaking intents: For example, a user asks the model to justify an incorrect math theorem or fails to correct the model's buggy code. Specifically, we investigate ``pigeonholing" in two scenarios: (1) when the user suggests a solution, and (2) when the conversation context includes the assistant's previous (incorrect) responses. Our experiments across 10 verifiable and open-ended tasks with 10 different models show that pigeonholing manifests in several ways: (1) repeating the incorrect answers from context (leading to 38-40% performance drop), (2) converging on a narrow set of answers in coding and text generation without exploring alternatives, and (3) flipping stance on controversial topics to align with the user or the assistant's previous claims. We find that pigeonholing worsens almost monotonically with the number of conversation turns (performance drops by additional 14+% as repeated mistakes increase from 1 to 5), and pigeonholing-induced mode collapse can happen even when the provided example is correct. As a step toward mitigation, we propose RLVR with synthetic errors which improves models by 43-60% under bad contexts compared to vanilla RLVR baselines.

</details>


### [33] [CALIBER: Calibrating Confidence Before and After Reasoning in Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24281)
*Conor Finlay,Joshua Kurien,Saurabh Dash,Marzieh Fadaee,Beyza Ermis*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces CALIBER, a framework for eliciting and supervising confidence estimates from reasoning language models both before and after reasoning, aligned with their respective information states.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Reasoning language models are increasingly required not only to produce answers but also to provide well-calibrated estimates of their likelihood of success. Existing approaches typically obtain a single confidence estimate either before or after reasoning, which ignores that confidence should depend on the model’s information state. This can yield poorly calibrated confidence, especially under distribution shift. The paper aims to disentangle and properly supervise these different forms of confidence.

Method: The authors distinguish two confidence types: pre-reasoning (prompt-level) confidence, which estimates the probability of eventually solving the prompt correctly, and post-reasoning (answer-level) confidence, which estimates the probability that the realized answer is correct. They propose CALIBER, a protocol that elicits both confidence estimates from the model and supervises each with a matching target: prompt-level success for pre-reasoning confidence, and answer correctness for post-reasoning confidence. They train and evaluate this framework on various reasoning benchmarks and compare it against single-confidence baselines using calibration metrics such as Expected Calibration Error (ECE), Brier score, and AUROC.

Result: On the BigMathDigits benchmark with a 7B model, CALIBER reduces Expected Calibration Error by 52.5% compared to the strongest single-confidence baseline, achieves the best Brier score and AUROC, and maintains accuracy within 2.1 points of the best-performing method. On a 30B model, it attains the best ECE on BigMathDigits while remaining competitive in Brier score and AUROC. Out-of-distribution evaluations on GPQA, TriviaQA, and SimpleQA show that CALIBER provides superior or competitive calibration, with the best ECE and Brier score on GPQA and TriviaQA and competitive performance on SimpleQA.

Conclusion: State-dependent confidence estimation—separating and aligning pre- and post-reasoning confidence with appropriate supervision targets—substantially improves calibration of reasoning language models, especially under distribution shift. CALIBER offers a unified and effective protocol for learning these distinct confidence estimates while preserving competitive accuracy.

Abstract: Reasoning language models are increasingly asked not only to answer difficult questions, but also to estimate their likelihood of success. Existing methods typically elicit confidence only once: either before thinking or after answering. We argue that confidence in reasoning models is state-dependent: before thinking, confidence should estimate the chance of the model correctly solving the prompt, while after thinking it should predict whether the realized answer is likely to be correct. This distinction determines the appropriate supervision target: prompt-level success should supervise confidence estimates made after seeing the prompt, while individual answer-level correctness should supervise confidence estimates made after answering. We introduce CALIBER (Calibration Before and After Reasoning), which elicits both estimates and supervises each with the target matched to its information state. Under this unified protocol, CALIBER reduces Expected Calibration Error (ECE) by 52.5% over the strongest single-confidence baseline on BigMathDigits for the 7B model, while achieving the best Brier score and AUROC, and remains within 2.1 points of the best accuracy. Further, on a larger 30B model, CALIBER achieves the best ECE on BigMathDigits while remaining competitive in Brier score and AUROC. Out of distribution, it achieves the best ECE and Brier score on GPQA and TriviaQA, and remains competitive on SimpleQA. Ablations further show that this position-target alignment is most beneficial under distribution shift where it consistently reduces calibration error across all out-of-distribution benchmarks.

</details>


### [34] [AVOC: Enhancing Hour-Level Audio-Video Understanding in Omni-Modal LLMs via Retrieval-Inspired Token Compression](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24286)
*Yijing Chen,Wenhui Tan,Xiaoyi Yu,Yuyue Wang,Xin Cheng,Kaisi Guan,Hao Jiang,Xiangyang Li,Guojie Zhu,Ruihua Song*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: AVOC is a token-compression framework that enables Omni-modal LLMs to understand long-form audio-video by selecting the most useful multimodal tokens under a fixed context budget, achieving SOTA on long video benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal LLMs work well on short audio-video clips but struggle with long-form content due to limited context windows and high redundancy in video and audio streams. There is a need to efficiently condense long multimodal sequences into a compact representation that still preserves information necessary for answering user queries. Prior approaches don’t adequately exploit principled information retrieval criteria to decide which multimodal tokens to keep.

Method: AVOC inserts a learnable token compression module between audio/video encoders and the LLM backbone. The compression is formulated as a top-K retrieval task: under a fixed token budget, the module selects a subset of multimodal tokens most supportive for the query. It operationalizes three classical information retrieval criteria—relevance, importance, and diversity—by designing specific mechanisms for each in the context of audio-video understanding, then combines them into a unified retrieval-style compression pipeline that outputs compressed multimodal tokens for downstream reasoning by the Omni-modal LLM.

Result: On long-form audio-video benchmarks OmniVideoBench and LVOmniBench, AVOC achieves new state-of-the-art performance, outperforming the previous second-best model by 4.9 and 5.5 average accuracy points, respectively. It also shows strong robustness on an Audio-Video Needle-in-a-Haystack task for content durations up to one hour, indicating its effectiveness in handling very long sequences with sparse relevant information.

Conclusion: Token compression framed as retrieval over multimodal tokens is an effective solution for long-form audio-video understanding in Omni-modal LLMs. By integrating relevance, importance, and diversity into a learnable compression module, AVOC can select a compact yet highly informative subset of tokens, overcoming context length limits while improving accuracy on long-video benchmarks and maintaining performance even on very long, sparse-information tasks.

Abstract: Multimodal Large Language Models have achieved remarkable progress in short-form audio-video understanding, yet long-form audio-video comprehension remains challenged by limited context windows and severe information redundancy. To address these bottlenecks, we propose AVOC, a framework for long-form audio-video understanding in Omni-modal Large Language Models. AVOC introduces a learnable token compression module between the modality encoders and the LLM backbone. We reframe multimodal token compression as a top-$K$ retrieval problem: given a fixed context budget, the module must retrieve a compact subset of tokens that best supports answering the user query. We draw inspiration from three classical Information Retrieval criteria for selecting informative units from a large candidate pool: relevance, importance, and diversity. AVOC instantiates each criterion as a tailored mechanism for audio-video understanding, and integrates them into a unified retrieval-style compression pipeline. Experiments show that AVOC achieves state-of-the-art performance on long-form audio-video benchmarks, surpassing the second-best model by 4.9 and 5.5 points in average accuracy on OmniVideoBench and LVOmniBench, respectively. Moreover, AVOC maintains robust performance on Audio-Video Needle-in-a-Haystack task at durations up to one hour.

We also discuss how alignment methods, data provenance and benchmark saturation change what it means to call a model "state of the art". The final section lists the research questions that we think deserve more attention.

Abstract: Czech has been part of Universal Dependencies since its first release in 2015. It has also been one of the best represented languages, with the Prague Dependency Treebank being order of magnitude larger than most other UD treebanks. More recently, three other datasets from the Prague family were added and the annotations thoroughly revisited, forming the "Prague Dependency Treebank-Consolidated" (PDT-C). In comparison to the original PDT, PDT-C is more than twice as large, but it is also much more diverse in terms of genres and domains. In this paper, we describe the conversion of the new resource to Universal Dependencies. While the two annotation schemes are relatively similar at the first sight, there are numerous small differences in topology of the dependency structures and in granularity of the POS and relation type inventories. We demonstrate a selection of such differences on examples, discuss the diverging motivations, as well as ways to overcome the differences during conversion. We argue that while PDT is less "universal" and more tightly bound to one language, its multi-layer annotation is rich and provides all information needed for basic UD trees, and much more.

</details>


### [38] [Automatic Part-of-Speech Tagging of Arabic-English Dictionary Senses through WordNet](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24359)
*Diaa M. Fayed,Aly A. Fahmy,Mohsen A. Rashwan,Wafaa K. Fayed*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Algorithm for POS tagging senses in an Arabic-English bilingual dictionary by transferring POS from English translation equivalents using WordNet.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To enable linking a bilingual dictionary (Al-Mawrid Arabic-English) to WordNet and standardizing it into WordNet-LMF format, which requires POS-tagged senses; and to provide a resource-light NLP approach for poor-resource languages where expert-built resources and large annotated corpora are costly.

Method: Use English translation equivalents (TEs) of each dictionary sense, obtain their POS tags from Princeton WordNet, perform disambiguation to select the correct POS, and then transfer those POS tags to the corresponding bilingual dictionary senses.

Result: High tagging accuracy is achieved with relatively low development cost using this transfer-and-disambiguation approach on the Al-Mawrid dictionary.

Conclusion: POS tagging of bilingual dictionary senses via resource-light transfer from WordNet is effective and inexpensive, and serves as a crucial step toward integrating bilingual dictionaries with WordNet/WordNet-LMF for NLP tools in low-resource languages.

Abstract: This paper proposed an algorithm for part-of-speech (POS) tagging senses of a bilingual dictionary. The algorithm is applied on the Al-Mawrid Arabic-English dictionary. The tagging task is accomplished by transferring the POS tags of the English translation equivalences (TEs) to the dictionary senses after dis-ambiguities process. The English POS tags of senses are acquired from the Princeton WordNet. POS tagging of bilingual dictionary senses is prerequisite to link a bilingual dictionary to WordNet and/or standardizing that dictionary into WordNet-LMF format where the synset (set of synonyms), not word, is the basic brick. The registered accuracy is high though the cost is little. Building NLP/HLT tools needs linguistic experts, large investments, and long time. For statistical approach, we need large annotated corpora and for rule-based approach, we need large lexicon that contains rich linguistic and world knowledge. That motivates the appearance of what are called resource-light approaches to develop natural language processing (NLP) tools for poor-resource languages.

*Jaroslava Hlaváčová,Marie Mikulová,Barbora Štěpánková,Milan Straka,Jan Hajič*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Languages like Czech have highly complex but regular inflection and derivation, making naive morphological dictionaries extremely large and difficult to maintain consistently for annotation and NLP applications. There is a need for an architecture that exploits regularity to keep the resource compact, maintainable, and reliable while supporting large-scale treebanks and tools.

Method: Design a morphological dictionary architecture where the distributed form is a flat list of <wordform, lemma, tag> but the hidden source uses manually maintained inflectional and derivational patterns plus conversion scripts. These patterns systematically generate the huge set of wordforms and lemmas from a much smaller pattern-based specification, ensuring consistency and reducing maintenance cost. The paper also describes how this resource is integrated into annotation workflows and NLP tools.

Result: They developed MorfFlex CZ, a Czech morphological dictionary with over 100 million wordforms and more than 1 million lemmas, compactly represented through a system of inflectional and derivational patterns. It is successfully used as a key resource for Prague Dependency Treebanks and powers state-of-the-art tools like MorphoDiTa, improving annotation consistency and tool performance.

Conclusion: A pattern-based morphological dictionary architecture like MorfFlex effectively manages the complexity of rich morphological systems, enabling very large yet maintainable lexicons. Such resources are crucial for consistent corpus annotation and provide a strong foundation for advanced NLP tools and applications in morphologically rich languages.

Abstract: We present MorfFlex, a morphological dictionary architecture suitable for languages with extensive regularity in both inflection and derivation. As the primary example of MorfFlex in use we introduce MorfFlex CZ, a morphological dictionary of Czech. It is distributed as a simple, unstructured list of <wordform, lemma, tag> triplets, however, its manually maintained, unpublished source files and conversion scripts encode a sophisticated system of inflectional and derivational patterns. These patterns dramatically reduce the otherwise enormous size of the dictionary, which currently contains over 100 million wordforms and more than 1 million lemmas. The MorfFlex CZ dictionary serves as an essential resource for ensuring the consistency of manual morphological annotation in the Prague Dependency Treebanks and underpins state-of-the-art automatic tools such as MorphoDiTa. In this paper, we focus on: (i) presenting an effective method for managing the rich morphological system within the dictionary, and (ii) demonstrating the utility of such a language resource for maintaining annotation consistency in corpora and supporting the development of advanced NLP applications.

### [40] [On the Stability of Prompt Ranking in Large Language Model Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24381)
*Shaoshuai Du,Penghao Liang,Yixian Shen,Chuanqi Shi,Hang Zhang,Lun Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how stable prompt rankings are under common sources of randomness, finds that the top prompt is often not reliably identified, and proposes a variance-aware selection method to make prompt choice more robust.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prompt engineering typically selects the best prompt by ranking candidates on a validation set, assuming that the top-ranked prompt is stable. The authors suspect that small changes in random seeds or evaluation subsets might change which prompt appears best, undermining reliability of prompt selection and benchmarking.

Method: They systematically evaluate multiple candidate prompts across three open-weight LLMs and two benchmark tasks, repeatedly varying random seeds and using limited evaluation subsets. They measure rank correlations and track how often the identity of the top prompt changes. They then introduce a stability-aware selection rule using a lower confidence bound that trades off mean performance and variance, and compare it to standard best-mean selection under different stability regimes.

Result: They find that overall rank correlations between evaluations are often moderate to high, but the specific prompt chosen as “best” frequently changes with different seeds or subsets, indicating unstable top-1 selection. Their lower-confidence-bound-based selection strategy reduces the likelihood of picking an unstable or overestimated prompt, improving robustness in settings with high variability while performing similarly to standard selection when rankings are more stable.

Conclusion: Prompt rankings for LLMs are not as stable as commonly assumed, especially for the top-performing prompt, making naive best-mean selection unreliable. Incorporating evaluation uncertainty via a simple lower-confidence-bound rule yields more robust prompt selection without sacrificing performance in stable scenarios, and the work underscores the need to account for statistical uncertainty when comparing prompts and benchmarking LLMs.

Abstract: Prompt-based interaction has become a dominant paradigm for using large language models (LLMs), where multiple candidate prompts are evaluated and the top-ranked one is selected for downstream use. This workflow implicitly assumes that prompt rankings are stable under minor variations in evaluation conditions. In this paper, we systematically study prompt ranking stability under common sources of variability, including random seeds and limited evaluation subsets. Across three open-weight LLMs and two benchmark tasks, we find that while overall rank correlations are often moderate to high, the identity of the top-performing prompt frequently changes, leading to unreliable selection decisions. To address this issue, we propose a simple stability-aware selection strategy based on a lower confidence bound, which accounts for both performance and variance. Our results show that this approach improves robustness in unstable settings while remaining competitive in more stable regimes. These findings highlight the importance of accounting for evaluation uncertainty in prompt selection and LLM benchmarking.
