### [42] [Beyond Logprobs: A Multi-Signal Confidence Engine for LLM-Based Document Field Extraction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24420)
*Nitesh Kumar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose ExtractConf, a confidence estimation engine that decides when LLM-extracted document fields can be trusted or should go to humans, greatly improving reliability over standard log-prob baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In critical enterprise document workflows (finance, compliance, procurement), a confidently wrong LLM extraction is more harmful than a missing one. Existing confidence indicators such as token log-probs, verbalized confidence, or self-consistency fail because they tend to label nearly everything as high-confidence at realistic operating points, offering little separation between correct and incorrect field extractions. The authors want a better, field-level confidence mechanism that works across document types without retraining or domain-specific rules.

Method: They introduce ExtractConf, which creates two structurally different LLM 'views' of the same document. The Hunter call is schema/field-guided, prompting the model to fill each target field (which encourages hallucinations when fields are absent). The Mapper call is document-guided, asking the model to scan the document holistically and only surface values grounded in the text (so it tends to miss low-salience fields). The system then measures cross-call disagreements, combines them with LLM internal uncertainty signals (e.g., log-probs, self-consistency), OCR quality, image quality, and spatial-layout features, and feeds these into a classifier that outputs a per-field confidence score. The classifier is designed to be domain-agnostic and not require task-specific rules or retraining for new schemas.

Result: On the DocILE benchmark (invoices with 55 fields and a 26% base failure rate), ExtractConf attains a ROC AUC of 0.928 and reduces selective prediction risk by 70% compared with mean log-prob baselines. At 80% coverage (automating 80% of fields and sending 20% to humans), it achieves 99.1% accuracy, supporting practical human-in-the-loop deployment. When transferred zero-shot to the CORD receipts dataset, it yields a ROC AUC of 0.858. A simple post-hoc Lasso-based recalibration further improves calibration, reducing Expected Calibration Error by 89% and Brier score by 43%, demonstrating that the underlying signals generalize across document domains.

Conclusion: Cross-view disagreement between two complementary LLM extraction modes (field-guided Hunter and document-guided Mapper), when combined with other uncertainty and document-quality features, provides a strong, domain-agnostic signal for confidence estimation in document information extraction. ExtractConf delivers much more reliable field-level confidence than standard log-prob or verbalized confidence methods, scales across document types with minimal adaptation, and makes high-coverage, high-accuracy human-in-the-loop automation feasible for high-stakes document processing pipelines.

Abstract: In high-stakes document processing pipelines, including financial reconciliation, compliance verification, and procurement automation, an LLM extraction that is silently wrong is more dangerous than one that is visibly absent. The central challenge is not extraction accuracy alone but reliable confidence estimation: knowing, field by field, whether an extraction can be trusted for automation or deferred to human review. Token-level log-probabilities, verbalized confidence, and multi-sample self-consistency all collapse toward all-positive behaviour at practical thresholds, offering no reliable separation between trustworthy and untrustworthy extractions.
  We present ExtractConf, a cross-domain, field-agnostic confidence engine that grounds confidence estimation in two structurally different readings of the same document. A field-guided Hunter call extracts each field under schema-slot completion pressure; a document-guided Mapper call scans holistically and surfaces values grounded in document content. This asymmetry yields different failure modes: Hunter hallucinates values for absent fields, while Mapper misses visually non-salient ones. Their disagreement is independently informative. ExtractConf fuses cross-call disagreement, LLM-internal uncertainty, OCR, image quality, and spatial layout into a classifier requiring no domain-specific rules or retraining. On DocILE (55-field invoices, 26% failure rate), it achieves 0.928 ROC AUC and reduces selective prediction risk by 70% over logprob-mean. At 80% coverage, accuracy reaches 99.1%, enabling a practical human-in-the-loop workflow. Zero-shot transfer to CORD receipts achieves 0.858 AUC; lightweight Lasso recalibration reduces ECE by 89% and Brier by 43%, confirming the signals generalise across document domains.

</details>


### [43] [Escaping the Self-Confirmation Trap: An Execute-Distill-Verify Paradigm for Agentic Experience Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24428)
*Shiding Zhu,Yudi Qi,Yajie Wang,Jiaze Li,Chao Song,Yaorui Shi,Yibo Miao,Hanqi Gao,Kai Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes EDV, a multi-agent Execute-Distill-Verify framework that builds reliable experience for LLM agents and avoids self-confirmation traps, improving performance on long-horizon benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agents learn from experience in single-agent self-reflection loops, where the same model executes tasks, evaluates its own trajectories, and decides what to store in memory. This makes them prone to a Self-Confirmation Trap: incorrect but internally consistent task trajectories are misclassified as successful experiences, get stored, and then repeatedly reused, causing error accumulation and harming long-term performance. The authors aim to make experience learning more reliable and robust for open-world interaction and long-horizon tasks.

Method: The authors design EDV, an Execute-Distill-Verify framework that decouples execution, summarization, and validation into collaborative multi-agent stages. In the Execute stage, multiple heterogeneous LLM agents tackle the same task space in parallel, generating diverse trajectories instead of a single agent’s path. In the Distill stage, a separate third-party agent compares and analyzes these trajectories to extract candidate experiences, reducing bias from any single executor. In the Verify stage, the executor agents re-evaluate these distilled experiences using a consensus mechanism, and only those that pass verification are stored into shared or private memory. This pipeline transforms experience learning from isolated self-reflection into a multi-agent collaborative construction process that filters out erroneous or noisy experiences before insertion into memory.

Result: On three difficult long-horizon benchmarks—tau2-bench, Mind2Web, and MMTB—EDV consistently surpasses strong baseline methods for experience-based LLM agents. The experimental results empirically show that the EDV framework can more effectively build and reuse experience, leading to better task performance and more robust self-evolution over time.

Conclusion: Decoupling execution, distillation, and verification into a multi-agent Execute-Distill-Verify framework is an effective way to avoid self-confirmation traps in LLM agent experience learning. By collaboratively constructing and validating experiences before they are written to memory, EDV yields more reliable experience repositories and improves performance on long-horizon tasks. The work highlights that robust agent self-evolution in open-world settings depends critically on the quality and reliability of constructed experiences.

Abstract: Experience-driven self-evolution is critical for large language model (LLM) agents to improve through open-world interaction. However, existing experience learning methods mostly rely on single-agent loops, where the same agent executes tasks, summarizes outcomes, and determines memory content. This setup makes agents vulnerable to the Self-Confirmation Trap: wrong-but-self-consistent trajectories are misidentified as successful experience, leading to cumulative errors during retrieval and reuse. To address this issue, we propose EDV, an Execute-Distill-Verify framework for reliable experience learning. In the Execute stage, multiple heterogeneous agents explore the same task space in parallel to generate diverse candidate trajectories. In the Distill stage, a dedicated third-party agent comparatively analyzes these trajectories to produce candidate experiences, reducing executor-centric summarization bias. In the Verify stage, the execution group validates candidates via a consensus mechanism, and only approved experiences are written into shared or private memory. By decoupling the three stages, EDV transforms experience learning from isolated self-reflection into collaborative construction, filtering erroneous and noisy content before memory insertion. We evaluate EDV on three challenging long-horizon benchmarks: tau2-bench, Mind2Web and MMTB. Results show EDV consistently outperforms strong baselines, validating that reliable experience construction is essential for robust agent self-evolution. Our code is available at https://github.com/shidingz/EDV.

</details>


### [44] [The African Language Tax: Quantifying the Cost, Latency, and Context Penalty of Tokenizing African Languages in Frontier LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24460)
*Olaoye Anthony Somide*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper quantifies how existing LLM tokenizers structurally overcharge and under-serve African languages by assigning them many more tokens than English for the same content.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Commercial LLMs charge and limit usage per token, but tokenization schemes produce different token counts for equivalent content across languages. African languages are suspected to be especially disadvantaged, yet this has not been systematically measured from the perspective of cost, latency, and usable context in enterprise deployments.

Method: The authors analyze tokenization "fertility" for 20 African languages covering five language families and three scripts using parallel corpora (mainly FLORES-200+, plus MAFAND-MT for Nigerian Pidgin). They apply 11 frontier and open tokenizers, including GPT-5 / o200k_base, cl100k_base, and Gemma 4, and compute per-language token premiums relative to English. They examine consistency across corpora and translate tokenization premiums into concrete implications for inference cost, latency, and effective context window size. They also build and release a measurement tool (afri-fertility), leaderboard, dataset, and practical mitigation guidance.

Result: Every examined African language uses more tokens than English for the same content on all 11 tokenizers, with a median 1.88x premium on GPT-5 / o200k_base and extremes up to 8.92x for N'Ko. Scripts such as Ethiopic and N'Ko incur the largest penalties (7–9x). The fertility patterns are extremely stable across corpora, with a Pearson correlation of 0.9998 between FLORES-200+ and SIB-200. These token premiums translate into up to 8.9x higher inference costs, similar multipliers in generation latency (e.g., 7.4x for Amharic vs English), and an effective context window as low as 11% of English's for some languages. Gemma 4's tokenizer shows improved efficiency for African languages, lowering the mean premium from 3.31x to 2.38x, but still fails to remove the structural disadvantage.

Conclusion: Tokenization schemes used by current commercial and open LLMs systematically disadvantage African languages by inflating token counts, which directly increases costs, latency, and reduces usable context compared to English. This creates a built-in economic and functional inequity—a "digital divide"—encoded at the tokenizer-vocabulary level. While better tokenizers like Gemma 4 can partially mitigate the effect, no existing approach eliminates it, motivating targeted tokenizer design, evaluation tools (like afri-fertility), and practical strategies to reduce the burden on African language users and builders.

Abstract: Commercial large language models bill, scale latency, and budget context per token. Yet tokenizers assign more subword tokens to the same meaning in some languages than in others, so speakers of languages with high token-fertility pay a structural penalty before a model is ever invoked. This penalty is documented for multilingual settings in general, but it has not been measured systematically for African languages at the level of enterprise deployment economics and cognitive context capacity. We measure it across 20 African languages spanning five language families and three scripts (Latin, Ge'ez/Ethiopic, N'Ko; 19 appear in the primary FLORES-200+ corpus, with Nigerian Pidgin measured via MAFAND-MT only), using parallel corpora so that the language effect is isolated from content. Across 11 frontier and open tokenizers on FLORES-200+, every African language carries a tokenization premium above English (median 1.88x on GPT-5 / o200k_base, up to 8.92x for N'Ko); the penalty is largest for Ethiopic and N'Ko scripts (reaching 7-9x) and is near-invariant across corpora (FLORES vs SIB-200 Pearson r = 0.9998). Translated into deployment terms, this results in up to 8.9x inference cost and an equivalent generation-latency multiplier (N'Ko vs English on GPT-5; 7.4x for Amharic), and as little as 11% of English's effective context window. The best currently available tokenizer for African languages, Gemma 4, reduces the mean premium from 3.31x (cl100k_base) to 2.38x, but no tokenizer eliminates the penalty. We release an open measurement tool (afri-fertility), a public leaderboard, a results dataset, and mitigation guidance for African builders. The penalty falls hardest on the languages whose speakers can least afford it, a digital divide encoded directly into the subword vocabulary.

In Turkish and similar low-resource languages, there is little annotated data and weak technological defenses, leaving users exposed. The authors aim to close this gap by providing data and evaluation specific to Turkish scam detection and assessing how modern LLMs can help in such low-resource, high-risk contexts.

Method: The authors construct the first public Turkish multi-modal dataset for scam detection, containing 100 aligned audio–transcript pairs labeled as scam or benign conversations. The study underscores the importance of expanding AI safety and fraud-prevention research beyond high-resource languages and motivates the development of more inclusive, robust multi-modal systems tailored to low-resource, real-world threat scenarios.

Abstract: Scam phone calls exploit vulnerable communities worldwide, yet research on detection has focused almost exclusively on English and other high-resource languages. This research investigates how large language models (LLMs) can support scam detection in Turkish by introducing the first public multi-modal dataset of 100 aligned audio-transcript pairs of scam and benign conversations. Our results suggest that transcript-based inputs consistently outperform direct audio processing, while human-corrected and uncorrected transcripts perform comparably. By centering a low-resource language and real world threat, this work highlights the urgent need for culturally and linguistically inclusive AI safety research and more robust multi-modal systems for fraud prevention.

### [47] [AGORA: An Archive-Grounded Benchmark for Agentic Workplace Document Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24526)
*Honglin Guo,Qi Zhang,Yu Zhang,Weijie Li,Rui Zheng,Zhikai Lei,Qiyuan Peng,Zhiheng Xi,Tao Gui,Qi Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Agora, a challenging benchmark for testing how well language-model agents can reason over large, messy document archives using sparse evidence.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing benchmarks for language models focus on parametric knowledge or narrow retrieval tasks and do not simultaneously test archive-grounded reasoning, deliberate multi-step exploration, and robustness across diverse real-world domains. There is a need to measure how well agentic LLM systems handle realistic workplace file collections that are too large to fit into any context window and contain inconsistencies in terminology, units, and time conventions.

Method: The authors construct Agora, a benchmark of 362 questions paired with eight domain-specific collections comprising 9,664 authentic documents (372M tokens). They use an agentic pipeline that (1) synthesizes cross-document tasks, (2) obfuscates content to prevent information leakage, and (3) filters by difficulty. The benchmark is explicitly designed so that document collections exceed model context windows, forcing agents to explore and reason rather than exhaustively read everything.

Result: They evaluate eight large language models on Agora and observe that performance is substantially below saturation; the best model achieves only 59.4% accuracy and shows strong variation in performance across different domains. This suggests existing LLM agents are still weak at archive-grounded reasoning over large, messy document sets.

Conclusion: Agora is a demanding and realistic benchmark for assessing archive-grounded reasoning in LLM agents. The relatively low accuracy of current models and variability across domains indicate that archive-grounded, agentic exploration over large document collections remains an open challenge, highlighting the need for better methods and systems in this area.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly deployed as agents that reason over documents rather than answer from parametric knowledge. We study archive-grounded reasoning: locating sparse evidence across a large, messy collection of workplace files, reconciling inconsistent terminology, units, and time conventions, and computing an answer. Existing benchmarks address only parts of this setting and none jointly stresses archive-groundedness, agentic exploration, and cross-domain coverage. We introduce Agora, a benchmark pairing 362 questions with eight domain collections of 9,664 authentic documents and 372M tokens, far exceeding any model's context window, so agents must explore deliberately rather than scan exhaustively. Agora is built by an agentic pipeline combining cross-document task synthesis, leakage-preventing obfuscation, and difficulty filtering. Evaluating eight models, we find the task far from solved: even the strongest reaches only 59.4% accuracy, with notable variation across domains.

Our results demonstrate that cross-lingual exploration significantly improves knowledge transfer and factual recall, representing a more efficient compute Pareto frontier than native-language scaling. Furthermore, we observe corresponding improvements in cross-lingual consistency, exceeding what can be explained by accuracy gains alone. Overall, our work establishes multilingual prompt exploration as a highly effective inference-time strategy for unlocking latent parametric knowledge.

### [50] [MEMPROBE: Probing Long-Term Agent Memory via Hidden User-State Recovery](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24595)
*Enze Ma,Yufan Zhou,Wei-Chieh Huang,Jie Yang,Huanhuan Ma,Zixuan Wang,Chengze Li,Chunyu Miao,Philip S. Yu,Zhen Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MEMPROBE, a benchmark to directly evaluate what user information long-term memory systems in LLM agents actually retain, by reconstructing user state from stored memories instead of judging memory only via downstream task performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current evaluations of long-term memory in LLM agents rely mostly on indirect signals such as personalization quality, task success, or answer quality. These do not directly assess the fidelity and completeness of the underlying memory store and leave it largely unaudited. The authors want a way to explicitly measure and audit what structured information about users is truly retained across interactions, recognizing that good task performance can occur even with poor or shallow memory.

Method: They propose viewing memory as an auditable artifact and operationalize this via MEMPROBE. In this benchmark, simulated users each have a hidden, taxonomy-based user-state bank with 31 dimensions. Agents with different memory systems assist these users through a series of tasks designed to leak user-state information in a controlled way. After the interaction trajectory, the authors attempt to reconstruct the hidden user-state bank purely from the agent’s stored memories, evaluating under two settings: full memory-store access and limited top-k retrieval. They use synthetic ground-truth user states for scalability and run MEMPROBE on 50 users and 5 different representative memory systems.

Result: Experiments show that high task completion can be achieved even by a memoryless baseline, meaning task success saturates and is not diagnostic of memory quality. In contrast, the ability to recover user state from memory is moderate overall (around 0.6 category-balanced recovery) and degrades when only top-k retrieved memories are used. This demonstrates that recoverable memory and task performance are distinct capabilities and that current systems do not reliably store rich, balanced user representations.

Conclusion: MEMPROBE is presented as the first benchmark focused on direct evaluation of memory recovery in LLM agents, scoring reconstructed user states against ground truth. The authors argue that explicit recovery of user state should be a key optimization target for future memory architectures and training regimes, so that agents become more faithful and accurate in what they remember about users over long-term interactions.

Abstract: Long-term memory promises LLM agents that grow more capable across sessions, maintaining an accurate, evolving understanding of the user that interaction forms. In practice, however, this memory is evaluated mostly through downstream behavior, such as later answers, personalization quality, or task success, which tests that understanding only indirectly and leaves the memory artifact itself largely unaudited. We argue that long-term memory should instead be evaluated as an auditable post-interaction artifact: after ordinary assistance, what structured user state can be reconstructed from the memory the agent leaves behind? We instantiate this view in MEMPROBE, a benchmark in which a memory-equipped agent assists simulated users, each carrying a hidden, taxonomy-anchored user-state bank, across a trajectory of leak-controlled tasks, after which that bank is reconstructed from the agent's resulting memory under both full-store and top-k access. Built on synthetic ground truth for efficient, scalable measurement, MEMPROBE spans 50 simulated users with 31 hidden dimensions each (1,550 recovery targets) and tests 5 representative memory systems. Testing state-of-the-art memory agents, we find that successful assistance and recoverable memory behave as distinct capabilities. Task completion nearly saturates, even for a memoryless baseline, while category-balanced recovery stays moderate (about 0.6) and drops further under top-k retrieval. MEMPROBE is the first benchmark to study memory recovery directly, reconstructing the user state a system retains and scoring it against ground truth. We see recovery as a concrete objective for future memory agents to optimize, and MEMPROBE as a step toward an environment where agents are trained to remember their users, growing more faithful the longer they know them.

### [51] [To Compare, or Not to Compare: On Methodological Practices in Evaluating Social Bias](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24596)
*Federico Marcuzzi,Xuefei Ning,Roy Schwartz,Iryna Gurevych*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a unified framework for evaluating social bias in LLMs and shows that models appear much more biased in comparative question settings than in isolated ones, especially with Chain-of-Thought and larger models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work on social bias in LLMs gives conflicting results because benchmarks and setups differ a lot, especially in how questions are framed (isolated vs comparative). This fragmentation hides how structural factors of evaluation shape measured bias. The authors want a principled way to compare and understand these effects.

Method: They standardize a range of heterogeneous social-bias benchmarks into one controllable evaluation framework. Within this framework they systematically vary structural factors: isolated demographic assessments vs forced-choice comparative questions, presence vs absence of Chain-of-Thought, availability of neutral options, and degree of contextual specification. They then evaluate multiple LLM families and sizes under these controlled conditions.

Result: They find a large ‘paradigm gap’ between isolated and comparative settings: isolated questions tend to suppress explicit prejudice, while comparative forced-choice questions strongly elicit discriminatory preferences, especially when the context is underspecified. Chain-of-Thought amplifies this comparative bias. Bias signals remain even when models are given neutral fallback options or claim to respond randomly, and the strength of this comparative prejudice increases with model size across families.

Conclusion: Evaluations and real-world deployments must account for the structural framing of queries. Comparative, underspecified tasks can dangerously amplify latent bias, particularly with CoT and larger models. Researchers should actively use comparative settings to uncover hidden biases, but practitioners should avoid or carefully constrain comparative deployments in ambiguous, real-world applications.

Abstract: As Large Language Models are increasingly deployed in critical applications, robustly evaluating their social biases is paramount. However, the current literature suffers from widespread methodological fragmentation, which yields contradictory conclusions. This stems largely from ignoring the structural framing of benchmark-level evaluations. To resolve this, we introduce a unified and controllable framework that standardizes heterogeneous benchmarks to systematically contrast isolated demographic assessments with forced-choice comparative settings. Crucially, this allows us to disentangle the confounding effects of Chain-of-Thought reasoning, neutral fallback options, and other structural artifacts in social bias evaluations. Our evaluation across multiple model families reveals a massive, systematic paradigm gap: while isolated assessments limit prejudice activation, comparative settings act as aggressive catalysts for latent discrimination, a shift primarily driven by underspecified contexts. Alarmingly, CoT reasoning exacerbates social biases under comparative settings, and this systemic bias persists as a deterministic prejudice even when models are provided neutral fallback options or claim to answer randomly. Finally, we demonstrate that this comparative prejudice is a generalized phenomenon that scales positively with model size. Ultimately, we offer a crucial methodological guideline: while researchers must leverage comparative settings to robustly audit hidden biases, practitioners cannot safely rely on comparative deployments in ambiguous real-world tasks.

</details>

World modeling is shown to be a key ingredient for scaling general agents, improving both simulation quality and downstream task performance across multiple domains.

Abstract: A world model predicts environment dynamics based on current observations and actions, serving as a core cognitive mechanism for reasoning and planning. (i) We first focus on building foundation models for agentic environment simulation. We introduce Qwen-AgentWorld-35B-A3B and Qwen-AgentWorld-397B-A17B, the first language world models capable of simulating agentic environments covering 7 domains via long chain-of-thought reasoning. Leveraging more than 10M environment interaction trajectories of 7 domains in real-world environments, we develop Qwen-AgentWorld through a three-stage training pipeline: CPT injects general-purpose world modeling capabilities from the state transition dynamics and augmented professional corpora, SFT activates next-state-prediction reasoning, and RL sharpens simulation fidelity through a tailored framework with hybrid rubric-and-rule rewards. To evaluate language world models, we present AgentWorldBench, a comprehensive benchmark constructed from real-world interactions of 5 frontier models on 9 established benchmarks. Empirical results demonstrate that Qwen-AgentWorld significantly outperforms existing frontier models. (ii) Beyond foundation models, we further investigate two complementary paradigms through which world modeling enhances general agents. First, as a decoupled environment simulator, Qwen-AgentWorld supports scalable and controllable simulation of thousands of real-world environments for agentic RL, yielding gains that surpass real-environment training alone. Second, as a unified agent foundation model, world-model training acts as a highly effective warm-up that improves downstream performance across 7 agentic benchmarks. Abstract: The evaluation of cultural grounding context becomes complex when multiple cultures convey the same moral lesson. However, it remains uncertain whether these models preserve culturally grounded meaning when equivalent moral lessons are conveyed through distinct cultural forms. This study introduces a multilingual evaluation narrative framework that integrates a cross-linguistic collection of 414 proverbs spanning 15 languages and uses four LLMs to generate 13k narratives. By employing semantically equivalent proverbs as culturally grounded prompts, the analysis assesses whether models preserve meaning across languages, how cross-lingual conditioning influences narrative realization, and whether different model families converge on similar interpretations. Results indicate that cross-lingual prompting largely preserves proverb-level semantic meaning while systematically redistributing agency, social positioning, and narrative structure. Additionally, strong inter-model convergence is observed in both monolingual and cross-lingual settings, suggesting that multilingual LLMs rely on shared semantic abstractions despite architectural and linguistic differences. These findings shed light on the need for more comprehensive evaluations of cultural grounding. Relying exclusively on semantic similarity in multilingual narrative assessments may overestimate cultural preservation by neglecting culturally meaningful variations in narrative expression.

### [54] [Privacy-Preserving RAG via Multi-Agent Semantic Rewriting: Achieving Confidentiality Without Compromising Contextual Fidelity](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24623)
*Yuanhe Zhao,Tianyu Zhang,Huafei Xing,Derek F. Wong,Jianbin Li,Tao Fang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes a privacy-preserving multi-agent framework that rewrites retrieved documents in RAG systems to remove sensitive data while preserving semantics, significantly reducing privacy leakage without adding online latency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Retrieval-Augmented Generation systems fetch external documents that may contain private or sensitive data, which can be exposed via malicious prompts or targeted attacks. Existing defenses are limited in preserving utility while removing sensitive information, and often add latency to inference. There is a need for a systematic, efficient method to sanitize retrieved content before it is used by large language models.

Method: Introduce a multi-agent semantic rewriting pipeline with three specialized agents: (1) a privacy extraction agent that identifies sensitive identifiers and private information in retrieved documents, (2) a semantic analysis agent that captures and preserves the semantic core and contextual meaning of the text, and (3) a reconstruction agent that rewrites the document to remove or obfuscate sensitive elements while retaining utility. The system is deployed as an offline, asynchronous preprocessing step on the retriever’s corpus, so rewritten documents are used at inference without extra latency. The framework is evaluated on the ChatDoctor and Wiki-PII datasets across six LLM backbones and compared to the SAGE baseline using privacy leakage metrics and semantic similarity scores (e.g., BLEU-1).

Result: Under targeted privacy attacks, the framework dramatically reduces the number of successfully exposed private instances across multiple models. For LLaMA-3-8B, privacy exposure is reduced from 144 instances in the baseline to 1 after applying the method. At the same time, the approach maintains strong semantic fidelity: it achieves a BLEU-1 score of 0.122, slightly surpassing the SAGE method’s 0.117, indicating better preservation of contextual meaning while still providing stronger privacy protection.

Conclusion: Semantic rewriting via a multi-agent framework is an effective way to sanitize retrieved content in RAG systems, significantly mitigating privacy leakage from malicious prompts while preserving the usefulness of the information. Because the sanitization is performed offline as a preprocessing step, the method avoids additional latency in online inference. The publicly released code supports reproducibility and further research on privacy-preserving RAG architectures.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation enhances large language models by incorporating external knowledge, but deploying it in sensitive scenarios risks privacy leakage via malicious prompts. To address this, we propose a multi-agent framework that sanitizes retrieved content through semantic rewriting. By employing three specialized agents for privacy extraction, semantic analysis, and reconstruction, our approach collaboratively removes sensitive identifiers while preserving the semantic core. We evaluate the framework on the ChatDoctor and Wiki-PII datasets across six large language models. Experimental results demonstrate a significant reduction in privacy leakage under targeted attacks. For instance, we reduced targeted information exposure in LLaMA-3-8B from 144 instances in the baseline to just 1. Furthermore, we maintain strong contextual fidelity with a BLEU-1 score of 0.122, outperforming the existing SAGE method's 0.117. Finally, the framework operates as an asynchronous preprocessing module, introducing no additional latency to online inference, as all rewriting is executed as a one-time offline preprocessing step. To promote reproducibility, the source code of this work is publicly available at https://github.com/foursoils/Privacy-Preserving-RAG.

### [55] [The Warrant Gap: Claim-Conditioned Re-scoring for Fact-Checking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24627)
*Arka Ujjal Dey,John Collomosse*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based fact-checkers often label claims as Supported using evidence that doesn’t truly justify the claim. Existing structured decomposition methods lose important full-claim context, causing these warranting errors. There is a need for a method that preserves claim context while assessing whether evidence actually licenses the verdict, and for an automatic metric that reflects this warrant quality.

Method: They introduce SIFT, which takes evidence spans extracted by decomposition and re-scores them conditioned on the full original claim to better reflect whether they warrant the claim. Additionally, they define WSP (Warranted Supports Proportion), an automatic NLI-based check that measures the proportion of Supports verdicts whose cited evidence semantically entails the claim. They test these with multiple open-source LLM backbones on standard fact-checking datasets (FEVER, SciFact, 5PILS, DP).

Result: On dataset/model combinations where naive decomposition significantly hurts accuracy (up to 27.6 percentage points), SIFT recovers much of this lost accuracy and also increases WSP compared to directly prompting the backbone models. WSP, as a metric, aligns strongly with human-annotated gold evidence, achieving an AUC of 0.92 and precision of 0.98 when used to decide if evidence truly warrants a claim.

Conclusion: Claim-conditioned re-scoring of evidence (SIFT) makes LLM-based fact-checking more reliable by ensuring that cited spans genuinely warrant Supported verdicts. The accompanying WSP measure provides a high-quality automatic way to quantify how often Supports labels are actually justified, and it correlates well with human judgment across several fact-checking benchmarks and model backbones.

Abstract: Fact-checking systems built on LLMs achieve high verdict accuracy on standard benchmarks, yet routinely output Supports labels whose cited evidence does not license the claim. Structured decomposition is the natural way to inspect those warrants, but rigid extraction protocols strip the full-claim context that facets need. We introduce SIFT -- claim-conditioned re-scoring of extracted evidence spans against the full claim -- paired with WSP (Warranted Supports Proportion), an automatic NLI check that the cited warrant entails the claim. We evaluate on FEVER, SciFact, 5PILS, and DP across four open-source backbones. SIFT recovers accuracy on cells where naive decomposition costs up to 27.6 points, while raising WSP above direct prompting; WSP itself calibrates against human gold evidence at AUC 0.92 and precision 0.98.

### [57] [Harmonic: Hierarchical State Space Models for Efficient Long-Context Language Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24650)
*Petr Nyoma*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Harmonic is a hierarchical state space model for language modeling that uses multi-timescale recurrent levels driven by prediction errors, achieving better long‑context performance and linear compute complexity compared to Transformers and Mamba.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Transformers struggle with long sequences due to quadratic attention cost and memory limits, and positional encodings like RoPE impose practical context-length limits. There is a need for architectures that handle very long contexts with linear scaling in sequence length while maintaining or improving language modeling quality, and that can be integrated into existing LLMs without such positional constraints.

Method: The authors design Harmonic, a hierarchical SSM consisting of three stacked recurrent levels operating at progressively slower timescales. Instead of passing raw hidden states upward, each level sends its prediction error to the slower level above, which then refines the representation. They compare Harmonic against similarly sized Transformers and Mamba models on standard language modeling benchmarks (enwiki8 and WikiText-103), evaluating bits-per-token (bpt) at various context lengths (1K–64K). At larger scale, they replace all attention layers in the TinyLlama 1.1B model with a HarmonicBlock to create Hallamonic, and study its loss behavior across sequence lengths beyond RoPE’s designed limit on benchmarks like Lambada and fineweb-edu held-out. Complexity analysis focuses on O(L) compute per forward pass vs. O(L^2) for attention.

Result: On enwiki8, with equal token budgets and ~28M parameters, Harmonic beats a comparable Transformer by +1.4% (1K tokens), +6.7% (8K), and +11.4% (32K) in bits-per-token, and also outperforms Mamba by 0.7–1.8% across tested lengths. At 64K tokens, Mamba and Transformer exhaust 80GB H100 memory and cannot train, while Harmonic successfully trains and reaches 6.169 bpt. Similar trends hold on WikiText-103, where Harmonic’s advantage over the Transformer ranges from +1.7% to +7.2% bpt across 1K–32K. At 1B scale, replacing all attention layers in TinyLlama with HarmonicBlock yields Hallamonic, which maintains stable loss from 1K to 8K sequence lengths on Lambada and fineweb-edu held-out, while TinyLlama’s performance collapses beyond its 2K RoPE limit, with a gap of +9.4 bpt at 8K tokens on Lambada. Harmonic’s forward compute scales linearly with sequence length.

Conclusion: A hierarchical, prediction-error-driven SSM can match or surpass Transformer and Mamba baselines for language modeling, especially at long context lengths, while offering linear-time scaling and better memory efficiency. Integrating HarmonicBlocks into existing LLM architectures removes hard positional encoding limits (like RoPE’s) and yields more robust performance on sequences far beyond the original context window, suggesting Harmonic as a promising alternative building block for scalable long-context language models.

Abstract: We present Harmonic, a hierarchical state space model (SSM) for language modeling. The architecture stacks three recurrent levels at progressively slower timescales; each level receives the prediction error of the level below as input, rather than its raw hidden state. On enwiki8 with equal token budgets, Harmonic outperforms a comparable Transformer (28M params) by +1.4% at 1K tokens, +6.7% at 8K tokens, and +11.4% at 32K tokens (bpt, lower is better). It also outperforms Mamba at every tested length by 0.7--1.8%. At 64K tokens, both Mamba and Transformer run out of memory on an 80GB H100; Harmonic trains successfully, reaching 6.169 bpt. Results replicate on WikiText-103 (H-TF gap +1.7% to +7.2% across 1K--32K). At 1B parameter scale, replacing all attention layers in TinyLlama 1.1B with HarmonicBlock eliminates the RoPE positional encoding limit: the resulting Hallamonic model maintains stable loss across sequence lengths 1K--8K on two independent clean benchmarks (Lambada and fineweb-edu held-out), while TinyLlama degrades catastrophically past its 2K-token RoPE limit (gap: +9.4 bpt at seq=8K on Lambada). Compute is O(L) per forward pass vs. O(L^2) for attention.
  Logs: https://github.com/Omibranch/harmonic-logs.

</details>

### [59] [DREAM: Dense Retrieval Embeddings via Autoregressive Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24667)
*Yixuan Tang,Yi Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes DREAM, a method to train dense retrieval embeddings using only the next-token prediction signal from a frozen LLM instead of contrastive supervision with labeled positives/negatives.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Dense retrievers are central to retrieval-augmented AI systems but typically need contrastive training data with labeled query–document pairs, which is expensive and hard to obtain. The authors want to leverage the already-available supervision signal from large language models’ autoregressive training (next-token prediction) to train dense retrievers without explicit contrastive labels.

Method: They introduce DREAM (Dense Retrieval Embeddings via Autoregressive Modeling). A separate dense retriever produces similarity scores between a query and candidate documents. These scores are injected into selected attention heads of a frozen LLM, influencing how much attention each document receives when the LLM performs next-token prediction for a target output. The LLM itself is frozen; its next-token loss is backpropagated through the modified attention mechanism to update only the retriever parameters, thereby learning retrieval embeddings from autoregressive supervision.

Result: On standard retrieval benchmarks BEIR and RTEB, using embedding backbones between 0.5B and 3B parameters, DREAM outperforms existing dense retrieval baselines consistently across scales, indicating that autoregressive modeling can effectively supervise dense retriever training.

Conclusion: DREAM shows that next-token prediction in a frozen LLM can be repurposed as a training signal for dense retrieval, providing a competitive alternative to contrastive learning that avoids the need for expensive labeled positive/negative document pairs and scales effectively across model sizes.

Abstract: Dense retrieval embedding models are a fundamental component of modern retrieval-based AI systems. Most dense retrievers are trained with contrastive objectives, which require labeled positive and negative document pairs that are often costly and difficult to obtain. In this work, we investigate whether the autoregressive next-token prediction objective of a large language model (LLM) can provide supervision for dense retrieval. The intuition is simple: if a document contains information relevant to a query, conditioning on that document should make the target output easier for the LLM to predict. A key challenge is that the next-token prediction loss is computed inside the LLM, while the retriever is a separate embedding model. To address this challenge, we propose DREAM (Dense Retrieval Embeddings via Autoregressive Modeling), which injects retriever-generated query-document similarity scores into selected attention heads of a frozen LLM. During training, these scores determine how much attention each candidate document receives while the LLM predicts the target output. The resulting prediction loss provides gradients for retriever training through the attention mechanism. We evaluate DREAM on retrieval benchmarks BEIR and RTEB using embedding backbones ranging from 0.5B to 3B parameters. DREAM consistently outperforms existing baselines across different model scales. These results demonstrate that DREAM provides a promising approach for training dense retrievers through autoregressive modeling.

</details>

### [61] [Task Decomposition for Efficient Annotation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24734)
*Nupoor Gandhi,Emma Strubell*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose breaking complex structured annotation into smaller sub-tasks centered on key entities, formally model ‘inferential load’, and show that such decomposition lets mixed groups of human and model annotators produce high-quality annotations more cheaply under a fixed budget.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Structured annotations (e.g., discourse, syntax, semantic roles) over large corpora are crucial but very costly. Full-example, end-to-end annotation is cognitively demanding for humans and still expensive for models because of validation and supervision needs. Modern projects mix different annotator types (various humans plus models), yet we lack principled ways to redesign tasks so that different annotators focus on what they do best while keeping total cognitive/annotation cost low.

Method: They introduce a formal model of ‘inferential load’ based on the number of degrees of freedom in the space of valid annotations, inspired by centering theory’s notion of centers. They then propose decomposing complex annotation tasks into sub-tasks that first identify salient ‘centers’ (anchor entities) and then annotate around them, which reduces the effective output space for subsequent decisions. They offer practical guidelines for designing such decompositions and a procedure for optimally assigning sub-tasks to heterogeneous annotators (different humans, models) subject to a budget constraint.

Result: Using their inferential-load model, they demonstrate that decompositions which isolate and prioritize center identification reduce the aggregate inferential load of an annotation project, thereby lowering cost for a given quality level. They illustrate these gains in cost-efficiency via examples drawn from their prior work, showing that carefully designed sub-tasks can make better use of mixed annotator pools.

Conclusion: Task decomposition around identifiable centers can substantially reduce the cognitive and statistical complexity of structured annotation, enabling cheaper and higher-quality annotations in settings with heterogeneous annotators. Their framework provides both a theoretical justification (via inferential load) and practical recipes for decomposing tasks and assigning sub-tasks so as to optimize annotation quality under fixed resource budgets.

Abstract: High-quality annotations of structured representations are expensive to collect over large corpora. Manual annotation of structure is laborious, and model-based annotation, although cheaper to generate, requires expensive validation and potentially significant supervision to ensure that the annotation quality is strong enough to be useful downstream. In traditional annotation workflows, annotation of each complete example is performed end-to-end by a single annotator. However, structured annotation is complex, and each aspect of the task represents a unique challenge with an associated inferential load for a given annotator. Modern annotation projects can incorporate heterogeneous groups of annotators, including both models and human annotators with varying domain and linguistic expertise. It remains unclear, however, how to redesign annotation tasks in this setting, where efforts are discriminately allocated across heterogeneous annotators with respect to distinct annotation challenges.
  We propose to decompose annotation tasks into sub-tasks in order to reduce the aggregate inferential load of annotation projects. Inspired by the notion of centers from centering theory, we introduce a formal model of inferential load based on the degrees of freedom in the space of valid annotations. Using this model, we show that identifying these centers (i.e. salient anchor entities realized by annotation sub-tasks) constrains the output space complexity, and decompositions which isolate and advance center identification reduce the aggregate inferential load. We provide guidelines for decomposing complex structured annotation tasks, supported by examples demonstrating improved cost-efficiency from our prior work. Finally, we present a procedure for allocating sub-tasks across annotators to maximize quality under a fixed budget.

### [62] [CANDLE: Character-level Arabic Noise Deduplication using Lightweight Encoder](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24758)
*Faris Alasmary,Taif Nono,Orjuwan Zaafarani,Kholood Al Tabash,Ahmad Ghannam,Anas Salamah,Shouq Sadah,Lahouari Ghouti*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CANDLE introduces a lightweight, CTC-based model to automatically remove noisy repeated characters in Arabic text, improving both normalization quality and downstream tokenizer efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Repeated characters in Arabic text can either be correct spelling or informal elongation, especially in social media. Existing approaches often depend on rules, dictionaries, or morphological analyzers, which can be brittle and resource-heavy. There is a need for a language-agnostic, learning-based method that robustly distinguishes genuine spelling from noise, functions well on diverse text types, and reduces downstream NLP costs.

Method: The authors propose CANDLE, a character-level Arabic noise deduplication system built around a novel use of Connectionist Temporal Classification (CTC). They treat normalization as a sequence alignment problem: a character-based encoder predicts an aligned, deduplicated character sequence without handcrafted rules or external linguistic resources. They train a 6-layer CTC model and then apply knowledge distillation to compress it into a 2-layer student model to lower inference cost. They evaluate across three datasets: clean newspaper text, manually curated ambiguous cases, and noisy social media data, and compare to a classification-based baseline.

Result: The CTC-based CANDLE model achieves a Sentence Error Rate as low as 5.37% and substantially outperforms a classification-style baseline on all three benchmarks. Distillation from a 6-layer to a 2-layer model yields a 3x reduction in depth with only minimal loss in accuracy. Applying the deduplication as a preprocessing step leads to up to 12.8% relative reduction in tokenizer fertility across several Arabic LLM tokenizers, indicating fewer subword tokens per input and potential savings in inference cost and improved context window usage.

Conclusion: CTC is an effective formulation for character deduplication in Arabic, enabling accurate, rule-free normalization of repeated characters across both clean and noisy domains. The compressed CANDLE model remains efficient while preserving performance, and the normalization step provides concrete downstream benefits by reducing token counts for Arabic LLMs. Public release of code and models facilitates reproducibility and future extensions of this approach.

Abstract: Handling repeated characters in text can be tricky, since they can represent either the correct spelling of a word or informal character elongation often seen in social media posts. We present CANDLE, a lightweight system for character-level Arabic noise deduplication that addresses this challenge without relying on handcrafted rules, dictionaries, or morphological analyzers. At the heart of CANDLE is a novel application of Connectionist Temporal Classification (CTC) to this task, a formulation not previously explored for character deduplication, which frames normalization as a sequence alignment problem over a character-based encoder. Evaluated on three benchmarks spanning clean newspaper, manually curated ambiguous cases, and real-world social media text, the CTC model achieves a Sentence Error Rate (SER) as low as $5.37\%$ and consistently outperforms a classification-based baseline by a large margin. To reduce inference overhead, we distill the 6-layer CTC model into a 2-layer student, achieving a $3\times$ depth reduction with minimal performance degradation. Beyond deduplication accuracy, normalization yields a practical downstream benefit: a relative reduction in tokenizer fertility of up to $12.8\%$ across a diverse set of Arabic LLM tokenizers, directly lowering inference costs and improving context window utilization. We release all code and models publicly to support reproducibility and advance future research\footnote{https://github.com/abjadai/candle}.

### [63] [Posterior Refinement: Fast Language Generation via Any-Order Flow Maps](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24773)
*Manan Agarwal,Sheel Shah,Chanhyuk Lee,Jaehoon Yoo,Jerry Huang,Seunghoon Hong,Aditi Raghunathan,Jinwoo Kim,Nicholas M. Boffi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes FMLM+, a new non-autoregressive language modeling framework that combines the strengths of Masked Diffusion Models and Flow Map Language Models, enabling fast, high-quality generation with iterative refinement using far fewer function evaluations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Non-autoregressive generation can in principle refine text by repeatedly masking and regenerating parts of a sequence, but current approaches underperform. Masked Diffusion Models degrade when generating many tokens at once due to factorization error, while Flow Map Language Models, though strong in few-step generation, lack flexible inference-time refinement. There is a need for a method that preserves FMLM’s quality and efficiency while regaining the adaptability and iterative correction capabilities found in masking-based approaches.

Method: The authors extend Flow Map Language Models into FMLM+ by introducing masking-style noise schedules into the flow-based framework. During one-shot full-sequence generation, FMLM+ also computes a posterior consistency score for every token, indicating how globally compatible each token is with the rest of the sequence. They then introduce Posterior Refinement, an inference-time strategy that selectively masks and regenerates tokens with low posterior scores, allowing adaptive self-correction over a small number of steps, all within the FMLM+ architecture.

Result: Empirical evaluations on multiple language modeling benchmarks show that FMLM+ with Posterior Refinement achieves a better speed–quality tradeoff than both Masked Diffusion Models and standard Flow Map Language Models. Specifically, the approach matches the performance of strong discrete baselines while requiring up to 32 times fewer neural function evaluations, indicating substantial efficiency gains without sacrificing output quality.

Conclusion: FMLM+ effectively reconciles the flexibility of masking-based non-autoregressive models with the efficiency and quality of flow-based generation. By jointly generating sequences and scoring token-level posterior consistency, and then using those scores for targeted Posterior Refinement, the method delivers scalable, high-fidelity language modeling with significantly reduced computational cost. This framework positions non-autoregressive models as competitive and practical alternatives for fast, high-quality text generation with iterative refinement.

Abstract: Non-autoregressive generation offers a powerful paradigm for iterative refinement, allowing models to recursively critique, erase and regenerate arbitrary subsets of tokens. However, existing non-autoregressive models fail to realize this potential. Masked Diffusion Models (MDMs) suffer from factorization error, causing sample quality to collapse when generating multiple tokens simultaneously. Flow Map Language Models (FMLMs) circumvent this bottleneck via joint sequence transport for excellent few-step generation, but sacrifice the inference-time flexibility of MDMs. We introduce FMLM+, a framework that bridges this gap by equipping FMLM with masking-style noise schedules. While generating the full sequence in a single step, FMLM+ simultaneously scores the global consistency of each token a posteriori. We leverage this to introduce Posterior Refinement, a novel inference-time refinement strategy that enables the model to adaptively self-correct its outputs, matching the performance of discrete baselines with 32x fewer NFEs. Across diverse benchmarks, we demonstrate that FMLM+ with Posterior Refinement improves the speed--quality tradeoff over both MDM and FMLM families, providing a scalable foundation for high-fidelity language modeling.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agent memories are becoming complex data-management systems, but they are still evaluated mainly via task-level metrics, treating memory as a black box. This leaves gaps in understanding system-level behavior, such as cost, robustness to updates, and architectural trade-offs. The paper aims to open this black box and provide a principled, empirical understanding of how different memory designs behave under diverse workloads.

Method: The authors propose an analytical framework that breaks agent memory into four modules: (1) memory representation and storage, (2) extraction, (3) retrieval and routing, and (4) maintenance. Within this framework, they select 12 representative memory systems plus two baselines and evaluate them over five benchmark workloads covering 11 datasets. They perform: (a) end-to-end evaluations on tasks, (b) fine-grained ablation studies to isolate module contributions, and (c) cost–performance analyses focusing on different maintenance strategies (localized vs global).

Result: Experiments show that no single memory architecture performs best across all workloads. Performance depends strongly on the match between memory structure and workload bottlenecks. Ablation studies demonstrate differing impacts of each module on representation fidelity, retrieval precision, update correctness, and long-horizon stability. Cost analyses indicate that localized maintenance operations yield better cost-efficiency than global reorganizations under realistic settings.

Conclusion: Agent memory should be designed as an agent-native, modular data-management system rather than a monolithic component, with architecture tailored to workload characteristics. Localized maintenance strategies are particularly promising for balancing performance and cost. The provided framework, benchmarks, and code base offer a basis for more principled design and evaluation of LLM agent memory systems.

Abstract: Memory for large language model (LLM) agents has rapidly evolved from simple retrieval-augmented mechanisms into a data management system that supports persistent information storage, retrieval, update, consolidation, and dynamic lifecycle governance throughout agent execution. Despite this evolution, existing evaluations still benchmark agent memory mainly through end-to-end task success metrics (e.g., F1, BLEU), while treating the underlying system as a monolithic black box. As a result, critical system-level concerns, including operational costs, architectural trade-offs across memory modules, and robustness under dynamic knowledge updates, remain insufficiently explored. In this paper, we present a systematic experimental study of agent memory from a data management perspective. We propose an analytical framework that decomposes agent memory into four core modules: memory representation and storage, extraction, retrieval and routing, and maintenance. Under this framework, we evaluate 12 representative memory systems and two reference baselines across five benchmark workloads spanning 11 datasets. Our extensive end-to-end evaluation shows that no single architecture dominates across all scenarios; instead, effectiveness depends heavily on how well the memory structure aligns with the workload bottleneck. Furthermore, through fine-grained ablation studies, we quantify their individual effects on representation fidelity, retrieval precision, update correctness, and long-horizon stability. Finally, we reveal cost-performance trade-offs under realistic workloads, showing localized maintenance is more cost-efficient than global reorganization. Based on these findings, we identify promising directions towards building truly agent-native memory systems. The code is publicly available at https://github.com/OpenDataBox/MemoryData.

They advocate redirecting NLP research toward these information-market problems to better align with emerging agentic commerce ecosystems.

Abstract: Commercial NLP treats the shopping chatbot as a recommender or a conversion tool: its job is to match a user to a catalogue entry and close a sale. We sketch the architecture of such a market and argue that it rewards genuine product quality and yields truer competition than ranking-based storefronts. Its rich morphology, free word order, lack of capitalization, and frequent code-mixing make computational modeling difficult, and there were no robust gold-standard datasets or benchmarks to drive progress.

Method: The authors construct a gold-standard POS dataset of 32,354 Marathi news sentences, annotated manually by Marathi-proficient annotators using a 16-tag scheme aligned with Universal Dependencies. Despite Marathi being spoken by over 83 million people and ranking among the top twenty most spoken languages worldwide, it remains severely under-resourced in annotated corpora and standardised evaluation benchmarks. Marathi presents unique challenges for computational modelling owing to its rich morphology, relatively free word order, lack of capitalisation conventions, and pervasive code-mixing with Hindi and English. We introduce L3Cube-MahaPOS, a gold-standard POS tagging dataset for Marathi comprising 32,354 manually annotated sentences drawn from news text. Annotation was performed entirely manually by a team of Marathi-proficient annotators following a 16-tag Universal Dependencies-aligned scheme. A structured preprocessing pipeline covering Unicode normalisation, Devanagari-aware tokenisation, and noise filtering ensures label consistency across all splits. We benchmark the dataset across six model families spanning HMM, CRF, BiLSTM, BiLSTM+CharCNN, MuRIL, and the Marathi-specific transformer MahaBERT-v2. The best system achieves 88.67\% token-level accuracy and a macro-F1 of 81.67% over 15 evaluated tag classes. We release the dataset, annotation guidelines, and trained model checkpoints to foster further research in Marathi NLP.

</details>


### [68] [Less is More: Quality-Aware Training Data Selection for Scientific Summarization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24828)
*Maria Nefeli Paraskevopoulou,Tatiana Passali,Grigorios Tsoumakas*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper builds a very large biomedical long-document summarization dataset and shows that selecting only high-quality author abstracts as training data improves factual summarization compared to random data selection.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing scientific summarization datasets are too small and not well structured for modern long-context models, and they rely on author-written abstracts as gold summaries even though these abstracts often differ in quality and alignment with the full article. There is a need both for larger, better-suited datasets and for understanding how the variable quality of reference abstracts affects training and evaluation.

Method: The authors construct a large-scale dataset of 1.88 million full-text PMC biomedical and life science articles paired with their author-written abstracts. They then evaluate the quality of these abstracts as reference summaries using source-grounded metrics (which compare abstract content to the full paper) and model-based metrics. Using these quality signals, they create different subsets of the data (high-quality vs randomly sampled) and train summarization models on them to compare performance, especially on factual correctness metrics.

Result: The analysis reveals substantial variation in how well author-written abstracts are aligned with and grounded in their source articles. Models trained on subsets filtered for higher reference quality outperform models trained on randomly sampled data of the same size, and in some cases perform as well as or better than models trained on much larger random subsets, especially on factuality-oriented evaluation metrics.

Conclusion: Reference summary quality significantly influences the effectiveness of training scientific summarization models. By quantifying and exploiting quality signals in author-written abstracts, one can select better training data, leading to more factual and efficient summarization. Large, quality-annotated datasets like the one released in this work provide a valuable resource for developing long-context biomedical summarization systems.

Abstract: Scientific long-document summarization datasets commonly treat author-written abstracts as gold reference summaries, although their quality and alignment with the source article vary. At the same time, publicly available scientific summarization datasets remain limited in scale and structure for modern long-context models. In this work, we address both challenges by a) constructing and releasing one of the largest biomedical and life science datasets for long-document summarization, containing 1.88 million PMC articles, and b) analyzing the reference quality of author-written abstracts with source-grounded and model-based metrics. We show that author-written abstracts vary in their alignment with the full article and that these quality signals can guide training-data selection. Training on selected high-quality subsets outperforms random sampling at matched training sizes and can match or exceed larger random subsets on factuality-oriented metrics. Our findings suggest that reference quality is an important factor in scientific summarization and that quality-aware data selection can improve training efficiency.

### [69] [RIFT-Bench: Dynamic Red-teaming For Agentic AI Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23927)
*Yarin Yerushalmi Levi,Roy Betser,Amit Giloni,Lidor Erez,Itay Gershon,Oren Rachmil,Sindhu Padakandla,Roman Vainshtein*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes RIFT-Bench, a unified, graph-based, dynamic red-teaming benchmark and methodology for evaluating the security of agentic AI systems built on LLMs across heterogeneous architectures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Agentic AI systems using LLMs are becoming autonomous and introduce new, system-level attack surfaces not captured by traditional LLM security tests. Current evaluations are often bespoke to a particular system or domain, making it hard to compare security across different agent frameworks and architectures. There is a need for a scalable, unified way to discover and test vulnerabilities across diverse agentic systems and to systematically evaluate mitigations.

Method: The authors design RIFT-Bench, a two-phase dynamic red-teaming pipeline based on a novel hierarchical graph representation of agentic systems. In the Discovery phase, RIFT-Bench automatically extracts the system’s structure and builds a graph capturing components, tools, and interactions. In the Scanning phase, it uses this graph to deploy adaptive adversarial attacks (adversarial probes) targeting multiple attack vectors and objectives, then generates an automated, comprehensive security report. The framework is architecture-agnostic and supports dynamically adapting probes to the discovered system and testing different mitigation strategies.

Result: RIFT-Bench is applied to 45 different agentic AI systems spanning a wide variety of implementations and architectures. The experiments show that RIFT-Bench can generalize its discovery and scanning process across this heterogeneous set, successfully identifying vulnerabilities and characterizing security properties in a unified way. The framework also demonstrates its ability to evaluate the impact and effectiveness of various mitigation strategies within these systems.

Conclusion: RIFT-Bench provides a scalable, architecture-agnostic methodology for security evaluation of agentic AI systems. By using a graph-based discovery of system structure and adaptive adversarial scanning, it enables unified, automated, and comparable red-teaming across heterogeneous agent frameworks, and can also be used to assess and iterate on mitigation strategies. The authors argue that this makes RIFT-Bench a strong foundation for future work on securing agentic AI systems.

Abstract: Agentic AI systems powered by large language models (LLMs) are rapidly evolving into autonomous decision-making systems, exposing attack vectors beyond those of traditional LLM vulnerabilities. Existing security evaluations are often tied to specific implementations or domains, limiting unified comparison across heterogeneous systems. To address this gap, we introduce RIFT-Bench, a graph representation-driven methodology for dynamic red-teaming that enables unified evaluations across diverse agentic architectures. Building on a novel hierarchical representation, RIFT-Bench operates in two automated phases: Discovery, which extracts system structure, and Scanning, which deploys adaptive adversarial attacks and produces a comprehensive evaluation report. It evaluates the examined system itself, leveraging a broad set of dynamically adaptable adversarial probes across diverse attack vectors and objectives. We demonstrate the effectiveness of the proposed evaluation pipeline across 45 agentic systems spanning a diverse range of implementations, showing that the approach generalizes effectively to heterogeneous agentic architectures. Beyond systems and attacks, RIFT-Bench also supports direct evaluation of mitigation strategies. These key capabilities make RIFT-Bench a scalable foundation for security evaluation of agentic AI systems.

</details>


### [70] [Neuro-Symbolic Drive: Rule-Grounded Faithful Reasoning for Driving VLAs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23938)
*Xiangbo Gao,Xiukun Huang,Boyu Lu,Junge Zhang,Mengjie Mao,Jiachen Li,Wei Xiong,Zhengzhong Tu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Neuro-Symbolic Drive uses rule-based planner traces to supervise a driving vision-language-action (VLA) model, creating causally grounded chain-of-thought reasoning and improving trajectory prediction accuracy and safety metrics in simulation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing driving VLAs with Chain-of-Thought reasoning produce natural-language rationales that are often loosely related to the actual planned motion, lacking clear step-by-step decision semantics that are causally tied to the executed trajectory. The authors want a way to make the reasoning both interpretable and structurally coupled to the low-level driving actions, and they observe that classical rule-based planners already implement explicit, executable reasoning over safety constraints and maneuvers.

Method: Instrument a classical rule-based driving planner in simulation to log, at every rule-evaluation step, its internal decision trace along with the final executed trajectory. Serialize these traces into structured, rule-grounded reasoning descriptions that reflect safety checks, maneuver search, and trajectory selection. Pair these reasoning traces with the corresponding trajectories as supervision to fine-tune Qwen3.5-4B into a driving vision-language-action model, so that its generated chain-of-thought mirrors the planner’s symbolic logic and is by construction aligned with motion generation.

Result: On a simulator-generated benchmark, training with detailed rule-grounded reasoning substantially improves prediction performance. Under three-camera perception, average displacement error at 3s improves from 0.47 to 0.26 and miss rate from 8.30% to 6.40%. Under eight-camera perception, ADE@3s improves from 0.54 to 0.26 and miss rate from 10.13% to 5.99%. These gains show that planner-derived reasoning supervision benefits both accuracy and safety-related metrics.

Conclusion: Neuro-Symbolic Drive demonstrates that classical rule-based planners can serve as rich, executable sources of reasoning supervision for driving VLAs. By converting the planner’s neuro-symbolic logic into structured rule-grounded traces and using them to train a vision-language-action model, the approach yields causally connected, interpretable chain-of-thought and significantly better trajectory prediction in simulation. This suggests a promising path for combining symbolic planning and large multimodal models in autonomous driving.

Abstract: Driving VLA models incorporating Chain-of-Thought (CoT) reasoning are attractive because they leverage pretrained VLM representations and expose intermediate decisions in natural language, yet current rationales often lack the step-by-step decision semantics needed to keep the rationale causally connected to the planned motion. We introduce Neuro-Symbolic Drive, a neuro-symbolic driving framework that supervises a driving VLA with rule-grounded reasoning traces extracted directly from classical rule-based planners. Our key observation is that rule-based planners are symbolic AI systems that already function as executable reasoning engines: they reason about active safety constraints, search over candidate maneuvers, and select a final trajectory. We instrument these planners in simulation to capture both the executed trajectory and the internal decision trace at each rule-evaluation step. Each trace is serialized into structured rule-grounded reasoning and paired with the trajectory to fine-tune Qwen3.5-4B as a driving VLA. Because these traces are derived directly from the planner states that determine the action, they ensure reasoning is structurally coupled to motion generation by construction, rather than by post-hoc alignment. On our simulator-generated benchmark, detailed rule-grounded reasoning reduces ADE@3s from 0.47 to 0.26 and miss rate from 8.30% to 6.40% under three-camera perception, and from 0.54 to 0.26 and 10.13% to 5.99% under eight-camera perception. Neuro-Symbolic Drive thus converts neuro-symbolic planning logic into structured supervision. Code base: https://github.com/XiangboGaoBarry/Neural-Symbolic-Drive.
