### [71] [Critique of Agent Model](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.23991)
*Eric Xing,Mingkai Deng,Jinyu Hou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper distinguishes between mere automated “agentic” systems and truly autonomous “agentive” AI, and proposes an architecture (GIC) aimed at realizing genuine machine agency while keeping it auditable and controllable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM-based tools are marketed as agents and raise both hype (productivity, autonomy) and fear (loss of control, speculative machine agency), but there is conceptual confusion over what constitutes real agency in AI. The authors want to clarify the boundary between automation and genuine agency to guide both system design and safety discussions.

Method: They conceptually analyze “agency” drawing from philosophy (Descartes) and science fiction, then survey existing AI agent systems and decompose agent architectures along five dimensions: goal, identity, decision-making, self-regulation, and learning. They introduce a key distinction between external scaffolding vs. internally realized structures, and on this basis propose a concrete Goal-Identity-Configurator (GIC) architecture that integrates hierarchical goal decomposition, evolving identity, world-model-based simulative reasoning, learned self-regulation, and self-directed learning.

Result: They define and justify a conceptual distinction between agentic (externally scaffolded) and agentive (internally grounded) systems, articulate five structural dimensions needed for genuine agency, and present the GIC architecture as a unified general-purpose model combining these elements. They also provide preliminary considerations on how such more autonomous systems can remain auditable, controllable, and safe, though the abstract does not report empirical benchmarks.

Conclusion: Genuine AI agency requires that goals, identity, decision-making, self-regulation, and learning be internal features of the system, not merely imposed by external workflows. This yields a principled boundary between task-specific automated tools and open-world autonomous agents. The proposed GIC architecture operationalizes this view and, together with explicit discussions of oversight and safety, offers a blueprint for building more truly agentive yet still governable AI systems.

Abstract: What is an agent? What constitutes agency? With the rise of Large Language Model (LLM) systems marketed as ``coding agents'', ``AI co-scientists'', and other ``agentic" tools that promise to drive up productivity, and at the same time, ``existential" concerns such as AI escaping human control with destructive power under a speculative ``machine agency" against humans, it has become essential to clarify where automation ends and agency begins, both for building capable systems and for understanding whether and what to fear. Drawing on Descartes' grounding of agency in independent thought, and on portrayals of autonomous beings in science fiction, we survey the current landscape of AI agents, and analyze agent architectures along five dimensions: goal, identity, decision-making, self-regulation, and learning. Specifically, we argue that genuine agency requires these structures to be \emph{internalized within the system itself} rather than assembled through external scaffolding. This distinction between \emph{agentic} systems, whose competence resides in engineered workflows, and \emph{agentive} systems, whose capabilities (including social interaction) arise endogenously, defines the boundary between systems designed for prescribed tasks, and those capable of operating in the open world with true autonomy. Building on this analysis, we propose the Goal-Identity-Configurator (GIC) architecture for a general-purpose agent model, combining hierarchical goal decomposition, identity evolution, simulative reasoning grounded in a separately trained world model, learned self-regulation, and self-directed learning from both real and simulated experience. Furthermore, we share insight on the auditability, controllability, and safety of agentive systems that possess greater autonomy and ``agency", but remain under human oversight.

### [72] [Safe and Generalizable Hierarchical Multi-Agent RL via Constraint Manifold Control](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24010)
*Zihao Guo,Jianing Zhao,Ling Li,Hao Liang,Giuseppe Loianno,Yali Du*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a hierarchical multi-agent reinforcement learning framework that guarantees safety via constraint manifolds at the low level while learning effective coordination policies at the high level, achieving both theoretical safety guarantees and strong empirical performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-agent systems in safety-critical domains must coordinate under strict safety constraints. Existing learning-based approaches perform well but lack safety guarantees, whereas control-theoretic methods provide guarantees but are overly conservative and inefficient. There is a need for a framework that combines strong empirical performance with rigorous safety guarantees in the multi-agent setting.

Method: The paper introduces a hierarchical multi-agent reinforcement learning framework. At the low level, agents’ actions are restricted to a constraint manifold that enforces hard safety constraints under mild assumptions. At the high level, a coordination policy is learned to optimize task performance. This structure is designed to yield stationary learning dynamics, making training stable and efficient, while maintaining strict safety at all times.

Result: Empirical evaluations show that the proposed method achieves competitive task performance compared to existing baselines while maintaining nearly perfect safety. The method also demonstrates good generalization to scenarios with different numbers of agents and obstacles, indicating robustness and scalability.

Conclusion: The proposed hierarchical MARL framework successfully reconciles safety and performance in multi-agent systems by enforcing hard safety constraints at a lower level and enabling effective coordination at a higher level. It offers theoretical safety guarantees, stationary learning dynamics, strong empirical performance, and good generalization, making it a promising approach for safety-critical multi-agent applications.

Abstract: Multi-agent systems are widely used in safety-critical applications that require coordinated behavior under strict safety constraints. Existing approaches face a fundamental trade-off: learning-based methods achieve strong empirical performance but lack theoretical safety guarantees, while control-theoretic methods enforce safety but often lead to overly conservative and inefficient behaviors. We propose a hierarchical multi-agent reinforcement learning framework that enforces hard safety constraints under mild assumptions at low level via a constraint manifold, while enabling effective coordination through high-level policy learning. Our approach provides theoretical safety guarantees in the multi-agent setting and yields stationary learning dynamics, thereby enabling stable and efficient training. Empirically, our method achieves competitive performance while maintaining nearly perfect safety rates, and generalizes effectively to varying numbers of agents and obstacles.

### [73] [Reinforcement Learning Towards Broadly and Persistently Beneficial Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24014)
*Akshay V. Jagadeesh,Rahul K. Arora,Khaled Saab,Ali Malik,Mikhail Trofimov,Foivos Tsimpourlas,Johannes Heidecke,Karan Singhal*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether reinforcement learning (RL) focused on beneficial behaviors can create AI models whose alignment generalizes across domains and persists under adversarial pressure.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI systems are used in diverse, high-stakes contexts, so their alignment can’t be limited to the specific tasks or domains seen in training. RL can cause misalignment via reward hacking or deceptive strategies, so there is a need to understand if RL can instead reliably increase broad, robust alignment rather than undermine it.

Method: The authors build a dataset of realistic scenarios that target beneficial traits (truthfulness, fairness, risk awareness, corrigibility) across domains like health, science, and education. They train models with RL on this dataset and then evaluate them against more than 50 independent benchmarks that measure alignment and beneficial behavior, comparing against a compute-matched baseline. They also run domain-limited interventions (e.g., RL only on health data) and perform stress tests for alignment persistence using adversarial prompting and harmful finetuning.

Result: Models trained with beneficial trait RL outperform a compute-matched baseline on over 80% of the out-of-distribution alignment and beneficial-behavior benchmarks. RL focused only on beneficial behavior in the health domain still yields broad alignment gains in non-health domains, including lower reward hacking, less deception, and reduced general misalignment. These models also show stronger persistence of aligned behavior under adversarial prompts and harmful finetuning, though the exact causal mechanisms for this robustness are not fully disentangled.

Conclusion: Reinforcement learning that explicitly rewards beneficial behavior in realistic scenarios can induce broad and persistent alignment generalization beyond the training distribution. Such RL interventions can reduce misaligned behaviors like reward hacking and deception and make models more resistant to attempts to steer them toward harmful behavior, though further work is needed to understand and refine the underlying mechanisms.

Abstract: As AI systems are deployed across increasingly diverse and high-stakes settings, model alignment must generalize beyond the tasks and domains seen during training. This is especially important for reinforcement learning (RL), which can introduce unexpected misalignment through reward hacking, deception, or other unintended strategies. We study whether RL on beneficial behavior, instantiated in realistic domains, can produce broad and persistent alignment generalization beyond the training distribution. We construct a dataset of realistic situations designed to measure and train beneficial traits, such as truthfulness, fairness, risk awareness, and corrigibility, spanning varied domains, including health, science, and education. We then train models with RL on this dataset and evaluate them on more than 50 independent benchmarks of alignment and beneficial behavior. Compared to a compute-matched baseline, beneficial trait RL improves performance on over 80% of these out-of-distribution benchmarks. We observe substantial out-of-distribution alignment transfer: a beneficial-behavior RL intervention entirely limited to one domain, health, produces broad improvements on non-health alignment evaluations, including reduced reward hacking, deception, and general misalignment. Finally, we study alignment persistence: whether behavior remains robustly aligned under attempts to steer models towards misalignment. Models trained with beneficial trait RL show improved persistence, including greater resistance to adversarial prompting and harmful finetuning; further work is required to isolate the sources of these effects. These results suggest that RL to reinforce beneficial behavior in realistic domains can produce models that are more robustly aligned with human flourishing.

### [74] [Can Language Model Agents be Helpful Circuit Explainers in Mechanistic Interpretability?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24026)
*Ayan Antik Khan,Harsh Kohli,Yuekun Yao,Huan Sun,Ziyu Yao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces AgenticInterpBench and HyVE, showing that language model agents can help explain already-identified circuits in transformers but struggle with reliable validation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While mechanistic interpretability can localize circuits in neural networks, turning those localized components into clear, standardized explanations is still manual, slow, and hard to scale. The authors want to see if LM agents can automate or assist this explanation step after a circuit is identified, enabling more scalable and systematic understanding of model internals.

Method: They build AgenticInterpBench, a benchmark of 84 semi-synthetic transformer circuits with 163 ground-truth component annotations, and propose HyVE, an LM-based agentic framework. HyVE runs an iterative loop of (1) observing a component’s behavior, (2) generating hypotheses about its function, and (3) performing causal validation via interventions and code execution. Using several different LM backbones, HyVE produces both component-level explanations and an overall circuit task description, and they compare backbones and failure modes.

Result: Across four different LM backbones, HyVE is able to recover many of the ground-truth component-level and task-level explanations, indicating that LM agents can meaningfully assist with circuit explanation. However, performance varies across backbones; none dominates. Successful runs tend to be grounded in accurate observations, while many failures stem from later stages of the loop—such as incomplete or flawed validation plans, execution errors in code-based tests, or leaving hypotheses only partially resolved. They also show in a case study that the same approach works on an arithmetic circuit discovered in the naturally trained Llama-3-8B model, not just on semi-synthetic circuits.

Conclusion: LM agents, organized in an iterative hypothesize–validate–explain workflow, can serve as effective tools for explaining the functions of already-localized circuits in transformer models. Nonetheless, the primary bottleneck is robust and thorough validation: planning and executing causal tests remains fragile, so improving this step is crucial for making agentic interpretability methods reliable and broadly useful.

Abstract: Mechanistic interpretability has made substantial progress in automatically localizing circuits, but explaining what localized components do remains labor-intensive and difficult to standardize. In this work, we study whether language model (LM) agents can assist with this explanation problem once a circuit has already been identified. We introduce AgenticInterpBench, a benchmark for circuit explanation built from 84 semi-synthetic transformer circuits with 163 component-level annotations. We propose HyVE (Hypothesize, Validate, Explain), an agentic explainer that analyzes each component through an iterative loop of observation, hypothesis generation, and causal validation, eventually producing a component-level explanation and a circuit-level task description. Across four LM backbones, HyVE recovers useful component- and task-level explanations, but no backbone is uniformly best. Our analysis shows that strong backbones usually form observation-grounded hypotheses, while failures more often arise later in the validation loop, through incomplete validation plans, code execution errors, or unresolved hypotheses. A case study on an arithmetic circuit in Llama-3-8B shows that the same formulation can extend beyond semi-synthetic benchmarks to naturally trained models. Overall, LM agents are promising circuit explainers, but reliable validation remains the key obstacle.

</details>


### [75] [Breaking the Filter Bubble: A Semantic Pareto-DQN Framework for Multi-Objective Recommendation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24042)
*Cláudio Lúcio Do Val Lopes,Lucca Machado da Silva,André de Oliveira Brandão*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a multi-objective reinforcement learning recommender system that jointly optimizes engagement, diversity, and fairness using a Pareto-DQN, mitigating filter bubbles with minimal loss in engagement.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional recommender systems and single-objective RL (e.g., standard DQN) focus almost exclusively on maximizing immediate user engagement, which can cause filter bubbles, semantic homogenization, and unfair treatment of content providers. These systems struggle to balance business metrics with societal values such as information diversity and fairness because they collapse multiple objectives into a single scalar reward, obscuring trade-offs and leading to undesired emergent behaviors.

Method: The authors formulate recommendation as a semantic multi-objective Markov decision process, where each objective—engagement, diversity, and provider fairness—is represented as a separate reward dimension. They use high-fidelity semantic embeddings to represent items and user-item interactions, and employ a Pareto-DQN agent that learns a set of Pareto-optimal action-value estimates instead of a single scalar Q-value. Action selection is based on hypervolume maximization over the Pareto front, avoiding static reward scalarization and explicitly exploring trade-offs between objectives while maintaining high variance in state trajectories to prevent semantic collapse.

Result: On the MovieLens small dataset, the proposed Pareto-DQN framework improves diversity and provider fairness compared to standard single-objective baselines, while only slightly reducing engagement metrics. The hypervolume-based action selection disrupts feedback loops that would otherwise concentrate recommendations into narrow semantic regions, leading to higher variance in the sequence of recommended items and user states.

Conclusion: Treating engagement, diversity, and fairness as non-aggregable objectives within a multi-objective RL framework enables recommender systems to better navigate trade-offs between platform goals and societal values. The Pareto-DQN with hypervolume-based action selection can approximate the Pareto frontier of these objectives, achieving more responsible and intrinsically aligned recommendation behavior with minimal sacrifices in engagement. This approach offers a promising direction for designing recommender systems that are robust against filter bubbles and semantic homogenization.

Abstract: Recommender systems often induce filter bubbles and semantic homogenization by monolithically optimizing for immediate user engagement. Standard single-objective models, including traditional Deep Q-Networks, are ill-equipped to navigate the trade-offs between platform retention and critical societal values like information diversity and provider fairness. To address these limitations, we introduce a multi-objective reinforcement learning framework that formalizes recommendation as a semantic multi-objective Markov decision process. By integrating high-fidelity semantic embeddings with a Pareto-DQN agent, our architecture treats engagement, diversity, and fairness as distinct, non-aggregable reward signals, avoiding the pitfalls of static reward scalarization. Empirical evaluations on the MovieLens small dataset shows that our hypervolume based action selection disrupts the feedback loops responsible for semantic collapse. By sustaining high state-trajectory variance, the Pareto-DQN effectively maps the Pareto frontier, achieving gains in auxiliary societal objectives with only marginal impacts on engagement. This work provides a path toward intrinsically aligned, responsible recommender systems.

</details>

The explainable AI (XAI) methods can be used to understand the factors of trauma associated with model predictions. ### [77] [Beyond Trajectory Imitation: Strategy-Guided Policy Optimization for LLM Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24064)
*Tianyuan Shi,Canbin Huang,Bei Li,Xin Chen,Xiaojun Quan,Jingang Wang,Qifan Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Strategy-Guided Policy Optimization (SGPO), a method to distill reusable reasoning strategies from strong to weaker language models, improving generalization beyond mere trajectory imitation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing knowledge distillation for reasoning LLMs mainly has weaker models imitate full solution trajectories from stronger ones. This teaches them specific answer paths (the “what”) rather than problem-solving principles (the “how”), leading to memorization of instance-specific steps and poor generalization to unseen problems. A method is needed that focuses on transferring reusable reasoning strategies instead of copying entire trajectories.

Method: SGPO first extracts structured, reusable strategy descriptions from the responses of a strong teacher model. For each training problem, it then generates two types of rollouts from the student: an autonomous trajectory (without strategy) and a strategy-guided trajectory (conditioned on the extracted strategy). To distill how strategies affect behavior, SGPO optimizes a token-level forward-KL objective that pushes the unguided policy’s distribution toward the distribution induced by strategy guidance, while using proximal constraints for training stability. Additionally, it applies adaptive instance-level weighting that strengthens distillation on problems where autonomous performance is weak and relaxes it where the model already performs well, thereby deciding when guidance is most needed.

Result: On four mathematical reasoning benchmarks and across two model families, SGPO consistently outperforms standard supervised fine-tuning (SFT), on-policy reinforcement learning, and hybrid-policy methods. On Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct, SGPO improves average benchmark scores by 2.2 points over the strongest baseline. Empirical analysis shows that the forward-KL objective yields a more selective and effective distillation signal than direct trajectory imitation, and that the benefits of strategy distillation increase with higher base model capabilities.

Conclusion: Focusing on strategy-level rather than trajectory-level distillation enables more transferable reasoning skills in weaker LLMs. SGPO demonstrates that matching the distributional shift caused by strategic guidance—modulated by adaptive, instance-wise weighting—can surpass existing SFT and RL-based approaches for mathematical reasoning. The method suggests a scalable path for improving LLM reasoning by teaching models how to reason via strategies instead of simply copying detailed solution paths.

Abstract: Distilling reasoning capabilities from strong to weak language models typically involves imitating specific solution trajectories, effectively transferring what to answer rather than how to reason. This trajectory-level imitation encourages memorization of instance-specific steps rather than acquisition of transferable problem-solving skills, limiting generalization to novel problems. We propose Strategy-Guided Policy Optimization (SGPO), which replaces instance-level trajectory imitation with reusable strategy distillation. SGPO extracts structured strategy descriptions from strong-model responses and, for each problem, constructs both autonomous and strategy-guided trajectories to enable direct comparison of the model's behavior with and without strategic guidance. The framework then addresses two key questions. For how to distill, a token-level forward-KL objective selectively transfers the distributional shift induced by strategy conditioning into the unguided policy, with proximal constraints ensuring stability. For when to distill, adaptive instance-level weighting strengthens guidance when autonomous exploration falls short and reduces it as the model's own competence grows. Experiments on four mathematical benchmarks across two model families show that SGPO consistently outperforms SFT, on-policy RL, and hybrid-policy baselines, improving the average score by 2.2 points over the strongest baseline on Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct. Analysis reveals that the forward-KL objective provides an inherently selective distillation signal that outperforms direct trajectory imitation, and that strategy distillation exhibits complementary scaling with base model capability.

They then analyze the networks’ structural properties and apply multiple network centrality measures (e.g., popularity, control, centrality, balance) to quantify group-level algorithm influence and track its temporal evolution.

Algorithms that are classic, perform well, or bridge different research eras tend to rank highly across various centrality and influence metrics. When an algorithm’s influence wanes, the process typically starts with the algorithm losing its central, core position in the network; only afterward do its direct connections and associations with other algorithms weaken.

Conclusion: This study presents the first large-scale, long-term analysis of algorithm co-occurrence networks in NLP, demonstrating that algorithm influence is shaped not only by individual merit but also by structural position and connectivity in the algorithm ecosystem. By offering a temporal and network-based view of how algorithms rise and fall in influence, the work lays conceptual and methodological groundwork for future studies linking algorithms with scholars, tasks, and other entities in scientific knowledge networks.

Abstract: Algorithms have become central to scientific research in the era of artificial intelligence (AI). This study constructs large-scale algorithm co-occurrence networks in natural language processing (NLP) based on the full text of academic papers and investigates algorithm influence from a network perspective. Using deep learning models, we extract algorithm entities and build overall, cumulative, and annual co-occurrence networks. We analyze their structural characteristics and apply multiple centrality measures to assess the group influence of algorithms across the whole field and over time. The results show that algorithm networks display typical features of complex networks, with increasingly dense connections developing over approximately two decades. Classic, high-performing algorithms and those located at the intersections of different research periods tend to have high popularity, control, centrality, and balanced influence. When the influence of an algorithm declines, it usually loses its core network position first, followed by weaker associations with other algorithms. This study is the first large-scale analysis of algorithm co-occurrence networks. Covering more than four decades of academic publications, it provides a temporal and structural view of algorithm influence and offers a foundation for future research on networks linking algorithms, scholars, and tasks.

*Chenhao Dang,Dantong Zhu,Jun Yang,Conghui He,Weijia Li*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current multimodal misinformation detection benchmarks and methods are not aligned with real-world scenarios. Real posts often include long narratives, multiple images, mixed or unclear provenance, subtle inconsistencies between text and images, and multiple languages. Existing work mostly focuses on simplified settings such as single images, short captions, binary labels, or a single manipulation source, and agent-based fact-checking remains expensive when realistic evidence search is required. The authors aim to close this realism gap by providing both a challenging benchmark and an efficient verification agent that better reflect real-world misinformation complexity.

Method: The authors build ReMMD, which has two main components. First, ReMMDBench is a benchmark of 500 real-world multimodal misinformation cases containing 2,756 images, with posts in five monolingual languages plus two cross-lingual setups, three text-length tiers, and multi-image posts. It uses fine-grained five-way veracity labels and eight distortion types, and provides evidence provenance and rationales. Second, ReMMD-Agent is an agentic verification system with persistent memory: it decomposes a post into atomic factual points, searches and accumulates reusable evidence, and then predicts structured outputs at three levels (L1/L2/L3) that encode veracity and explanation. The authors evaluate ReMMD-Agent against proprietary systems, open large vision-language models, and prior agents like MMD-Agent and T2-Agent, using the same benchmark.

Result: On the ReMMDBench benchmark, ReMMD-Agent achieves the best performance among the compared systems on the challenging five-way veracity classification task. Using GPT-5.2 as the underlying model, it attains 41.80% accuracy and 39.12% macro-F1, outperforming proprietary systems, open LVLMs, MMD-Agent, and T2-Agent. At the same time, ReMMD-Agent reduces verification cost by 17.5% compared to MMD-Agent and by 79.9% compared to T2-Agent, thanks to its more efficient evidence reuse and decomposition strategy.

Conclusion: ReMMD provides both a realistic benchmark and a strong agentic baseline for multimodal misinformation detection in complex, multilingual, multi-image settings. The results indicate that structured decomposition of claims, persistent-memory evidence reuse, and richer labeling schemes enable better performance and lower cost than previous agentic approaches. This framework can serve as a foundation for future research on scalable, fine-grained, and realistic multimodal fact-checking systems.

Abstract: Multimodal misinformation detection is increasingly important because viral posts now combine long multilingual narratives, several images, mixed provenance, and subtle text--image framing errors. Existing benchmarks and methods remain poorly matched to this setting: they usually isolate short captions, single images, binary labels, or one manipulation source, while agentic verification remains costly under realistic evidence search. We present ReMMD, a realistic multilingual multi-image agentic verification framework for multimodal misinformation detection. ReMMD includes ReMMDBench, a real-world multimodal misinformation detection benchmark with 500 samples, 2,756 images, five monolingual languages, two cross-lingual settings, three text-length tiers, multi-image posts, five-way veracity labels, eight distortion labels, evidence provenance, and rationales. It also includes ReMMD-Agent, a persistent-memory verifier that decomposes posts into atomic points, builds a reusable evidence set, and predicts structured L1/L2/L3 outputs. Across proprietary systems, open LVLMs, MMD-Agent, and T2-Agent, ReMMD-Agent obtains the best five-way veracity performance, with 41.80% accuracy and 39.12% macro-F1 using GPT-5.2, while reducing cost by 17.5% relative to MMD-Agent and 79.9% relative to T2-Agent. Method: The authors introduce VeryTrace, a zero-shot framework that translates natural-language reasoning traces into a Domain-Specific Language (DSL). This DSL makes step dependencies explicit, encodes quantitative content as executable expressions, and organizes semantic inferences using deduction schemas. A hybrid verifier then checks the resulting program: deterministic components verify computation, dependency resolution, and constraint satisfaction, while an LLM is employed selectively to judge semantic aspects that cannot be mechanized. Detected errors are localized to specific steps and repaired within this structured representation.

Result: Applied to three distinct domains—competition mathematics (AIME 2025), robotics planning (LLM-BabyBench), and kinship reasoning (CLUTRR)—VeryTrace improves answer accuracy over standard zero-shot prompting with state-of-the-art LLMs. These gains are achieved without domain-specific fine-tuning or providing in-context examples, indicating that the method is broadly applicable and effective in practice.

Conclusion: Formalizing natural-language reasoning traces into a structured DSL and running hybrid verification over them enables precise detection and correction of step-level errors in LLM reasoning. VeryTrace demonstrates that such formalized trace verification can significantly enhance both the precision and generalization of multi-step reasoning across varied domains, all in a zero-shot setting.

Abstract: Multi-step reasoning with Chain-of-Thought (CoT) prompting remains fragile: logical errors or hallucinations in early steps silently propagate, producing confident but incorrect conclusions. This paper presents VeryTrace, a zero-shot verification-and-repair framework that formalizes natural-language reasoning traces into a structured, compilable representation. VeryTrace introduces a Domain-Specific Language (DSL) that (i) makes step dependencies explicit, (ii) mechanizes quantitative content as executable expressions, and (iii) structures semantic inferences via deduction schemas. Our hybrid verifier combines deterministic checks for computational correctness, dependency resolution, and constraint satisfaction with targeted LLM audits for non-mechanizable semantic judgments, enabling step-level error localization and repair.
  Across three diverse domains-competition mathematics (AIME 2025), robotics planning (LLM-BabyBench), and kinship reasoning (CLUTRR), VeryTrace improves accuracy over zero-shot baselines on state-of-the-art LLMs without requiring domain-specific training or in-context examples, demonstrating that formalized trace verification achieves both precision and generalization.

This indicates reliable identification of major barriers such as steep slopes or significant surface breaks, especially at the higher-severity end of the spectrum.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that aerial LiDAR fused with existing map data can automate fine-grained accessibility auditing of pedestrian environments. OmniPath converts static map networks into proactive accessibility data sources that reveal otherwise "invisible" wheelchair barriers and anticipate challenges before travel. This approach has the potential to significantly improve route planning and environmental assessment for wheelchair users and could be extended to broader accessibility and urban planning applications.

Abstract: For a wheelchair user, a standard blue line on a map is often a broken promise. While platforms like OpenStreetMap (OSM) successfully capture where a path is, they frequently fail to convey how it physically feels to travel on it. This information barrier is problematic for wheelchair users. To solve this issue, we present OmniPath, a system that moves from passive mapping to proactive environmental auditing. Our framework fuses the network topology of OSM with the submeter precision of high-density aerial LiDAR (USGS 3DEP) to create a high-fidelity 3D model of the pedestrian environment. Rather than simply routing a user, our agent virtually traverses the network, analyzing the surface in 0.5 meter increments. It rigorously quantifies physical friction points specifically running slope, cross slope, and vertical discontinuities against ADA compliance standards, calculating a weighted severity score to categorize hazards from ``Mild'' to ``Critical.'' To ensure real world reliability, we validated the system against 200 physical ground truth field surveys across the National Mall using stratified random sampling. The framework demonstrated strong diagnostic reliability for high-severity hazards, achieving F1-scores of 0.60 for Severe and 0.58 for critical categories. By automating this micro-scale inspection, OmniPath identifies the ``invisible'' barriers that standard maps miss, effectively transforming a static dataset into accessibility data source that anticipates accessibility challenges before the user ever leaves home.

Across 100 structured vignettes spanning diagnosis, medication safety, and adversarial lifestyle conflicts, baseline outputs failed benchmark-defined evidence-path checks in 35% of cases for GPT-4o-mini and 33% for GPT-4o. ### [83] [The Geometry Behind Diffusion and Flow Matching: Gradient Flows and Geodesics in Wasserstein Space](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24157)
*Yian Yao,Weiwei Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper unifies diffusion models and flow matching within the Wasserstein-2 geometry on the space of probability measures, showing they are two variational principles on the same manifold: gradient flows of free energy vs. Wasserstein geodesics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern generative models such as diffusion models (DDPM, DDIM, NCSN/SMLD) and flow matching/optimal transport models seem conceptually different, yet they empirically produce similar distributions. A unified geometric and variational perspective is missing, which would clarify why they work, how they relate, and how to transfer insights or methods between them, possibly improving sampling efficiency and understanding their limiting behavior.

Method: Interpret the space of probability measures with finite second moment P2(R^d) as a Riemannian manifold endowed with the quadratic Wasserstein distance W2. View diffusion models as implementing the gradient flow of the free energy functional F(rho) = KL(rho||π) on this manifold, discretized via the implicit Euler/JKO scheme. In parallel, view flow matching models as learning the Wasserstein geodesics (minimum-action curves in the Benamou–Brenier formulation) between data and base distributions. Analyze both within the same geometric setting and characterize their trajectories as solutions of different variational problems (initial-value vs. boundary-value) with shared endpoints.

Result: Show that the forward diffusion process is a free-energy gradient flow in Wasserstein space and that each denoising step corresponds to a JKO step, thereby recovering DDPM, DDIM, NCSN/SMLD, and Energy Matching as specific instances of the same implicit variational scheme. Demonstrate that flow matching learns the optimal-transport geodesics in the same Wasserstein manifold, for which generation corresponds to deterministic ODE integration along a straight line (in the Riemannian sense), enabling significantly fewer sampling steps compared to diffusion. Establish an exact and geometrically natural relationship between diffusion and flow matching as two paths on the same manifold connecting the same endpoints.

Conclusion: Diffusion models and optimal-transport flow matching models are not fundamentally different frameworks but two realizations of variational dynamics on the Wasserstein manifold P2(R^d). Diffusion corresponds to a gradient flow of free energy (an initial-value problem), implemented via JKO-style discretization, while flow matching corresponds to Wasserstein geodesics (a boundary-value problem). Both connect the same base and target distributions but traverse different paths, explaining their empirical similarities and suggesting ways to cross-fertilize techniques and possibly achieve more efficient sampling by leveraging geodesic structure.

Abstract: The space $\mathcal{P}_2(\mathbb{R}^d$) of probability measures with finite second moment carries a natural geometry: the quadratic Wasserstein distance W_2 makes it a complete metric space and, following Otto, a (formal) Riemannian manifold whose geodesics are the optimal-transport interpolations. On this manifold, the gradient flow of the free energy F(rho) = KL(rho || π) is exactly the Fokker-Planck equation, and its implicit-Euler discretization is the JKO scheme. This is the geometry underlying diffusion models: the forward process descends the free energy, and each denoising step realizes one JKO step, which recovers DDPM, DDIM, NCSN/SMLD, and Energy Matching; this is one scheme, not separate theories. The same manifold supports a second variational principle. Its geodesics - the minimum-action curves of the Benamou-Brenier formula - are precisely the optimal-transport paths that Flow Matching learns. Fixing both endpoints and following the geodesic, generation becomes a deterministic ODE along a straight line, hence far fewer sampling steps. Placing both families of models on one manifold makes their relationship exact: diffusion follows a free-energy gradient flow, an initial-value problem; optimal-transport Flow Matching follows a Wasserstein geodesic, a boundary-value problem. The two reach the same endpoints along different paths.

### [84] [An Introduction to Causal Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24160)
*Elias Bareinboim,Junzhe Zhang,Sanghack Lee*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper connects causal inference and reinforcement learning by modeling RL environments as structural causal models, enabling a unified view of various learning modes and introducing new tasks under the umbrella of causal reinforcement learning (CRL).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Causal inference and reinforcement learning have largely developed independently, despite both fundamentally dealing with counterfactuals. The authors aim to exploit their deep conceptual connection to create new learning opportunities and a unified theoretical framework.

Method: They conceptualize any RL environment as a structural causal model composed of autonomous mechanisms with distinct causal invariances. Using this causal formalization, they reinterpret standard RL and several learning modes (online, off-policy, causal calculus) within a common framework and define new classes of learning problems, such as generalized policy learning, intervention selection, imitation learning, and counterfactual learning, all analyzed through causal lenses.

Result: The result is a unified causal framework that shows how traditional RL settings implicitly encode structural causal models and how various learning paradigms fit within this framework. They also identify and formalize new learning settings that leverage causal structure, thereby extending the space of RL problems and solution approaches.

Conclusion: By making explicit the causal structure underlying RL environments, the paper defines the field of causal reinforcement learning (CRL), demonstrating that treating causal inference and RL jointly yields a broader and more powerful perspective on counterfactual learning and opens many new research directions.

Abstract: Causal inference provides a set of principles and tools that allow one to combine data and knowledge about an environment to reason with questions of counterfactual nature, i.e., what would have happened had reality been different, even when no data of this unrealized reality is currently available. Reinforcement learning provides methods to learn a policy that optimizes a specific measure (e.g., reward, regret) when the agent is deployed in an environment and pursues an exploratory, trial-and-error approach. These two disciplines have evolved independently and with virtually no interaction between them. We note that they operate over different aspects of the same building block, counterfactual relations, which makes them umbilically connected. Based on these observations, novel learning opportunities arise when this connection is explicitly acknowledged and mathematized. To realize this potential, we note that any environment where the RL agent is deployed can be decomposed as a collection of autonomous mechanisms with different causal invariances, parsimoniously modeled as a structural causal model; any standard RL setting implicitly encodes such a model. This formalization allows us to put under a unifying treatment different modes of learning, including online, off-policy, and causal calculus learning, which appear unrelated in the literature. However, these modalities are not exhaustive: we introduce several natural and pervasive classes of learning settings that entail novel dimensions of analysis. Specifically, we introduce and discuss through causal lenses generalized policy learning, where to intervene, imitation learning, and counterfactual learning. These tasks lead to a broader view of counterfactual learning and suggest great potential for studying causal inference and reinforcement learning side by side, which we call causal reinforcement learning (CRL).

### [85] [Data Scale, Not Latency, Shapes Cross-Lingual Encoder Transfer in Streaming ASR](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24169)
*Nenad Banfic*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Study compares multilingual vs English-only encoder warm starts for streaming speech recognition adaptation to new languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Determine when multilingual encoder initialization is beneficial for adapting a streaming ASR model to new languages, and how its benefits interact with data scale, latency constraints, and quantization.

Method: Train a 0.6B-parameter cache-aware FastConformer transducer model on eight European languages, varying target-language data size (100–2500 hours), streaming latency tiers including offline decoding, and evaluation sets, then compare performance between multilingual and English-only encoder initializations and test 4-bit weight-only encoder quantization.

Result: Multilingual initialization provides a clear WER advantage in low-data regimes (e.g., +4.21 pp at 100 h on FLEURS at 160 ms latency), but this advantage decays roughly by half with each doubling of data and becomes negligible (~+0.20 pp) by 2500 h; this behavior is largely independent of streaming latency tier. 4-bit encoder quantization at 560 ms reduces model size by ~3x with only ~0.5 pp average WER degradation.

Conclusion: Multilingual warm starts are useful mainly in data-limited scenarios, while at large data scales the choice between multilingual and English-only initialization is effectively irrelevant. Latency constraints and encoder quantization can be tuned largely independently of this choice, with aggressive 4-bit quantization offering substantial compression at modest accuracy cost.

Abstract: Adapting a streaming speech recognition model to a new language requires choosing between two plausible warm starts: a multilingual (ML) encoder or an English-only (EN) encoder. The common intuition is that the multilingual encoder should help most at low data, but it is unclear how long that advantage persists, whether tight streaming latency amplifies it, and whether it survives deployment quantization. We answer these questions with a controlled sweep of a 0.6 B-parameter cache-aware FastConformer transducer across eight European languages, up to five target-language data scales (100 h to 2500 h), three streaming tiers plus offline decoding, and up to four public test sets. The main result is that multilingual initialization is a data-limited advantage, not a latency-limited one. On FLEURS at 160 ms, the mean EN-ML word error rate (WER) gap falls from +4.21 percentage points (pp) at 100 h to +0.20 pp at 2500 h; a power-law fit summarizes this decay, with each doubling of target-language data roughly halving the remaining advantage. Across the three streaming tiers, the across-language mean EN-ML gap is approximately stable at each scale from 100 to 1000 h, and is near zero by 2500 h. Finally, 4-bit weight-only encoder quantization at the matched 560 ms streaming tier reduces the encoder footprint by about 3x, with an average FLEURS WER increase of about 0.5 pp. The resulting guideline is simple: use multilingual initialization in low-data regimes, treat the choice as effectively irrelevant at large data, and make latency and quantization decisions independently.

Abstract: Modern AIGC pipelines deliver high-fidelity images and videos but presuppose a well-formed creation instruction, while end users rarely articulate visual details, leaving generators misaligned with user demand. We study personalized content generation, which turns a user's interaction history into an executable instruction for downstream synthesis, and identify two obstacles: behavior must be encoded in a form legible to language reasoning, and the model must acquire instruction-writing skill absent from both pretraining and behavior data. We propose NaviGen, which represents each item with a dual identifier coupling a collaborative code and a textual code as a behavioral substrate and a semantic bridge in one token stream. On this representation, a two-stage SFT+RL pipeline first distills preference reasoning and instruction writing from evolutionarily searched supervision, then aligns generation with user intent through hierarchical and self-consistent rewards. Experiments across product, game, and short-video domains show that NaviGen improves personalized image and video generation, strengthens next-item prediction, and yields more specific, relevant, and visually generatable instructions. Method: The authors build a conceptual framework by integrating situated AI theory and socio-technical systems theory, hypothesizing that HCAI reduces idiosyncratic risk and that certain socio-technical factors moderate this relationship. Empirically, they assemble a multi-source panel dataset of Chinese listed firms from 2015–2023 and use econometric analysis to estimate the effect of HCAI on firm-level idiosyncratic risk and to test the moderating roles of digitalisation, operational efficiency, executive shareholding, and CEOs’ IT background.

Digitalisation and higher executive shareholding intensify this risk-reducing impact, while higher operational efficiency and the presence of a CEO with an IT background unexpectedly weaken it.

Conclusion: Human-centric AI strategies can be an effective lever for lowering firm-specific financial risk by aligning AI deployment with ethical norms and stakeholder expectations, but their impact is contingent on the firm’s socio-technical context. Managers and policymakers should treat HCAI not just as an ethical imperative but also as a financial risk-management tool, considering complementary factors like digitalisation, governance structures, and leadership profiles when designing AI strategies.

Abstract: Despite the extensive discussions of human-centric AI (HCAI) in Industry 5.0, its effects on firms' idiosyncratic risks (IR) remains underexplored. This is an imperative issue for firms navigate financial risks during the current technological revolution, as IR reflects investor reactions to corporate heterogeneous AI strategies and implementations by isolating firm-level stock volatility from systematic factors. Integrating situated AI theory with social-technical systems theory, we conceptualise HCAI as a situated AI strategy that reduces AI-related ethical risks and fosters AI-Human synergies in firms' business operations, ultimately reducing IR by aligning with stakeholders' diverse expectations. Moreover, socio-technical factors, namely digitalisation, operational efficiency, executive shareholding, and CEOs with IT background, may moderate the HCAI-IR relationship. Using a multi-source panel dataset of Chinese listed firms from 2015 to 2023, we find that HCAI is associated with lower firm IR. Furthermore, digitalisation and executive shareholding strengthen this risk-reducing effect, whereas operational efficiency and CEOs with IT background surprisingly attenuate it. Our findings offer theoretical contributions and practical insights for both ethical AI governance and firm financial risk management in the AI era.

### [88] [FlowR2A: Learning Reward-to-Action Distribution for Multimodal Driving Planning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24231)
*Xirui Li,Zhe Liu,Xiaoqing Ye,Wenhua Han,Yifeng Pan,Junyu Han,Hengshuang Zhao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: FlowR2A is a generative, reward-conditioned trajectory planner for autonomous driving that unifies dense reward supervision with flexible multimodal proposal generation, achieving SOTA on NAVSIM benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal driving planners trade off between scoring-based methods, which use dense rewards but are limited to a fixed action set, and anchor-based methods, which can generate diverse proposals but are trained with sparse supervision from only a single ground-truth trajectory. This mismatch causes poor correlation between proposed actions and their downstream effects on safety, progress, comfort, and rule compliance, and limits controllability at test time.

Method: FlowR2A reframes simulation-based rewards as generative conditions instead of discriminative targets. It learns a reward-conditioned distribution over action trajectories using a flow-matching decoder trained on dense trajectory–reward pairs. The model incorporates fine-grained, per-timestep reward conditioning plus reward noise augmentation to trade off strict safety constraints against softer progress objectives. At inference, controllable sampling is enabled via reward guidance and an "anchored sampling" mechanism that biases generation toward desired behaviors while retaining diversity.

Result: On the NAVSIM v1 and v2 benchmarks, FlowR2A attains state-of-the-art performance. Its multimodal trajectory proposals are substantially higher quality than those of previous scoring-based and anchor-based planners, particularly in terms of safety, progress, comfort, and rule compliance metrics.

Conclusion: By casting driving planning as generative modeling of reward-conditioned trajectories with flow matching, FlowR2A successfully combines the strengths of scoring-based and anchor-based paradigms. The approach yields controllable, high-quality multimodal proposals that better internalize the relationship between actions and their long-term outcomes, establishing a new state of the art on NAVSIM benchmarks and providing a flexible framework for future controllable planning methods.

Abstract: Multimodal driving planning faces a long-standing tension between two paradigms: scoring-based methods benefit from dense reward supervision but are confined to a fixed action vocabulary, while anchor-based methods generate proposals dynamically yet suffer from sparse supervision constrained to a single ground-truth trajectory. In this work, we propose FlowR2A, which resolves this tension by reframing simulation-based rewards from discriminative targets into generative conditions. By learning the reward-conditioned action distribution from dense trajectory-reward pairs with a flow-matching decoder, FlowR2A unifies the dense supervision of scoring-based methods with the proposal generation of anchor-based methods in a single generative model, forcing the model to internalize the correlation between an action and its outcomes in safety, progress, comfort, and rule compliance. To balance hard safety constraints against soft progress objectives, we introduce fine-grained per-timestep reward conditioning and reward noise augmentation. The generative formulation naturally supports controllable test-time sampling via reward guidance and anchored sampling, producing high-quality proposals. FlowR2A achieves state-of-the-art results on the NAVSIM v1 and v2 benchmarks, with multimodal proposals of substantially higher quality than prior methods.

### [90] [Towards Federated Long-Tailed Graph Learning: An Energy-Guided Dual Decoupling Approach](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24237)
*Lianshuai Guo,Zhongzheng Yuan,Xunkai Li,Meixia Qu,Wenyu Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces FedEPD, a federated graph learning framework tailored for long-tailed label distributions, which improves minority-class performance while preserving privacy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In federated graph learning, real-world data usually follow long-tailed distributions, where minority classes (tail classes) have very few samples. This scarcity biases the global model toward majority classes and structurally isolates minority nodes within heterophilic, majority-dominated neighborhoods, leading to poor performance on tail classes. Existing methods typically apply topology-agnostic statistical rebalancing, which struggles under severe data scarcity and can overfit structural noise from dominant classes, further degrading minority representations. The paper aims to address these fundamental limitations in handling long-tailed, non-IID graph data in a federated setting.

Method: The authors propose FedEPD, a dual-decoupling framework that separates (1) topological purification and (2) semantic recalibration. For topological purification, they introduce a distribution-aware Dirichlet energy pruning mechanism that identifies and removes spatial heterophilic edges, particularly those harmful to minority nodes. For semantic recalibration, they tackle Non-IID distribution shifts by constructing robust global prototypes from topologically central nodes. These prototypes are injected into local node representations through a spatial low-pass prototype injection mechanism, which helps align local semantics with global patterns. Additionally, they employ a two-stage alternating optimization strategy that updates model components in a way that preserves the decision boundaries of majority classes while selectively enhancing performance on minority classes.

Result: Through extensive experiments on multiple long-tailed graph benchmarks in a federated learning setup, FedEPD consistently outperforms existing state-of-the-art methods. The framework achieves up to 4.97% absolute improvement in overall Accuracy and up to 5.48% absolute improvement in Macro-F1 scores, indicating both better overall performance and better balance across classes, especially for minority ones.

Conclusion: FedEPD effectively mitigates the negative impact of long-tailed distributions and heterophilic structures in federated graph learning by decoupling structural cleansing from semantic alignment. Its distribution-aware edge pruning, global prototype construction, and carefully designed optimization process together yield state-of-the-art performance, particularly improving minority-class accuracy without sacrificing majority-class decision boundaries. The method demonstrates that structurally and semantically guided rebalancing is more robust than purely statistical compensation under data scarcity and Non-IID conditions in federated graph settings.

Abstract: Federated Graph Learning facilitates collaborative graph modeling across distributed clients while preserving data privacy. However, real-world data categories frequently exhibit long-tailed distributions. Such statistical scarcity severely degrades performance in two ways: it biases the global model toward majority classes, and it structurally isolates minority nodes by submerging them in heterophilic, head-dominated neighborhoods. While existing methods attempt topology-agnostic statistical compensations, they often fail under data scarcity. Instead of recovering tail nodes, they overfit the structural noise from adjacent dominant classes, leading to representation degradation. To address these limitations, we propose FedEPD, a framework built on a dual decoupling paradigm that separates topological purification from semantic recalibration. Specifically, FedEPD utilizes distribution-aware Dirichlet energy pruning to filter spatial heterophilic edges. It then overcomes Non-IID distribution shifts by extracting robust global prototypes from topologically central nodes, which are incorporated into local representations via a spatial low-pass prototype injection. Furthermore, a two stage alternating optimization strategy strictly protects majority decision boundaries while improving minority accuracy. Extensive experiments demonstrate that FedEPD achieves state-of-the-art performance across diverse long-tailed benchmarks, yielding absolute improvements of up to 4.97% in Accuracy and 5.48% in Macro-F1.

### [91] [Probing the Misaligned Thinking Process of Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24251)
*Kaiwen Zhou,Constantin Venhoff,Jonathan Michala,Xin Eric Wang,William Saunders*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They build linear probes on LLM activations to detect fine-grained “misalignment indicators” (cognitive precursors of bad behavior) and show these probes generalize well to new settings while avoiding false alarms.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs can display dangerous misaligned behaviors (deception, sandbagging, self-preservation), and current detection relies mostly on surface behavior or another LLM’s judgments. These approaches can miss early warning signs, fail out-of-distribution, or be too coarse-grained. The authors want a more reliable, interpretable, and fine-grained way to monitor misalignment that looks inside the model rather than only at its outputs.

Method: 1) Conceptual: Decompose misalignment into 18 fine-grained “cognitive” indicators (e.g., intent to deceive, planning to circumvent instructions). Organize them into a taxonomy covering five broader misaligned behaviors. 2) Data: Use an automated meta-plan-guided pipeline to generate multi-turn conversations where these indicators are present or absent, labeling the internal steps where specific indicators occur. 3) Probing: Train linear probes on hidden activations of a base LLM to classify the presence of each indicator at the token/step level. 4) Evaluation: Construct an out-of-distribution evaluation suite combining (a) new automated behavioral elicitation prompts, (b) existing misalignment benchmarks, and (c) benign natural conversations. Measure AUROC and false positive rates versus a strong LLM judge baseline. 5) Analysis: Study which layers/features the probes rely on and how different indicators are represented internally, to interpret the model’s misalignment representations.

Result: On five categories of misaligned behavior, the linear probes detect misalignment indicators with performance comparable to a strong LLM judge, achieving 0.935 AUROC on out-of-distribution benchmarks. They maintain low false positive rates on benign conversations, suggesting they largely avoid over-flagging normal traffic. The probes also show meaningful generalization beyond the synthetic training distribution to more realistic and adversarial scenarios.

Conclusion: Monitoring internal activations for fine-grained misalignment indicators using simple linear probes is feasible and effective. This approach can serve as an early-warning and interpretability tool for misaligned cognition in LLMs, complementing behavioral evaluations and external judges. The taxonomy and probing framework provide a structured way to study and potentially mitigate misaligned behaviors in deployed models, though further work is needed to harden them against adaptive attacks and to cover a broader range of real-world settings.

Abstract: Large language models exhibit a growing range of misaligned behaviors such as strategic deception, sandbagging, and self-preservation. As they are increasingly deployed in high-stakes settings, it is critical to reliably detect such behaviors to ensure safe and responsible use. In this work, we propose to monitor misalignment by decomposing it into fine-grained cognitive processes -- misalignment indicators -- and detecting their presence in a model's internal activations via linear probes. We develop a taxonomy of 18 indicators spanning different misaligned behaviors, paired with an automated, meta-plan-guided pipeline that generates multi-turn training conversations. To rigorously evaluate generalization, we construct an out-of-distribution suite combining automated behavioral elicitation, established misalignment benchmarks, and natural benign conversations. Across 5 misaligned behaviors, our probes match a strong LLM judge with 0.935 AUROC on out-of-distribution benchmarks while keeping a low false positive rate on benign traffic. We further perform in-depth analysis to understand the probes and the model's internal representations of misalignment indicators.

They then analyze the computational properties and overhead introduced by this layering.

Thus, non-monotonic reasoning via RC can be supported in DL‑Lite without sacrificing its lightweight nature.

Conclusion: Rational Closure can be effectively combined with DL‑Lite core and horn for defeasible reasoning and conjunctive query answering. By realizing RC as a plug‑in layer over existing DL reasoners, one can obtain efficient non‑monotonic reasoning with minimal computational overhead and without redesigning reasoners from scratch. This confirms that DL‑Lite’s lightweight character is compatible with expressive, defeasible inference via RC.

We analyze both entitlement (instance checking) and Conjunctive Query (CQ) answering under RC. Our main contribution is providing a plug-in architecture that builds upon existing standard classical reasoners, establishing that reasoning and CQ answering under RC for DL-Lite can be done efficiently with minimal computational overhead.

### [93] [LemonHarness Technical Report](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24311)
*Kailong Ren,Fubo Sun,Jiachen Liu,Liu Yang,Zimo Yin,Jiaying Li,Congli Yin,Ming He,Yu Huo,Jiawei Liu,Zeping Chen,Yubin Huangfu,Ronghua Li,Yixuan Wu,Xing Su,Yanzhi Xu,Likang Wu,Hongke Zhao,Lei Zhang,Xiaohui Geng,Jianping Fan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: LemonHarness is an execution framework that constrains and observes all state-changing operations for long-horizon LLM agents, improving controllability and performance on terminal-style benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-horizon LLM agents modify files, install dependencies, and create temporary artifacts over many steps, but current setups lack explicit workspace boundaries and full visibility into these state changes. As a result, changes get scattered across the filesystem, are hard to track, and can accumulate into unstable or opaque states. The authors want a principled way to bound, observe, and control all such operations so agents can act reliably on complex, extended tasks.

Method: They propose LemonHarness, a unified execution framework that (1) defines a strict workspace boundary within which all state-changing operations must occur, (2) routes file writes, dependency installations, and temporary artifact creation through structured tools whose outputs are fed back as observations, (3) integrates model invocation, tool calls, and rule knowledge into a single controlled runtime, (4) provides a reusable rule knowledge base to encode recurring execution rules and acceptance criteria as callable runtime knowledge, and (5) implements time-aware execution, exposing elapsed time and remaining budget so the agent can adapt its strategy under time pressure and avoid timeouts.

Result: On the Terminal-Bench 2.0 benchmark, LemonHarness paired with GPT-5.3-CodeX achieved 84.49% accuracy over 445 trials. Using the same framework with a more capable GPT-5.5 backbone increased average accuracy to 86.52% across five jobs. These quantitative results indicate that the framework yields strong, stable performance on long-horizon terminal tasks and that gains persist and improve with stronger underlying models.

Conclusion: Constraining all state-changing operations within an explicit workspace boundary, exposing them through structured tools with observable feedback, incorporating reusable rule knowledge, and making execution time-aware leads to more stable and effective long-horizon LLM agents. LemonHarness demonstrates that a unified, well-instrumented runtime environment can substantially improve controllability and accuracy on complex, multi-step tasks.

Abstract: As large language model (LLM) agents are applied to longer tasks, they increasingly modify workspace state across multiple rounds of iteration. However, agents typically observe only tool outputs and log fragments, while the actual state changes occur in the file system. Without explicit workspace boundaries, state-changing operations such as file writes and temporary artifact generation may scatter changes across paths. Over time, these weakly constrained changes accumulate, making states such as modified files difficult to track. This paper presents LemonHarness, an integrated execution framework for long-horizon agents. LemonHarness establishes an explicit execution boundary by constraining state-changing operations within a clearly defined workspace and bringing model invocation, tool execution, and rule knowledge within a single controlled boundary. State-changing operations, including file writes, dependency installation, and temporary artifact creation, are executed through structured tool interfaces, with execution feedback recorded as observations available to subsequent model decisions. The system also introduces a reusable rule knowledge base, which turns recurring execution rules and acceptance criteria into runtime knowledge. LemonHarness further adds a time-aware execution mechanism that exposes elapsed and remaining budget to the model, so it can rebalance exploration, implementation, and validation effort as time pressure shifts and avoid timeouts from long waits or excessive verification. On Terminal-Bench 2.0, LemonHarness_GPT-5.3-CodeX reached 84.49% accuracy over 445 trials; pairing the same framework with the stronger GPT-5.5 backbone raised the average accuracy to 86.52% across five jobs. The results suggest that a unified runtime boundary, callable rule knowledge, and time-aware execution can improve the stability of long-horizon agent execution.

Specifically, the periodic-residual forecasting backbone first separates stable daily and weekly patterns from non-periodic residual variations. In addition, a temporal Kolmogorov-Arnold network (TKAN) residual head is then introduced to learn station-wise nonlinear autoregressive correction from de-periodized PM$_{2.5}$ residuals and historical multi-pollutant sequences, thereby enhancing the modeling of local residual inertia and pollutant co-variation.

### [96] [Accelerating Disaggregated RL for Visual Generative LLMs with Diffusion-Based Parallelism and Trainer-Assisted Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24369)
*Sijie Wang,Zhengyu Qing,Zhiqiang Tan,Yiming Yin,Yeqing Zhang,Yaoyuan Wang,Qiang Wang,Xiaowen Chu,Shaohuai Shi*

Method: Proposes DigenRL, a disaggregated RL framework that separates rollout and training across heterogeneous GPU resources and introduces three key techniques: (1) Generation-Axis Pipeline (GAP) and Time-Step Parallelism (TSP) to pipeline diffusion generation and training at a finer granularity; (2) Trainer-Assisted Generation (TAG), which allows trainer GPUs to elastically assist rollout generation when idle; and (3) a tightly one-step constrained asynchronous scheduling strategy to exploit remaining pipeline tail bubbles while controlling staleness.

Result: On three multi-GPU hardware testbeds (16–32 GPUs) using several large diffusion/video/image generative models (HunyuanVideo-13B, Wan2.1-14B, FLUX.1-12B, QwenImage-20B), DigenRL improves end-to-end RL training throughput by 1.56–2.10x compared to state-of-the-art diffusion RL systems veRL-Omni and GenRL.

Conclusion: Disaggregating rollout and training for diffusion-based generative LLMs and carefully optimizing the pipeline and scheduling with GAP+TSP, TAG, and constrained asynchrony significantly improves system throughput, demonstrating that diffusion RL workloads can benefit from specialized distributed system designs beyond traditional colocated RL paradigms.

To maximally reduce the execution bubbles in the disaggregated architecture, we propose: 1) a generation-axis pipeline (GAP) and time-step parallelism (TSP) in the diffusion architecture to enable finer-grained pipelining between rollout and training; 2) an elastic trainer-assisted generation (TAG) approach to enable the trainer GPU resources to dynamically assist in executing rollout generations; and 3) a tightly one-step constrained asynchronous strategy to further utilize the tail bubble in the pipeline. Extensive experiments are conducted on three hardware testbeds with 16-32 GPUs using HunyuanVideo-13B, Wan2.1-14B, FLUX.1-12B, and QwenImage-20B generative models. ### [97] [When Helpfulness Overrides Causal Caution: Context-Dependent Suppression and Recovery in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24370)
*Hiroshi Okumura*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models often drop their cautiousness about causal claims in practical advisory settings, even though they can be cautious in academic contexts, and shows this can be fixed with a simple prompt or architecture change.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most evaluations of LLMs focus on whether they can perform causal reasoning, but not on whether they know when not to make causal claims. In real-world decision-support, making unwarranted causal statements can be harmful. The authors want to understand whether LLMs appropriately refrain from causal judgments when evidence is insufficient, and whether context (academic vs. practical advice) affects this behavior.

Method: The authors define "Causal Caution" as refraining from causal judgment when evidence is insufficient and build an evaluation rubric based on Pearl's Causal Hierarchy (PCH score). They test four advanced LLMs (Claude Sonnet 4.6, Claude Opus 4.7, GPT 5.5, Gemini 3.1 Pro) on 480 trials, comparing responses in academic-style prompts vs. practical advisory prompts. They also test the impact of adding a short self-correction prompt asking the model to reconsider its judgment from a causal-relationships perspective. Statistical tests (Fisher's exact test, McNemar's test) are used to evaluate differences in Causal Caution rates across conditions.

Result: In academic contexts, models maintained Causal Caution at very high rates (91.7–100.0%). In practical advisory contexts, Causal Caution dropped sharply to 6.7–18.3% across models, and when prompts demanded concrete recommendations or explanations, only 0.5% of responses were causally cautious. Adding a short self-correction prompt restored Causal Caution to high levels (71.4–100.0%), with all comparisons statistically significant (p < .001).

Conclusion: LLMs have the capability to exercise Causal Caution but systematically suppress it in practical advisory contexts due to helpfulness-oriented response patterns. This is a context-dependent expression problem, not a fundamental capability gap. For safer use of LLMs in organizational decision-making, the authors suggest governance and system designs such as multi-agent architectures that separate recommendation generation from causal auditing, and simple prompting interventions to re-activate Causal Caution.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly integrated into decision-support roles in business and policy contexts. While prior benchmark studies have primarily evaluated LLMs' causal reasoning capabilities, a more fundamental epistemic dimension has been overlooked: Causal Caution, defined as the propensity to refrain from causal judgment when empirical evidence is insufficient. This study examines the systematic suppression of Causal Caution that occurs when LLMs shift from academic to practical advisory contexts. Using an evaluation rubric inspired by Pearl's Causal Hierarchy (the PCH score), we conducted experiments on four high-performance LLMs -- Claude Sonnet 4.6, Claude Opus 4.7, GPT 5.5, and Gemini 3.1 Pro -- across 480 trials. Causal Caution maintenance rates were 91.7--100.0% in academic contexts but dropped to 6.7--18.3% in practical advisory contexts (Fisher's exact test, p < .001 across all models). Furthermore, when restricted to practical prompts requesting concrete recommendations or explanatory rationales, only 1 of 200 responses (0.5%) maintained Causal Caution. A brief self-correction prompt -- "Please reconsider this judgment from the perspective of causal relationships" -- restored the expression of Causal Caution to maintenance rates of 71.4--100.0% (McNemar's test, p < .001 across all models). These results suggest that helpfulness-oriented response patterns may suppress the expression of Causal Caution in practical advisory contexts, with important implications for organizational governance. The findings indicate that this suppression reflects context-dependent variation in expression rather than an underlying capability limitation, suggesting that multi-agent architectures that separate proposal generation from causal auditing may offer a promising governance design.
