### [99] [Age of LLM: A Strategic 1v1 Benchmark for Reasoning, Diplomacy and Reliability of Large Language Models under Fog of War](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24391)
*Arnaud Ricci*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes Age of LLM, a competitive 1v1 strategy-game benchmark where LLMs battle on a grid with fog of war, diplomacy, and strict JSON action constraints, to study reasoning under adversarial uncertainty.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM benchmarks are often static, public, and easily contaminated by training data, and they rarely probe long-horizon, adversarial, partially observed decision-making. The authors want a dynamic, private, game-based environment that stresses belief tracking, reliability in following formal schemas, and strategic/diplomatic reasoning between models.

Method: They design a private game engine for a 13x7 grid-based, base-destruction strategy game with fog of war, secret uranium for nuclear weapons, and rich diplomacy mechanics. Two LLMs take alternating turns, each turn submitting actions in a strict JSON schema where illegal actions are discarded. Models receive a rules-only prompt and must discover strategies themselves. The authors pit 15 reasoning-oriented LLMs against random opponents and maps, collecting 54 matches and 5,258 actions. They then analyze strategies (e.g., nuclear vs conventional), game duration, diplomacy usage, error types in illegal actions, and correlations between reliability and win rate.

Result: Empirically, a “nuclear rush” strategy dominates most games, especially on rules-coherent subsets, largely due to mechanical properties of secret-simultaneous launch rather than a failure of deterrence reasoning. Traditional military conquest is relatively rare but leads to faster wins. Diplomatic interactions are frequent in messages but almost never develop into binding, sustained agreements. Around 58% of illegal actions stem from errors in tracking fog-of-war or game state, suggesting that illegal-action frequency captures belief-tracking difficulty. A tentative correlation appears between higher reliability (fewer illegal actions) and higher win rates, though this is exploratory and weak given the small, unbalanced corpus.

Conclusion: Age of LLM provides a preliminary but informative benchmark and dataset for studying how LLMs plan and reason under adversarial uncertainty, including their belief-tracking, emergent deception, and model-specific “cognitive personas.” While the current ranking of 15 models is not positioned as a definitive leaderboard due to dataset limitations, the released replays, viewer, and format are intended as infrastructure for future research on interactive, strategic evaluation of LLMs.

Abstract: We introduce Age of LLM, a turn-based 1v1 benchmark in which two LLMs face off on a 13x7 grid to destroy the enemy base. Three stressors are deliberate: fog of war, full diplomacy (messages, ceasefires, ultimatums; uranium kept secret), and a reliability dimension where every turn must follow a strict JSON schema and an illegal action is silently discarded. The engine is private and each match uses a fresh random map seed and opponent, mitigating the data contamination that affects public benchmarks. Models receive a (near) rule-only prompt with no build-order advice (two tactical seed phrases were present during data collection; see Section 2.7). We benchmark 15 reasoning models across 54 matches and 5,258 actions. Findings: (1) the nuclear rush dominates (78% on the rules-coherent v0.11+ sub-corpus; 85% corpus-wide) with a sole-launcher signature that is largely mechanical under secret-simultaneous launch rules, not a cognitive deterrence failure; (2) military conquest is rare but faster (12.3 vs 18.9 turns); (3) diplomacy is prolific yet almost never consummated; (4) ~58% of illegal actions are fog/state errors, making the illegal-action rate a measure of belief-tracking; (5) -- the least established, and the only one we label exploratory -- a weak link associates reliability with winning. The corpus is small, unbalanced and not side-swapped, so the ranking is a preliminary descriptive view, not a contribution. Beyond ranking, the turn-by-turn traces of actions and messages make the corpus a lens on how LLMs reason under adversarial uncertainty -- their belief-tracking, spontaneous deception, and per-model cognitive "personas" -- which we frame as a future research direction. We release the replay format, an isometric viewer and all replays; engine source on request.

Clinical ECG reporting instead unfolds iteratively, requiring progressive context integration and bidirectional editing. Because its agents use ECG analysis models already in clinical use, the underlying findings are clinically trustworthy; and as a cloud-based web service, \textsc{ATRIA} is ready for immediate deployment.   <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for automated explanation that is faithful to the underlying certificates, efficient, and suitable for deployment without cloud dependencies.

Method: They design a cycle-consistent neural architecture with two networks: NN1 maps verification certificates to natural language explanations, and NN2 maps explanations back to certificates. A symbolic verifier is used to check that the reconstructed certificates still satisfy or violate the temporal properties, and this is used as a differentiable proxy for explanation faithfulness. The model performs better in 10 of 12 verdict/method categories, with three categories achieving 100% soundness. Inference is also dramatically more efficient: about 185 ms per certificate versus 160 s with the multi-LLM pipeline (an 860x speedup), with the added benefits of running offline, producing deterministic outputs, and having zero marginal cost per inference.

Conclusion: A dedicated, cycle-consistent neural architecture tailored to verification certificates can provide faithful, grounded natural language explanations that surpass general-purpose LLM prompting in accuracy, speed, determinism, and deployability. This shows that trained specialization is a strong solution for explaining structured formal artifacts like verification proofs, particularly in domains such as financial compliance where stakeholders require understandable yet rigorously faithful justifications and where cloud-based LLM inference is undesirable or impractical.

Abstract: Formal verification produces machine-checkable certificates that attest to the satisfaction or violation of temporal properties, yet these certificates remain opaque to non-specialist stakeholders. We propose a cycle-consistent neural architecture that generates faithful natural language explanations of verification certificates. A forward network NN1 maps certificates to explanations, and an inverse network NN2 reconstructs certificates from explanations; a symbolic verifier closes the loop, providing a differentiable faithfulness proxy. A pointer-generator mechanism ensures lexical grounding by copying state names directly from the certificate. We evaluate on 420 test certificates spanning six verification methods (bounded proof, k-induction, inductive invariant, lasso, reachability, witness pair) in both YES and NO verdict variants, drawn from a financial compliance domain with 207 named states. Our trained architecture, combined with a hybrid inference-time routing strategy, achieves 90.0% cycle-verified soundness, surpassing a multi- LLM few-shot baseline (76.1% for the best of 16 LLM combinations across four frontier models) by 13.9 percentage points. The neural model wins on 10 of 12 verdict/kind categories, with three categories reaching 100% soundness. The architecture offers 860x faster inference (185 ms vs. 160 s per certificate for the full multi-LLM baseline), offline operation, deterministic outputs, and zero per-inference cost. These results demonstrate that trained specialization outperforms general-purpose LLM prompting for structured certificate explanation, while eliminating the deployment constraints of cloud-based inference.

### [103] [Can Aggregate Invariants Accelerate Continuous Subgraph Matching? Limits, Laws, and a Dynamic Spectral Index](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24421)
*Minghao Chen,Jiale Zheng*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies whether spectral filtering (using Laplacian eigenvalue bounds) can effectively accelerate continuous subgraph matching on dynamic graphs, and develops a dynamic local-spectra index plus an evaluation methodology showing when and how such filters help.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Static subgraph matching has seen substantial pruning improvements from spectral filtering based on Laplacian interlacing, but it is unclear if similar aggregate structural tests can be efficiently maintained and remain effective for continuous subgraph matching on dynamic graphs. The authors want to understand whether spectral techniques still bring value when graphs evolve, where updates are frequent and maintaining exact global spectra might be too costly or lose pruning power.

Method: 1) The authors formalize a perturbation relaxation for maintaining spectral bounds under graph updates and derive the tightest safe lazy-maintenance rule, then analyze its pruning power over sequences of edge updates. 2) They analyze the distribution of pruning utility versus recomputation cost across vertices and show that hubs are ineffective for pruning, which motivates a selective, exact recomputation strategy focusing on small neighborhoods; they implement a dynamic local-spectra index that maintains exact local spectra on touched neighborhoods. 3) They integrate these spectral tests into a decoupled continuous subgraph matching benchmark and compare against a control system that is identical except for the spectral component, evaluating across multiple engines, datasets, stream types, and query workloads, including a specially constructed radius-stratified workload that stresses radius-sensitive pruning behavior.

Result: 1) Lazily maintained spectral bounds quickly become ineffective; even the tightest safe relaxation loses almost all pruning power within four touching updates, exactly where pruning would have been useful. 2) Exact spectral maintenance is made affordable by selectively recomputing only for vertices with small neighborhoods, taking advantage of an observed and theoretically supported anti-correlation between pruning utility and recomputation cost; hubs never prune, while low-degree vertices can be updated in microseconds per change while preserving exactness. 3) In the benchmark, the integrated spectral tests remove up to 51% of candidate matches or skip up to 47% of update enumerations without changing enumeration intermediates (other than skipping first-level bindings) across a diverse set of graphs, streams, and queries; in a specially constructed workload, they achieve up to a 99.9% reduction in intermediates and 748× speedup. They also confirm that spectral filtering mainly speeds up operations scaling with candidate set sizes, not adjacency-guided exploration.

Conclusion: Spectral filtering can effectively accelerate continuous subgraph matching on dynamic graphs, but only when maintained exactly and selectively at local neighborhoods rather than via lazy perturbation bounds, which rapidly lose pruning power. The proposed dynamic local-spectra index enables microsecond-level updates on small neighborhoods and provides substantial pruning benefits in realistic and constructed workloads, primarily by reducing candidate construction and list scans while leaving the core exploration unchanged. The authors advocate an intermediate-invariance methodology for assessing CSM filters and release their local-spectra index as a reusable component for future systems.

Abstract: Spectral filtering recently delivered substantial pruning for \emph{static} subgraph matching: Laplacian interlacing rejects candidates whose neighborhoods cannot host the query. We study whether such aggregate structural tests can accelerate \emph{continuous} subgraph matching (CSM) over dynamic graphs, and answer in three parts. First, lazily maintained spectral bounds are infeasible exactly where spectral pruning has value: we characterize the tightest safe rule over a formalized perturbation relaxation and show that even it loses essentially all pruning power within four touching updates. Second, exact maintenance is affordable when selective: pruning utility and recomputation cost are anti-correlated across vertices -- hubs provably never prune -- so recomputing small-neighborhood spectra on touch sustains exact local spectra at microseconds per update, complete by construction. Third, integrated into a decoupled CSM benchmark against an identical-minus-spectra control, the tests remove up to $51\%$ of candidates or safely skip up to $47\%$ of update enumerations, yet enumeration intermediates remain unchanged -- beyond the gates' skipped first-level bindings, typically zero -- across two engines, four real graphs, two stream types, and $77$ solved queries; a constructed radius-stratified workload confirms the instrument detects the exception when one exists ($-99.9\%$ intermediates, $748\times$ faster). Aggregate tests accelerate what scales with candidate sets -- construction, list scans -- never adjacency-guided exploration. We distill an intermediate-invariance methodology for evaluating CSM filters and release a reusable dynamic local-spectra index.

### [104] [ReM-MoA: Reasoning Memory Sustains Mixture-of-Agents Scaling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24437)
*Heng Ping,Arijit Bhattacharjee,Peiyu Zhang,Shixuan Li,Wei Yang,Ali Jannesari,Nesreen Ahmed,Paul Bogdan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces ReM-MoA, a memory-augmented mixture-of-agents framework that sustains and improves performance as more reasoning layers are added, by using ranked cross-layer reasoning memory and diversified routing of traces.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Mixture-of-Agents (MoA) architectures aim to scale LLM reasoning at inference time by composing multiple agents in layered pipelines. However, as depth increases, their performance either degrades, plateaus early, or saturates, failing to fully leverage additional layers. The authors are motivated to find mechanisms that preserve and amplify the benefits of deeper multi-agent reasoning instead of losing them, identifying the lack of structured, cross-layer reasoning memory and diversity-preserving information flow as key bottlenecks.

Method: The authors propose ReM-MoA, which augments MoA with a persistent, structured reasoning memory and a routing strategy: (1) Ranked Reasoning Memory: all reasoning traces from every layer are stored and scored by a dedicated Reviewer Agent that compares traces and ranks them, forming a cross-layer memory of reasoning quality. (2) Curated Diversified Memory Routing: agents at each layer are exposed to curated subsets of both successful and failed reasoning traces, promoting diversity in exploration while still propagating high-quality patterns. (3) Optional multi-domain Reviewer distillation: the Reviewer is further trained using supervision from a stronger frontier model across multiple domains to improve ranking fidelity. These mechanisms are evaluated across multiple benchmarks and depth/width configurations.

Result: On five reasoning benchmarks (math, formal logic, code, knowledge, commonsense), ReM-MoA consistently outperforms prior MoA variants, and its performance keeps improving with greater depth and width, unlike baselines that saturate or degrade. The performance gap between ReM-MoA and existing MoA methods widens as depth increases, indicating that the proposed memory and routing mechanisms enable more effective scaling of multi-agent reasoning.

Conclusion: Structured cross-layer reasoning memory and diversified trace routing are crucial missing components for scalable multi-agent LLM inference. By ranking and selectively reusing reasoning traces across layers, and by ensuring agents see diverse combinations of past successes and failures, ReM-MoA can sustain and amplify performance gains as the architecture deepens. This establishes ReM-MoA as a more scalable approach to mixture-of-agents reasoning and highlights memory-augmented, reviewer-driven coordination as a promising design direction for future multi-agent LLM systems.

Abstract: Mixture-of-Agents (MoA) architectures improve inference-time scaling by organizing multiple LLM agents into layered reasoning pipelines. However, existing MoA variants fail to sustain gains as depth increases, exhibiting degradation, early plateauing, or saturation. We propose ReM-MoA, a memory-augmented MoA framework that sustains scaling through two mechanisms: (1) a Ranked Reasoning Memory that persistently stores and ranks reasoning traces from all layers using a comparative Reviewer Agent, and (2) a Curated Diversified Memory Routing scheme that exposes different agents to distinct combinations of successful and failed traces, preserving exploration diversity while propagating high-quality reasoning. We further introduce an optional multi-domain Reviewer distillation pipeline that improves ranking quality through frontier-model supervision. Across five reasoning benchmarks spanning math, formal logic, code, knowledge, and commonsense, ReM-MoA consistently outperforms prior MoA variants across both depth and width scaling, and its advantage widens with depth, establishing structured cross-layer reasoning memory as a key missing mechanism for scalable multi-agent inference.

### [105] [Bayesian control for coding agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24453)
*Theodore Papamarkou,Vladislav Smirnov,Viktor Mazanov,Artem Vazhentsev,Preslav Nakov,Timothy Baldwin,Artem Shelmanov*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing coding agents use tools like cheap critics and expensive verifiers, but their orchestrators typically rely on rigid, heuristic rules that do not model uncertainty or optimize the trade-off between performance and tool cost. This leads to suboptimal use of tools, especially when verifiers are expensive and feedback is noisy.

Method: They cast tool orchestration as a cost-sensitive sequential hypothesis testing problem. A Bayesian controller maintains a belief (probability) that the current code candidate is correct, then sequentially chooses actions—gather more evidence via diagnostics, refine the candidate, call an expensive verifier, or stop—so as to optimally balance expected coding success against tool costs. The belief state is updated with evidence from tools and is also used as a correctness confidence score.

Result: Across six different code generators and nine coding benchmarks, their Bayesian controller improves the cost–performance trade-off compared to rule-based orchestration, particularly in regimes where verification is costly and critics provide useful but imperfect signals. The learned belief state also yields a correctness score that better correlates with actual correctness than standard measures like token probabilities or simple counts of tool successes.

Conclusion: Modeling orchestration of coding tools as Bayesian, cost-sensitive sequential decision-making leads to more efficient use of diagnostics and verifiers, especially in realistic, noisy settings. The resulting belief state not only guides tool decisions but also serves as a superior, interpretable uncertainty metric for code correctness, outperforming common baselines.

Abstract: Modern coding agents pair LLM generators with various tools, including cheap diagnostics and expensive verifiers. The tool-use decisions are typically governed by orchestrators that often use fixed rules and ignore uncertainty. We formulate orchestration as cost-sensitive sequential hypothesis testing: a Bayesian controller maintains a belief over candidate correctness and dynamically decides whether to gather more evidence, refine the candidate, verify it, or stop. Across six generators and nine coding benchmarks, Bayesian control proves to be most valuable when verification is costly and critics are informative but imperfect. Beyond control, the belief state yields an interpretable correctness score that outperforms token-probability and raw tool-success baselines for uncertainty quantification.

### [106] [CompressKV: Semantic-Retrieval-Guided KV-Cache Compression for Resource-Efficient Long-Context LLM Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24467)
*Xiaolin Lin,Jingcun Wang,Olga Kondrateva,Yiyu Shi,Bing Li,Grace Li Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: CompressKV is a KV-cache compression framework for long-context GQA-based LLMs that keeps only semantically important tokens using specialized attention heads and layer-wise budget allocation, achieving near-full performance with a tiny fraction of KV memory.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-context LLM inference is bottlenecked by the large memory and compute cost of key-value caches, especially on resource-constrained hardware. Existing KV eviction methods use simple heuristics over all attention heads, ignoring that different heads serve different roles. This can cause critical tokens to be discarded, significantly harming model quality. A more principled, head-aware way to select which tokens to keep is needed to improve the resource–performance trade-off.

Method: The authors introduce CompressKV, a KV-cache compression framework tailored to GQA-based LLMs. They first analyze attention heads and identify Semantic Retrieval Heads (SRHs) that tend to focus on prompt boundaries and semantically important mid-context evidence. Instead of aggregating scores over all heads, CompressKV uses only SRHs to score tokens and decide which KV pairs to retain under a memory budget. Additionally, they estimate the eviction error for each layer offline and use this to allocate different KV budgets per layer, prioritizing layers where eviction is more harmful. The method is evaluated on standard long-context benchmarks.

Result: On LongBench and Needle-in-a-Haystack benchmarks, CompressKV consistently outperforms prior KV cache eviction baselines under the same memory constraints. On LongBench QA tasks, it preserves over 97% of the performance of a full KV cache while using only 3% of the KV memory. On Needle-in-a-Haystack, it reaches 90% accuracy with just 0.7% of the KV storage. These results indicate that leveraging SRHs and layer-wise budget allocation yields better accuracy for the same or smaller KV cache size.

Conclusion: CompressKV effectively improves the resource–performance trade-off for long-context LLM inference by using head-specialization (Semantic Retrieval Heads) and informed layer-wise budget allocation to guide KV cache compression. It shows that understanding and exploiting the functional roles of attention heads allows aggressive KV reduction while preserving most of the original model performance, making long-context inference more feasible on limited hardware.

Abstract: Long-context large language model (LLM) inference is increasingly constrained by the memory footprint and decoding cost of key-value (KV) caches, limiting sustainable deployment on resource-constrained hardware. Existing KV cache eviction methods typically apply heuristic token scoring over all heads in GQA-based LLMs. These methods ignore the different functionalities of attention heads, leading to the eviction of critical tokens and thus degrading the performance of LLMs. To address this issue, we propose CompressKV, a resource-efficient KV-cache compression framework for GQA-based LLMs. Instead of aggregating attention scores from all heads, CompressKV identifies Semantic Retrieval Heads (SRHs) that capture both the initial and final tokens of a prompt and semantically important mid-context evidence, and uses them to select tokens whose KV pairs should be retained. Furthermore, CompressKV allocates cache budgets across layers according to offline estimates of layer-wise eviction error. Experiments on LongBench and Needle-in-a-Haystack show that CompressKV consistently outperforms existing KV-cache eviction methods across memory budgets. Notably, it preserves over 97\% of full-cache performance using only 3\% of the KV cache on LongBench question-answering tasks and achieves 90\% accuracy with just 0.7\% KV storage on Needle-in-a-Haystack. These results demonstrate an improved resource--performance trade-off for long-context LLM inference. Our code is publicly available at: https://github.com/TUDa-HWAI/CompressKV

</details>


### [107] [The Latent Bridge: A Continuous Slow-Fast Channel for Real-Time Game Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24470)
*Bojie Li,Noah Shi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They build a real-time AI agent for general computer use and games by coupling a slow but smart reasoning vision-language model with a fast but less capable reactive model, comparing text-based and latent feature-based communication bridges between them.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Typical vision-language models either think slowly but well (good planning, seconds per response) or act quickly but poorly (milliseconds, weak planning). Real-time control tasks like games need both fast reaction (tens of milliseconds) and long-horizon planning (seconds), which a single existing model cannot provide. The authors want a way to combine these complementary strengths without retraining the large models themselves.

Method: They freeze two models of similar size: an 8B parameter reasoning VLM (Qwen3-VL-8B-Thinking) and a 9B reactive VLM (MiniCPM-o 4.5). The only trainable part is a communication bridge from the slow to the fast model. They study three setups: Fast-Only (F), a standard Text Bridge (T) where the slow model outputs a textual suffix read by the fast model, and a novel Latent Bridge (L) that maps the slow model’s internal residual representations into the fast model’s input-embedding space in a LLaVA-style, avoiding text generation/parsing. They train an action decoder per channel on held-out environment seeds and evaluate on 7 Atari games and the MetaDrive driving simulator.

Result: Across 7 Atari games and MetaDrive, the Latent Bridge matches or outperforms the Text Bridge everywhere while never catastrophically hurting performance when the Text Bridge helps. It yields large gains on MsPacman (+57%) and RoadRunner (+28%) over Fast-Only, and is neutral when additional reasoning is not useful (e.g., MetaDrive). Combining Text and Latent bridges harms performance severely on at least one game (RoadRunner -96%), showing interference. Quantitatively, the usefulness of the bridge is highly correlated with how much slow reasoning alone beats fast reaction (correlation r=0.93 between Text and Latent gains over Fast-Only).

Conclusion: Coupling a slow reasoning VLM with a fast reactive VLM via a learned latent-space bridge is an effective and predictable way to get real-time agents that benefit from deliberation without incurring text-generation latency. The Latent Bridge is a safe, often superior drop-in replacement for text-based communication, and it only helps when the underlying slow model is actually better than the fast one on the task. The destructive interference from combining channels suggests using a single, well-designed bridge. The authors provide resources for reproducibility, such as replay data and pipelines.

Abstract: A real-time agent for general computer use - with games as the most demanding case - must act within tens of milliseconds while still planning over seconds. These two regimes sit at opposite ends of the latency-quality tradeoff. A reasoning VLM (Qwen3-VL-8B-Thinking) deliberates effectively but requires ~1.5 s per response - far too slow for a 15 Hz control loop. In contrast, a reactive VLM (MiniCPM-o 4.5) acts in milliseconds but underperforms on planning-heavy tasks. We couple two frozen models of matched scale (9B reactive, 8B reasoning), leaving the communication channel as the sole trainable component. The standard coupling is a Text Bridge (T): the slow model writes a suffix the fast model reads. We introduce a learned continuous Latent Bridge (L) that projects the slow model's residuals into the fast model's input-embedding space in a LLaVA-style manner, avoiding any text round-trip; both are compared against Fast-Only (F). On 7 Atari games and a driving domain (MetaDrive), tuning the action decoder per channel on held-out seeds, the Latent Bridge matches or beats the Text Bridge in every domain: it significantly improves two games (MsPacman +57%, RoadRunner +28%) and is a safe drop-in elsewhere. Combining both channels interferes destructively (RoadRunner -96%), so only one should be used. The benefit is highly predictable: the bridge helps if and only if slow reasoning already beats fast reaction (T > F) - the Latent and Text gains over Fast-Only move together at r=0.93. MetaDrive is the controlled negative, where the Latent Bridge is demonstrably inert because the Text Bridge adds no value. We release replay recordings and reproducible pipelines.

### [108] [On the Smallness of the Large Language Models Scaling Exponents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24504)
*Sauro Succi,Peter V. Coveney,Alex Hansen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that current LLM scaling laws point to energy use that grows unsustainably, and that proposed statistical corrections (like the pedestal effect) do not really fix this, with some insight drawn from analogies to turbulence models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand whether pushing LLM performance mainly by scaling model and data size is compatible with realistic energy and resource constraints, and to test if recent claims that scaling exponents are biased (due to ignoring a non-zero irreducible loss) might restore sustainability.

Method: The authors analyze how empirical scaling exponents for loss vs. compute/data/model size imply energy demands as models grow, examine the impact of correcting for a finite asymptotic loss floor (pedestal effect) on these exponents, and conceptually compare the role of data smoothness/roughness in LLMs to similar effects in phenomenological turbulence models.

Result: They find that even when one accounts for a non-zero limiting loss (pedestal effect), the effective scaling exponents still correspond to an energy growth that is too steep to be sustainable at very large scales. They also argue that the structure and roughness of the data distribution can influence measured exponents, in a way reminiscent of how intermittency and roughness affect scaling in turbulence, but this does not qualitatively change the unsustainability conclusion.

Conclusion: Scaling current LLMs along current empirical scaling laws leads to energy requirements that are fundamentally unsustainable, and correcting those laws for pedestal effects or invoking data smoothness arguments does not meaningfully alter this. Alternative approaches that go beyond brute-force scaling are needed for long-term progress that is compatible with energy constraints.

Abstract: We discuss reasons why the scaling exponents of current Large Language Models (LLMs) applications are indicating an unsustainable regime in terms of energy resources. We further show that attributing the smallness of such exponents to a numerical bias due to the neglect of a non-zero value of the loss function in the limit of infinite data (``pedestal effect") does not remove the unsustainability issue. Finally, the effects of the smoothness (roughness) of the data on the scaling exponents is commented upon based on an analogy with phenomenological models of fluid turbulence.

Abstract: Rare diseases affect millions of individuals worldwide, yet timely diagnosis remains a major public health challenge due to scarcity of specialized clinical expertise. Synthetic-data ablations suggested that phenotype-anchored narratives provide useful training signal for long-tail rare diseases, with a monotonic scaling trend within the tested data range. Together, RaDaR and its development and validation framework provide a deployable rare-disease reasoning model and a reproducible development framework for diagnostic AI under data scarcity.

### [110] [Reinforcement Learning for Computer-Use Agents with Autonomous Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24515)
*Marta Sumyk,Oleksandr Kosovan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a reinforcement learning framework for Computer-Use Agents that uses an autonomous vision-language model as a noisy but scalable reward signal, and corrects its noise to improve GUI task success.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Training reinforcement learning agents to operate complex desktop GUIs is hard because environments are open-ended and lack scalable, machine-readable reward signals. Many tasks are visually grounded (success is visible in the final UI state) and cannot be easily captured by handcrafted rewards or dense manual annotations, making standard RL training impractical.

Method: The authors introduce an RL fine-tuning pipeline for GUI agents where a Vision-Language Model (VLM) acts as an automatic evaluator. Given the user’s original instruction and the final GUI screenshot, the VLM outputs a binary judgment of whether the task was completed. This judgment serves as a terminal reward during policy optimization. Recognizing that the VLM is imperfect, they explicitly model its outputs as noisy binary rewards and derive a noise-corrected reward estimator tailored to Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO), adjusting the policy gradient to account for estimated evaluator noise.

Result: Across three benchmarks—macOSWorld, Windows Agent Arena, and OSWorld—the proposed noise-corrected evaluator reward improves GUI agent success rates compared to both zero-shot agents and standard RL fine-tuning using raw (uncorrected) evaluator rewards. On average, success rates increase by 12.6 percentage points over zero-shot baselines and by 5.1 percentage points over using raw evaluator feedback without noise correction.

Conclusion: Autonomous vision-language evaluation can be used as a practical, scalable reward signal for reinforcement learning in GUI environments, provided that the evaluator’s noise is explicitly modeled and corrected. Incorporating noise-aware reward estimation into PPO yields substantial performance gains for Computer-Use Agents across diverse desktop benchmarks, easing the dependence on handcrafted rewards and manual labeling.

Abstract: Computer-Use Agents (CUAs) execute high-level user goals by perceiving and acting directly within graphical user interfaces. However, reinforcement learning for CUAs remains difficult because open-ended desktop environments rarely provide scalable, machine-readable reward signals: task success is often visually grounded and hard to specify with handcrafted reward functions or dense manual labels.
  We propose an RL fine-tuning framework that uses autonomous vision-language evaluation as a scalable supervision signal for GUI agents. Given a final screenshot and the original instruction, a Vision-Language Model judges task completion and provides terminal feedback without task-specific heuristics or manual labels during policy optimization.
  Because autonomous evaluators are imperfect, we model their feedback as a noisy binary reward channel and derive a noise-corrected reward estimator for Proximal Policy Optimization. Experiments across macOSWorld, Windows Agent Arena, and OSWorld show that corrected evaluator rewards outperform both zero-shot baselines and raw evaluator rewards, improving success rates by an average of 12.6 percentage points over zero-shot performance and 5.1 points over raw evaluator fine-tuning. These results suggest that autonomous evaluation can serve as a practical reward signal for RL in GUI environments when evaluator noise is explicitly modeled and corrected.

### [111] [Governed Shared Memory for Multi-Agent LLM Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24535)
*Yanki Margalit,Nurit Cohen-Inger,Erni Avram,Ran Taig,Oded Margalit*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces and studies the “fleet-memory” problem for multi-agent LLM systems, identifying key failure modes and proposing concrete system primitives and an implementation to manage shared memory safely and robustly in production.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLM deployments move from single agents to fleets of agents and tenants sharing infrastructure, they need shared memory services. Naively using vector stores or long-context retrieval causes governance and safety problems: data can leak across tenants, stale or contradictory information can persist, and it becomes impossible to audit how knowledge was derived. The authors are motivated to formally characterize these problems and design systems-level mechanisms that support secure, auditable, and well-governed shared memory in real production settings.

Method: The authors formalize the fleet-memory problem and categorize four fundamental failure modes in shared memory: unauthorized leakage, stale propagation, contradiction persistence, and provenance collapse. They then define four corresponding primitives—scoped retrieval, temporal supersession, provenance tracking, and policy-governed propagation—and implement them in a multi-tenant memory service called MemClaw. To evaluate this in realistic conditions, they introduce ArgusFleet, a reproducible test harness focused on governance dimensions, and run experiments and live production measurements rather than standard baseline comparisons.

Result: Using ArgusFleet, the system achieved complete (100%) reconstruction of depth-four provenance chains with accurate writer identity and sub-second latency per hop. Propagation tests showed strong intra-fleet visibility without cross-fleet leakage, and in strong-write mode the memory system achieved write-to-visible latency equal to a single search round-trip. During production evaluation they uncovered subtle architectural issues: asymmetric scope enforcement, where sub-tenant isolation failed on some direct ID-based reads, and a pipeline ordering conflict where synchronous near-duplicate detection could suppress contradictory writes before the contradiction-handling logic executed.

Conclusion: The study concludes that simply relying on long-context retrieval is inadequate for multi-agent, multi-tenant LLM memory in production. Robust shared memory requires explicit, carefully designed systems-level abstractions around scope, time, provenance, and policy, and live evaluation is essential to reveal enforcement gaps and pipeline-ordering problems that are invisible in purely theoretical or design-level analysis.

Abstract: Multi-agent LLM environments require robust mechanisms for shared knowledge management. This paper formalizes the fleet-memory problem and identifies four foundational failure modes: unauthorized leakage, stale propagation, contradiction persistence, and provenance collapse. To address these, we define explicit systems-level primitives: scoped retrieval, temporal supersession, provenance tracking, and policy-governed memory propagation. These primitives are implemented in MemClaw, a production multi-tenant memory service, and evaluated via ArgusFleet, a reproducible harness testing four governance dimensions. Rather than a baseline comparison, this study measures a live production service, emphasizing real-world architectural insights and negative results. Key Evaluation Results Provenance: Successfully reconstructed 100% of depth-four derivation chains with correct writer identity at sub-second per-hop latency. Propagation: Demonstrated high intra-fleet visibility with zero cross-fleet leakage. Under strong write mode, write-to-visible latency was optimized to a single search round-trip. Production Architectural Issues Discovered Asymmetric Scope Enforcement: Tenant isolation held, but sub-tenant scope was initially bypassed on direct GET-by-id requests for agent-scoped credentials (disclosed and remediated during the study). Pipeline Ordering Conflict: While contradiction supersession works for admitted writes, a synchronous near-duplicate gate can prematurely reject contradictory writes before the asynchronous contradiction detector can evaluate them. Conclusion: Long-context retrieval alone is insufficient for production multi-agent memory. Governed shared memory demands explicit systems-level abstractions, and live evaluation is vital to expose enforcement and pipeline-ordering failures missed by design-only treatments.

### [112] [GUI vs. CLI: Execution Bottlenecks in Screen-Only and Skill-Mediated Computer-Use Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24551)
*Xiao Zhou,Siyue Zhang,Yilun Zhao,Jinbiao Wei,Tingyu Song,Arman Cohan,Chen Zhao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a controlled benchmark to fairly compare GUI-based and CLI-based computer-use agents on identical desktop tasks, revealing distinct bottlenecks for each modality and showing that CLI performance can surpass GUI when skills are sufficiently augmented.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of computer-use agents blur the comparison between graphical (GUI) and command-line (CLI) interaction because they differ in tasks, initial states, verification methods, and allowed actions. This makes it unclear whether performance differences arise from the interaction modality itself or from experimental confounds. The authors aim to create a fair, matched benchmark that isolates the effect of interaction modality and uncovers where GUI vs CLI agents actually struggle.

Method: They build a benchmark of 440 desktop tasks spanning 18 applications and 12 workflow categories. For each task, they define a shared goal, initial state, and final-state verifier, then create two matched execution settings: (1) screen-only GUI agents that interact purely via graphical actions, and (2) skill-mediated CLI agents that interact via programmatic command interfaces (“skills”). Both are restricted to actions native to their modality but otherwise face identical conditions. They evaluate strong baseline GUI and CLI agents, then introduce verifier-guided skill augmentation for CLI, where observed failures and verifier feedback guide the creation or refinement of CLI skills to improve coverage of needed actions.

Result: In the controlled setting, the best GUI agent achieves a 59.1% full task pass rate, while the best original (unaugmented) CLI agent achieves 48.2%. After applying verifier-guided skill augmentation, the CLI success rate increases to 69.3%. This indicates that the apparent underperformance of CLI agents is largely due to incomplete or insufficiently expressive skill interfaces rather than fundamental limitations in the model’s capability to operate via CLI.

Conclusion: GUI and CLI agents face fundamentally different execution bottlenecks. GUI agents are primarily constrained by the difficulty of maintaining reliable, grounded interaction over long, multi-step workflows in visual environments. CLI agents, in contrast, are mainly constrained by the breadth, design, and maintainability of their skill interfaces; when these are expanded and refined using verifier feedback, CLI performance can not only close the gap with but exceed GUI performance. This implies that progress on computer-use agents will require modality-specific improvements: better grounding and long-horizon control for GUI, and better skill coverage and scalable skill engineering for CLI.

Abstract: Computer-use agents can execute software tasks through either graphical interfaces or programmatic command interfaces, but existing evaluations confound interaction modality with differences in tasks, initial states, verifiers, and permitted actions. We introduce a matched execution-layer benchmark of 440 desktop tasks across 18 applications and 12 workflow categories, where screen-only GUI agents and skill-mediated CLI agents receive identical goals, states, and final-state verifiers while being restricted to modality-native actions. In this controlled setting, the strongest GUI agent reaches a 59.1% full pass rate, outperforming the strongest original-skill CLI agent at 48.2%; however, verifier-guided skill augmentation raises CLI success to 69.3%, showing that much of the CLI deficit comes from incomplete skill coverage rather than model capability alone. These results suggest that GUI and CLI expose different execution bottlenecks: GUI agents are limited by reliable grounded interaction over long-horizon workflows, whereas CLI agents are limited by the coverage and scalability of their skill interfaces.

### [113] [Quant Convergence: Bridging Classical Value Investing and Modern Factor Models for Systematic Equity Selection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24575)
*Augusto Eiji Yamazaki,Hugo Garrido-Lestache Belinchon*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper tests whether Benjamin Graham-style value investing rules, used as a feature “filter,” can improve the robustness and risk profile of modern machine learning stock selection models compared to using complex factors alone.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern ML models in finance are powerful but prone to overfitting and chasing short-term noise, which can lead to fragile portfolios that crash in adverse markets. The authors want to see if classic, conservative value-investing principles—specifically Graham’s “margin of safety”—can be formalized as a low-pass filter on features to constrain these models and reduce tail risk.

Method: Using 20 years of S&P 500 data, the authors construct three feature sets: (1) pure Benjamin Graham rule-based value metrics, (2) modern factor-style market features, and (3) a hybrid combining both. They then train complex ML models (XGBoost, AutoGluon, Random Forest variants) on these feature sets and evaluate them through a strict buy-and-hold stock selection strategy over a four-year out-of-sample period (March 2022–March 2026). Performance is assessed via returns, drawdowns, and risk-adjusted metrics such as the Calmar Ratio.

Result: AutoGluon, using complex features, achieves high cumulative returns (222.68%) but suffers a large maximum drawdown (39.78%) due to concentrated exposure to volatile tech stocks ahead of a crash. In contrast, a Random Forest model based solely on Graham-style value features delivers the highest return (232.13%) while maintaining a superior risk profile (Calmar Ratio 1.38). A Random Forest trained on the combined feature set generates strong returns (202.91%) and achieves the lowest maximum drawdown (34.53%) among all tested models, showing a better balance between momentum and value-based risk control.

Conclusion: Graham-inspired value rules, when encoded as input features or filters, can materially improve the robustness of modern ML-driven stock selection by constraining risk-taking and reducing vulnerability to market crashes. The study suggests that the traditional “margin of safety” concept is not obsolete but functions as an effective risk-control mechanism for complex AI models, often leading to better or comparable returns with significantly lower downside risk.

Abstract: Modern finance relies heavily on complex machine learning models to find patterns in the stock market. However, as these AI models get more complicated, they often memorize short-term market noise instead of finding companies with real, lasting value. We designed this research to test if Benjamin Graham's classic value investing rules could act as a mathematical "low-pass filter" to keep these modern models in check. We built three different sets of features - pure Graham rules, modern market factors, and a mix of both - and tested them against highly complex models (XGBoost and AutoGluon) using 20 years of S&P 500 data. By applying a strict buy-and-hold strategy over a four-year test period (March 2022 to March 2026), the results showed that more complex algorithms do not always win. While the AutoGluon model captured high returns (222.68%), it suffered a substantial 39.78% drop because it bought volatile tech stocks right before the market crashed. On the other hand, the pure Graham Random Forest achieved the highest overall return (232.13%) with much less risk (1.38 Calmar Ratio). Furthermore, the Combined Random Forest successfully mixed momentum with Graham's rules, making a 202.91% return while keeping the lowest maximum drop (34.53%) of any model tested. Ultimately, this research proves that Graham's "margin of safety" isn't outdated; it is actually a highly effective way to prevent modern AI from taking on too much risk.

### [114] [LLMs Prompted for Legal Context Object More: Overrefusal from Small On-Premises LLMs in Criminal Legal Context](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24585)
*Anastasiia Kucherenko,François Brouchoud,Dimitri Percia David,Andrei Kucharavy*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors evaluate several contemporary small LLMs likely to be deployed on-device. They systematically compare refusal rates on legal prompts under different contextual framings, including no prefix, authoritative institutional role prefixes (e.g., acting for a supreme court or as a defense lawyer), and a known role-play jailbreak prefix, then measure changes in refusal frequency.

Result: Authority-style role prefixes dramatically increase refusal rates, by factors of roughly 2–20 compared with the same prompts without such prefixes. The role-play jailbreak prefix has inconsistent effects across models, sometimes greatly increasing refusal rates and sometimes having little impact.

The authors argue that this instability and overrefusal problem requires further research and mitigation to reduce bias risks in legal applications.

Abstract: While the validity of LLMs' use in the legal context remains subject to ethical and legal debate, legal professionals are already experimenting with personal LLMs, if only for translation and reformulation. However, even such a seemingly innocuous use can introduce biases through case processing speed if LLM assistants selectively refuse assistance on certain topics. To better anticipate such biases, we investigate several modern small LLMs that are most likely to be used as on-device assistants, to assess the impact of overrefusal on legal prompts.
  Surprisingly, we find that authority-style prefixes (``you are acting as an assistant of the national supreme court'', ``[...] defense lawyer'') systematically increase refusal rates by 2--20x over the no-prefix baseline, while a known role-play jailbreak prefix shows mixed effects, sharply increasing refusals in some models and barely shifting them in others. ### [115] [AdversaBench: Automated LLM Red-Teaming with Multi-Judge Confirmation and Cross-Model Transferability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24589)
*Khanak Khandelwal*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces AdversaBench, an automated red-teaming pipeline that generates and validates adversarial prompts to systematically evaluate large language model failures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As large language models grow in capability, manually red-teaming them to uncover failures does not scale. There is a need for a systematic, automated way to generate difficult adversarial prompts and to reliably verify whether model responses truly constitute failures, across multiple task types such as reasoning, instruction-following, and tool use.

Method: The authors design AdversaBench, an end-to-end pipeline that: (1) starts from seed prompts in different categories; (2) applies five structured mutation operators to generate adversarial variants; (3) queries a target model with these variants; and (4) uses a three-judge panel of models plus a meta-judge as a tiebreaker to automatically label outputs as failures or not. They run experiments on 45 seed prompts across reasoning, instruction-following, and tool-use categories, measuring operator effectiveness, required attacker iterations, judge agreement statistics, and cross-model transfer of adversarial prompts.

Result: All 45 seed prompts generated at least one confirmed failure. Operator performance differed markedly by category: the inject_distractor operator was ineffective on instruction-following seeds (0.00 mean reward) but highly effective on reasoning and tool-use seeds (0.80–0.83). Instruction-following attacks took more iterations (2.4 vs. 1.1 for other categories), revealing higher difficulty in survival curve analysis. Judge pairs agreed 80–87% of the time, but Cohen’s kappa was near zero due to skewed label distributions, indicating that raw agreement rates by category are more useful. Adversarial prompts crafted against Llama 3.1 8B transferred effectively in a zero-shot manner to Llama 3.3 70B, showing cross-model generality.

Conclusion: AdversaBench provides a scalable, automated framework for adversarially evaluating large language models, showing that every tested seed prompt can yield at least one real, automatically confirmed failure. The study highlights how attack operator effectiveness is task-dependent, why naive binary failure rates can obscure difficulty, and how evaluation metrics like Cohen’s kappa can mislead under label skew. The successful zero-shot transfer of adversarial prompts to larger models suggests that the framework uncovers general behavioral vulnerabilities rather than quirks of a specific model, supporting AdversaBench as a useful benchmark and tool for red-teaming LLMs.

Abstract: Scaling adversarial evaluation of large language models requires both a method for generating hard inputs and a reliable way to confirm that resulting failures are real. We present AdversaBench, an end-to-end red-teaming pipeline that mutates seed prompts with five structured operators, queries a target model, and confirms failures through a three-judge panel with a meta-judge tiebreaker. We report experiments on 45 seeds across three categories: reasoning, instruction-following, and tool use. Every seed produced a confirmed failure. Four findings stand out. First, operator effectiveness varies sharply by category: inject_distractor scores 0.00 mean reward on instruction-following seeds but 0.80-0.83 on reasoning and tool-use. Second, binary failure rate hides difficulty: instruction-following seeds required 2.4 attacker iterations on average versus 1.1 for other categories, a gap visible in survival curves. Third, pairwise judge agreement of 80-87% coexists with near-zero Cohen's kappa due to label skew; category-level disagreement rates are more informative. Fourth, adversarial prompts generated against Llama 3.1 8B transfer zero-shot to Llama 3.3 70B, suggesting the mutations exploit general behavioral patterns rather than model-specific weaknesses. Code, dataset, and analysis scripts are available at https://github.com/khanak0509/AdversaBench .

</details>


### [116] [ASALT: Adaptive State Alignment for Lateral Transfer in Multi-agent Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24601)

Method: ASALT introduces observation-level and state-level adapter networks that map target-domain observations and global states into a shared embedding space compatible with the source domain. The adapters generate embeddings that align heterogeneous domains, making it possible to transfer strategies even when the raw dimensions differ.

Result: On several configurations of standard MARL benchmark environments, ASALT outperforms existing baselines in terms of sample efficiency and overall return in cooperative tasks. The experiments also show that the benefit of ASALT depends on how different the source and target domains are, and that ASALT is less prone to negative transfer compared to other methods when observation and action spaces differ.

Conclusion: ASALT enables strategy transfer in MARL between domains with mismatched state and observation dimensionalities by using learned adapter modules to create a shared embedding space. This improves performance and sample efficiency in cooperative settings and reduces negative transfer, although its effectiveness is sensitive to the degree of mismatch between domains.

Abstract: Multi-agent reinforcement learning (MARL) addresses the problem of training multiple agents that pursue collaborative, competitive, or mixed objectives. Prior work has investigated transfer learning between source and target domains in MARL; however, the majority of existing approaches impose the constraint that the dimensionalities of the observation space and the global state space must be identical across domains. In this paper, we introduce a method that explicitly accommodates mismatched state-space dimensionalities between source and target domains. The proposed approach, ASALT, incorporates both observation-level and state-level adapters that map the target-domain observations and global states into a shared embedding space, thereby enabling more effective transfer of knowledge across both actors and critics. These adapters can generate embeddings that support efficient strategy transfer across heterogeneous domains. Experimental results on multiple configurations in standard benchmark environments demonstrate that ASALT surpasses existing baselines in terms of sample efficiency and global return in cooperative settings, but its effectiveness depends on the degree of mismatch between source and target domains. Furthermore, our findings indicate that ASALT mitigates negative transfer, which frequently constitutes a major obstacle when transferring policies between domains with differing observation and action spaces.

### [117] [Uncertainty-Aware Longitudinal Forecasting of Alzheimer's Disease Progression Using Deep Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24604)
*Arya Hariharan,Shreyank N Gowda,Anala M R*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A deep learning framework models multi-year Alzheimer’s disease progression with probabilistic trajectories and explicit uncertainty decomposition, outperforming common baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinicians need models that forecast how an individual with Alzheimer’s disease will evolve over multiple future visits, along with how reliable those forecasts are, instead of just a one-step, point diagnosis. Existing deep learning methods mostly treat diagnosis as a flat, single-step classification task and provide little insight into ordered disease stages, longer-term trajectories, or how uncertainty grows over time.

Method: The paper introduces a probabilistic deep learning framework combining three pieces: (1) an ordinal diagnosis predictor based on a Temporal Fusion Transformer (TFT) encoder with a CORAL ordinal output head, asymmetric loss weighting, and converter oversampling to enforce disease-stage ordering and increase sensitivity to MCI-to-dementia transitions; (2) an autoregressive Mixture Density Network conditioned on the TFT patient-context representation to generate five-year probabilistic trajectories for diagnosis and key clinical/biomarker measures (CDR-SB, MMSE orientation, hippocampal volume); and (3) decomposed uncertainty estimation, using analytical mixture variance for aleatoric uncertainty and a five-member bootstrap ensemble of encoders to capture epistemic uncertainty. The framework is trained and evaluated primarily on ADNI and externally validated on OASIS-3.

Result: On the ADNI dataset, the model surpasses linear, recurrent, and transformer baselines for next-visit diagnosis prediction, especially for discriminating MCI from dementia. The generated trajectories show near-nominal coverage of 90% credible intervals, with interval widths increasing appropriately over longer forecast horizons, and the predicted biomarker dynamics match expected patterns of Alzheimer’s progression. The bootstrap ensemble yields strong diversity in encoder representations and a clear epistemic uncertainty signal, which is elevated for rare progression patterns, for patients in MCI or dementia stages, and for subjects in the external OASIS-3 validation, where higher epistemic uncertainty correlates with greater prediction error.

Conclusion: A unified probabilistic, ordinal, and trajectory-focused framework can more accurately and transparently model Alzheimer’s disease progression over multiple years compared to standard baselines. By generating multi-horizon trajectories for diagnoses and biomarkers while explicitly separating aleatoric from epistemic uncertainty, the approach offers clinically relevant forecasts and reliability estimates, particularly useful in challenging or out-of-distribution cases such as rare progression archetypes and external cohorts.

Abstract: Longitudinal modelling of Alzheimer's disease progression is clinically useful only if it can describe not just the most likely next diagnosis, but how a patient may evolve over time and how reliable that forecast is. Most deep learning approaches reduce this problem to single-step classification, treating cognitively normal, mild cognitive impairment, and dementia as flat categories while providing limited insight into how uncertainty accumulates across future visits. We propose a probabilistic framework that combines ordinal diagnosis prediction, multi-horizon trajectory generation, and decomposed uncertainty estimation. A Temporal Fusion Transformer encoder is adapted with a CORAL ordinal output layer, asymmetric loss weighting, and converter oversampling to respect disease-stage ordering and improve sensitivity to MCI-to-dementia transitions. Conditioned on the learned patient-context representation, an autoregressive Mixture Density Network generates five-year probabilistic trajectories for diagnosis state, CDR Sum of Boxes, MMSE orientation, and hippocampal volume. On ADNI, the model outperforms linear, recurrent, and transformer baselines for next-visit diagnosis prediction, with the strongest gains on MCI-versus-dementia discrimination. Generated trajectories achieve near-nominal 90% credible interval coverage, widening uncertainty across the forecast horizon, and biomarker dynamics consistent with expected Alzheimer's disease progression. We further separate aleatoric from epistemic uncertainty using analytic mixture variance and a five-member bootstrap ensemble, which provides the strongest encoder diversity and output-level epistemic signal. Epistemic uncertainty is higher for rare progression archetypes, MCI and dementia patients, and under external evaluation on OASIS-3, where it increases alongside prediction error.

### [118] [ScaleToT: Generalizing Structured LLM Reasoning for Billion-Scale Low-Activity User Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24605)
*Tianbao Ma,Chang Xi,Yichuan Zou,Chengen Li,Linxun Chen,Zilong Lu,Yanan Niu,Zhaojie Liu,Han Li,Kun Gai*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ScaleToT is a framework that distills structured, Tree-of-Thought-style reasoning from a small LLM-processed subset of users into a lightweight model, enabling accurate user modeling for billions of low-activity users at low cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Rich interaction histories are usually needed for accurate user modeling, but many users are low-activity and only have sparse static profiles. Directly using LLMs to reason over all users would be too expensive, and LLM reasoning over sparse profiles can be unreliable. The paper aims to get the benefits of LLM-style structured reasoning for low-activity users at billion-scale while remaining computationally feasible and reliable.

Method: The authors introduce ScaleToT, a pipeline with three main stages. First, they run a small LLM on a subset of users to generate structured, typed user-state reasoning chains using a bounded entropy-guided Tree-of-Thought (ToT) refinement process, improving reliability of the inferred latent states. Second, they treat these curated chains as supervision to train a student model on static profiles via supervised fine-tuning and a custom Outcome-Driven Segment-Aware Implicit Reward Policy Optimization (OSIPO) procedure, so the student learns to reproduce useful reasoning from sparse input. Third, they transfer the student’s reasoning representations into a lightweight profile encoder that can be deployed across the entire user base, providing shared reasoning signals to downstream tasks without per-user LLM inference.

Result: In a real-world billion-scale advertising system focused on lifetime value (LTV) prediction, ScaleToT is evaluated both offline and online. Offline, only 7.32% of the user population needs LLM-based reasoning to train the system, yet the learned reasoning signals generalize to the remaining users. In an online randomized A/B test, the method yields a 6.738% increase in 30-day lifetime value (LT30) compared to the baseline, demonstrating both significant business impact and large savings in compute relative to performing LLM reasoning for all users.

Conclusion: ScaleToT shows that structured Tree-of-Thought reasoning from LLMs can be distilled and scaled to billions of low-activity users in a cost-effective way. By combining entropy-bounded ToT for reliable teacher chains, SFT and OSIPO for training a student reasoner, and a lightweight profile encoder for wide deployment, the framework achieves substantial gains in LTV prediction with limited LLM usage. This suggests a practical path to leveraging LLM-style reasoning in large-scale user modeling and recommendation without prohibitive inference costs.

Abstract: Accurate user modeling often depends on rich interaction histories, which are unavailable for billions of low-activity users. Large Language Models (LLMs) can infer latent user states from static profiles, but this reasoning becomes unreliable when profiles are sparse, and applying an LLM to billions of users is prohibitively expensive. We present ScaleToT, which learns structured reasoning from a small LLM-processed subset and extends it to the broader low-activity user population. To improve reasoning reliability, ScaleToT constructs typed user-state chains with a bounded entropy-guided Tree-of-Thought (ToT) refinement procedure. To make this structured reasoning usable from sparse profiles, the teacher-curated chains are used to train a student model on static profiles through supervised fine-tuning (SFT) and Outcome-Driven Segment-Aware Implicit Reward Policy Optimization (OSIPO). ScaleToT then transfers the student's reasoning representations to a lightweight profile encoder, providing shared reasoning signals for the remaining users without LLM inference. We evaluate ScaleToT on lifetime value (LTV) prediction in a billion-scale advertising deployment. A randomized online A/B test increased LT30 by 6.738\%, while offline reasoning covered only 7.32\% of the potential population, greatly reducing compute cost compared with full-population reasoning.

We connect token-level technical costs to workflow-level production functions, enterprise resource allocation, measurement and instrumentation methods, and emerging market-design questions. The framework shows that token expenditure and economic value are distinct: value depends on marginal productivity, workflow position, hidden reasoning activity, risk, and downstream propagation effects. The paper concludes by identifying open research directions in hidden-token measurement, empirical calibration, token productivity, dynamic allocation, and token-based markets.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In OBDA, users should query at the ontology level instead of directly over heterogeneous data sources. However, not every database query can be perfectly represented at the ontology level. There is a need to understand how to systematically derive the best approximations of data queries at the ontology level: abstractions that are as complete as possible or as sound as possible, within a reasonable query language and complexity.

Method: The authors work in an OBDA framework with existential rules and certain-answer semantics. Importantly, this added expressiveness does not increase the complexity of the main reasoning and computation problems considered. They further provide a characterization of maximally sound abstractions via a novel connection to maximum recovery in data exchange.

Conclusion: The proposed extension of UCQs is expressive enough to capture minimally complete and, when possible, perfect abstractions in OBDA without incurring additional complexity. Moreover, maximally sound abstractions can be understood and characterized through concepts from data exchange, specifically maximum recovery. This contributes to a more robust and theoretically grounded framework for query abstraction in ontology-based data access.

Abstract: In ontology-based data access (OBDA), multiple data sources are integrated via mappings to an ontology. We consider an OBDA setting based on existential rules and the certain answer semantics. We address the recent issue of query abstraction, which consists of abstracting data queries by translating them to the ontology layer. Since a perfect abstraction may not exist, the notions of minimally complete and maximally sound abstractions have been introduced.
  We study abstractions within an extension of UCQs with a limited form of inequality and a special predicate marking database constants. While this extension does not lead to an increased complexity of the problems of interest, it is able to express minimally complete abstractions, hence perfect abstractions when they exist. We also characterize maximally sound abstractions by making a new connection with the notion of maximum recovery stemming from data exchange.

Conclusion: SAFARI demonstrates that active, tool-augmented investigation with externalized memory can effectively diagnose agent failures in extremely long trajectories, breaking the dependence of evaluation accuracy on LLM context length and outperforming existing methods under tight token budgets and in settings where the fault is far outside the native context window.

Abstract: As autonomous agents tackle increasingly complex multi-step, multi-agent tasks, their execution trajectories have scaled beyond the constraints of even the largest context windows. Current methods for effectively diagnosing agent failures load the full trajectory into an LLM's context window, which suffers from attention dilution and fails when agentic traces inevitably exceed context limits. To address this, we introduce SAFARI (Scaling long-horizon Agentic Fault AttRibution via active Investigation), a framework that replaces linear context loading with a tool-augmented diagnostic loop. By equipping LLMs with a specialized toolbox to read and search trajectory segments alongside a persistent Short-Term Memory (STM) for cross-turn reasoning, SAFARI effectively decouples diagnostic accuracy from architectural context limits. Our experiments demonstrate that SAFARI outperforms state-of-the-art results by 20% on the Who&When dataset within a 1M token budget, and by 19% on TRAIL GAIA subset on a 25K token budget. Most significantly, SAFARI maintains a 0.58 precision even when the target fault resides 5x beyond the model's native context window, a scenario where traditional evaluators fail entirely.

The authors aim to leverage LLM knowledge to improve online RL in a more robust and practical way, avoiding the unreliability of having the LLM directly output environment actions.

Method: LaGO treats the LLM as a latent action prior that influences, but does not dictate, the agent’s policy during online reinforcement learning. Instead of generating explicit actions, the LLM provides guidance in a latent space that softly shapes policy optimization (e.g., on top of PPO), allowing the learned policy to deviate when beneficial. Conclusion: Embedding LLMs as latent action priors is an effective way to exploit their knowledge for online reinforcement learning and planning, without relying on them as fragile explicit controllers. The consistent performance gains and the correlation between LLM strength and guidance quality suggest that better language models can systematically enhance RL-based decision-making.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have shown strong potential for planning and sequential decision-making, but prior work often relies on using them as direct controllers, which requires precise action generation and can be unreliable in practice. This paper proposes Latent Action Guidance for Online Reinforcement Learning (LaGO), a framework that uses a pretrained LLM as a latent action prior to softly guide online policy optimization, rather than treating the LLM as an explicit planner or controller. Experiments on both a discrete-control benchmark, CLEVR-Robot, and a continuous-control benchmark, Meta-World, demonstrate that LaGO consistently improves both reward and success rate over Vanilla PPO. In particular, LaGO increases the average success rate from 15.1% to 27.2% on CLEVR-Robot and from 2.7% to 15.2% on Meta-World. Our analysis further shows that stronger pretrained LLMs provide more effective guidance, suggesting that LLM knowledge can improve planning and online decision-making.
