### [126] [Cost-Optimal Decision Diagrams for Stochastic Boolean Function Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24672)
*Xia Zong,Tuomo Lehtonen,Jussi Rintanen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They design and analyze an algorithm that, given different costs for checking variables and probabilities for their truth values, evaluates propositional formulas in a cost-optimal way, proving strong complexity results and showing practical performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In real-world decision-making (e.g., diagnostics, testing, or information-gathering), different pieces of information cost different amounts to obtain, and we often know how likely different situations are. When evaluating logical conditions (propositional formulas) under such heterogeneous costs and probabilities, we want strategies that minimize the expected information-gathering cost. Existing work lacked a general, exact, and practical algorithm for constructing such optimal evaluation strategies.

Method: They formalize the problem of evaluating a propositional formula when each variable has an associated observation cost and there is a probability distribution over all truth assignments. They then design a deterministic evaluation strategy using a branch-and-bound search that systematically explores possible variable-query orders. The algorithm uses variable-selection heuristics to guide the search, pruning rules to cut off provably suboptimal branches, and caching to avoid recomputation of equivalent subproblems. They also implement a greedy beam-search approximation for improved scalability. Finally, they conduct experiments on random instances and a structured heart-disease diagnosis example, and they provide complexity-theoretic analysis proving the problem is #P-hard and in PSPACE.

Result: The branch-and-bound algorithm is shown to be the first practical exact method that handles general propositional formulas with arbitrary variable costs and a given probability distribution over truth assignments. Experiments on random and structured instances demonstrate that the method scales to nontrivial problem sizes. The greedy beam-search variant illustrates a controllable trade-off between computational effort and solution quality. On the theoretical side, they establish that the optimal-evaluation problem is #P-hard but does not exceed PSPACE in complexity.

Conclusion: Optimal evaluation of propositional formulas under heterogeneous information costs and probabilistic beliefs is computationally challenging but tractable with a carefully designed exact algorithm for many practical instances. Their branch-and-bound method, augmented with heuristics, pruning, and caching, provides a usable tool, while the beam-search variant offers scalable approximations. The complexity-theoretic results clarify the inherent difficulty of the problem by placing it between #P-hardness and PSPACE containment.

Abstract: In many decision-making scenarios, acquiring information incurs different costs. We consider the problem of constructing a deterministic evaluation strategy that minimizes the expected cost of evaluating a propositional formula under variable costs and a probability distribution over truth assignments. We present a branch-and-bound algorithm with variable-selection heuristics, pruning, and caching. To the best of our knowledge, it is the first practical exact algorithm for this level of generality. Experiments on random instances demonstrate scalability and quantify the efficiency-quality trade-off of a greedy beam-search variant. We additionally evaluate a structured heart-disease diagnosis instance. Finally, we prove that the problem is $\#P$-hard and contained in $\mathrm{PSPACE}$.

</details>


### [127] [Decentralised AI Training and Inference with BlockTrain](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24722)
*Peter Toth*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces Spheroid BlockTrain, a decentralized protocol that trains different blocks of a model across distributed workers and then composes them at inference, achieving near end-to-end Transformer performance while reducing dependency on large centralized accelerator clusters.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Frontier AI currently depends on dense, centralized accelerator clusters controlled by large organizations, which disadvantages open or independent efforts and ties progress to scarce capital and specific data-center locations. The authors want a training approach that can leverage more heterogeneous, geographically distributed compute without requiring any single party to host or optimize a full model.

Method: They partition a model into independently trainable blocks, each worker training only one block on a local objective derived from a shared global target. Blocks do not keep or access full-model optimizer states. After training, the blocks are composed into a single model for inference. They test this on byte-level WikiText, conduct shared block-worker runs where multiple workers train the same block and their updates are averaged, and run real HTTP/TCP experiments that move serialized checkpoints and updates across public IP hosts. They also design an inference path that traverses the stack of blocks once per full output sequence, sending data over TCP among multiple public-network GPU hosts.

Result: On byte-level WikiText, BlockTrain achieves cross entropy 1.359 (perplexity 3.89), within roughly 0.04 cross-entropy of a same-setup end-to-end Transformer baseline, while each worker only trains one block. A configuration with six workers sharing block training achieves CE 1.385 using averaged same-block updates. Their HTTP/TCP experiments show a three-host public-IP run can improve CE from 5.580 to 1.811 while transferring 15.22 GB of model state and updates. For inference, the system can compose up to a logical 75.80B-parameter fp16 model spread across three public-network GPU hosts and outperforms a comparable plain autoregressive TCP pipeline because it emits an entire sequence per wide-area-network traversal instead of token-by-token.

Conclusion: Decentralizing training by block-partitioning models and using local objectives can closely match the performance of conventional end-to-end Transformer training while reducing the need for centralized, monolithic accelerator clusters. The protocol works over real-world HTTP/TCP links and scales to large logical model sizes across multiple public GPU hosts, with an inference design that is more WAN-efficient than naive token-wise pipelines. This suggests a viable pathway for more open, geographically distributed, and resource-flexible frontier model training and serving.

Abstract: Frontier AI training is increasingly shaped by access to dense, centrally controlled accelerator clusters. This creates a structural advantage for hyperscalers and large centralized laboratories, and makes open or independent AI efforts depend on scarce capital, privileged infrastructure, and data-center geography. We present Spheroid BlockTrain, a decentralized training protocol in which a model is partitioned into independently trainable blocks, each optimized on a local objective derived from the same global target and composed at inference into one model. On byte-level WikiText, BlockTrain reaches cross entropy 1.359 (perplexity 3.89), within about 0.04 CE of a same-setup end-to-end Transformer reference, while each active worker trains only one block and avoids full-model optimizer state. A shared six-worker block training run reaches CE 1.385 by averaging same-block updates into one assembled model. HTTP/TCP transport experiments move real serialized checkpoints and updates, including a public-IP three-host run that improves CE from 5.580 to 1.811 while moving 15.22 GB. For inference, the current BlockTrain path uses one block-stack traversal per full output and serves over direct TCP across three public-network GPU hosts up to a 75.80B-parameter logical fp16 shape, outperforming a matched plain-autoregressive TCP pipeline baseline because it emits a full sequence per WAN pipeline traversal rather than one token per traversal.

### [128] [Scaling Laws for Task-Specific LLM Distillation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24747)
*Lavinia Ghita,Dhruv Desai,Ioana Boier*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Empirical scaling laws for compressing domain-specific LLMs, studied in quantitative finance, showing how data, compression, supervision, and pruning schedule affect in-domain vs general knowledge, and proposing a CoT-based distillation that mitigates knowledge loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are powerful but expensive and slow, especially problematic in latency- and cost-sensitive domains like quantitative finance. Practitioners need principled guidance on how much they can compress models for a specific domain, what performance tradeoffs to expect (in-domain vs general knowledge), and which distillation/pruning strategies best preserve needed capabilities.

Method: Use quantitative finance as a testbed to empirically study scaling laws for domain-specific LLM compression. Perform iterative structural pruning combined with either logit-based or LoRA-based distillation. Introduce a blended chain-of-thought (CoT) supervision loss designed to stabilize KL-based distillation over reasoning traces. Systematically vary dataset size, compression ratio, supervision format, and pruning schedule, and measure effects on both in-domain and general knowledge benchmarks.

Result: They obtain empirical scaling laws describing how in-domain task performance and general-knowledge performance change under different levels of compression and supervision. In-domain quality degrades smoothly and predictably as compression increases, while performance on general-knowledge benchmarks collapses much earlier. Supervision format, especially CoT-based supervision, is shown to be the dominant factor governing the tradeoff: CoT supervision can actively restore general knowledge capabilities that pruning would otherwise remove.

Conclusion: Domain-specific LLMs can be aggressively compressed for latency- and cost-sensitive applications with predictable in-domain performance loss, but this comes at a disproportionate cost to general knowledge unless supervision is carefully designed. Chain-of-thought-based distillation is a powerful lever to recover general knowledge erased by pruning. The released FinHeadlineMix dataset, scaling-law analyses, and recommendations form a reusable framework to guide compression decisions in other specialized domains.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) achieve strong performance across a growing range of domains, yet their scale poses deployment challenges in applications where latency and cost constraints are critical. This paper derives empirical scaling laws for domain-specific LLM compression, quantifying how in-domain and general knowledge performance scale with dataset size, compression ratio, supervision format, and iterative pruning schedule. Using quantitative finance as our application domain, we compare logit-based and LoRA-based distillation under iterative structural pruning, introducing a blended chain-of-thought supervision loss that stabilizes KL-divergence distillation over reasoning traces. In-domain task quality degrades predictably under compression while general-knowledge benchmarks collapse well before the same point; supervision format is the key driver of this tradeoff, with chain-of-thought supervision actively recovering general knowledge that pruning erases. We release the headline dataset FinHeadlineMix, scaling law results, and practical recommendations to provide a reusable framework for domain-specific compression decisions.

### [129] [Can Scale Save Us From Plasticity Loss in Large Language Models?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24752)
*J. Fernando Hernandez-Garcia,Tomás Figliolia,Beren Millidge*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While loss of plasticity (often manifesting as catastrophic forgetting or reduced ability to learn new tasks) has long been known in older, small neural networks, it is unclear whether this remains a serious issue in today’s large transformer-based LLMs trained on natural language. Given that LLMs are often expected to support continual or long-horizon training, understanding if and how plasticity degrades in this paradigm is crucial for designing scalable, continually trainable models.

Method: The authors train GPT-style Transformer models of various sizes (from 5M to 314M non-embedding parameters) on a multilingual continual learning problem, as well as in a stationary multilingual setting. They quantify plasticity loss using performance deterioration on a held-out Vietnamese probing task, tracking how well models can still adapt to new data after extended training. They then analyze how the onset of plasticity loss scales with model size, deriving an empirical scaling law.

Result: Across all model sizes, they observe evidence of plasticity loss: models become less able to efficiently learn new information after prolonged training. The onset of this degradation follows a predictable scaling law that increases sublinearly with model size, indicating that larger models postpone but do not eliminate plasticity loss. They additionally find plasticity loss even under stationary multilingual training, not only in the presence of explicit task switches.

Conclusion: Transformer language models, even when large and trained on natural-language data, inevitably suffer from loss of plasticity after sufficiently long training. Larger parameter counts delay but do not prevent this effect, and plasticity loss arises both in continual-learning setups with task changes and in stationary training. Therefore, simply scaling up model size is unlikely to solve plasticity loss; new methods will be needed to maintain long-term adaptability in large language models.

Abstract: The loss of plasticity - the ability of a network to learn new information after having already learned older information - is a fundamental challenge in creating artificial neural networks capable of continual learning. Although this phenomenon has been known for decades, it has mostly been studied in older, relatively small architectures and rarely in natural-language domains. To determine whether loss of plasticity remains a problem in the modern transformer-based LLM paradigm, we study plasticity loss in GPT-style Transformer models trained on a multilingual continual learning problem. Consistent with prior work, we find evidence of plasticity loss across models ranging from 5M to 314M non-embedding parameters, as measured by deterioration on a held-out Vietnamese probing task. We further find that the onset of plasticity loss follows a predictable scaling law, growing sublinearly with model size. These results suggest that larger models may delay the measurable effects of plasticity loss, but that increasing parameter count alone is likely to be insufficient to completely prevent it. We also find evidence of plasticity loss under stationary multilingual training, challenging the view that the phenomenon is exclusive to continual learning with abrupt task changes. Overall, our results suggest that even large Transformer language models trained on natural-language will eventually lose the ability to efficiently adapt to new data after sufficiently long training, in both continual and stationary settings.

Conclusion: Systems-level, C++/CUDA-native design can yield measurable improvements over mainstream frameworks for large-scale training, without sacrificing numerical fidelity. BluTrain demonstrates that exposing every stack layer to native tuning lets the framework raise its own performance ceiling while remaining architecture-general and relatively lightweight.

Abstract: Progress in deep learning is, at scale, more a matter of systems engineering than of modelling: the behaviour of a model in training (its throughput, its memory footprint, and the numerical fidelity of the result) is determined less by the architecture itself than by how that architecture is expressed on the hardware. To achieve absolute control over this hardware expression while abstracting away systems complexity to make modelling seamless and eliminating the need for repetitive orchestration logic, BluTrain was architected from first principles as a robust, lightweight, and architecture-general training framework in standard C++ and the core CUDA programming model. Every layer is implemented natively: a typed tensor module with reverse-mode autograd, a linear-algebra library, a caching allocator, a multi-mode distributed-execution module, and an MLIR-based deep-learning compiler. In formal evaluations training a 124M-parameter GPT-2 baseline in FP32 on an 8-GPU 6000 Ada system, BluTrain outperforms industry-standard baselines in both throughput (sustaining an average of 407K tokens/s versus PyTorch's 395K tokens/s) and memory efficiency (achieving up to a 22% footprint reduction), while strictly preserving numerical fidelity and converging to a marginally lower final validation loss. With every layer explicitly open to native tuning, the performance ceiling is the framework's own to raise.

### [132] [Solving Inverse Problems of Chaotic Systems with Bidirectional Conditional Flow Matching](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24824)

Main category: cs.AI

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Inverse problems in chaotic dynamics—inferring initial conditions from observed final states—are very important but remain largely unsolved due to ill-posedness, non-uniqueness, instability, and chaotic time-reversed behavior. Existing approaches struggle with exponential error growth and with enforcing physical conservation laws, limiting their applicability to realistic long-timescale systems like planetary dynamics and globular clusters.

Method: The authors propose Bidirectional Conditional Flow Matching (Bi-CFM), a generative modeling framework that learns two-way mappings between the distributions of initial and final states of a chaotic system. This bidirectional conditional flow is trained to model the stochastic relationship induced by chaotic evolution and to reduce error accumulation by operating at the distribution level rather than single trajectories. For systems with conservation laws, they introduce Conservation-constrained Bi-CFM (CBi-CFM), which incorporates constraints so that the learned mappings respect conserved quantities such as energy or angular momentum.

Result: On benchmark chaotic systems including Lorenz, an electronic Circuit system, and high-dimensional Lorenz 96, Bi-CFM outperforms baselines across five distribution-level evaluation metrics and is more than 100× faster. Applied to a three-body planet-planet scattering problem, the conservation-constrained variant CBi-CFM yields solutions that obey conservation laws with errors on par with ground-truth simulations. On real astronomical data from globular clusters—collisional million-body systems evolved over ~10 billion years—the method attains improved accuracy compared with existing approaches.

Conclusion: Learning bidirectional distribution mappings via Bi-CFM provides an effective and scalable way to solve inverse problems in chaotic dynamics, overcoming key issues of ill-posedness and instability while being computationally efficient. The conservation-constrained extension CBi-CFM allows the method to be applied to realistic physical systems where conservation laws are critical. Overall, this framework opens a practical path toward inferring initial conditions of long-timescale, real-world chaotic systems from observed final states.

Abstract: Modeling chaotic systems is crucial yet challenging. Inverse problems in chaotic dynamics, namely inferring initial conditions from final states, remain largely unsolved because of ill-posedness, non-uniqueness, instability, and potentially chaotic time-reverse dynamics. We address this open problem with Bidirectional Conditional Flow Matching (Bi-CFM), which learns bidirectional mappings between distributions of initial and final states to capture the stochasticity of chaotic evolution and mitigate exponential error accumulation over time. Furthermore, for systems with conservation laws, we extend it to Conservation-constrained Bi-CFM (CBi-CFM). Across the classic Lorenz, Circuit, and high-dimensional Lorenz 96 systems, Bi-CFM improves five distribution-level metrics over baselines while achieving a speedup of more than two orders of magnitude. In the three-body planet-planet scattering problem in planetary dynamics, CBi-CFM better respects conservation laws, with conservation errors comparable to those of the ground truth. Finally, on real observations of globular clusters, collisional million-body systems shaped by $\sim 10^{10}$ years (10 Gyr) of evolution, our method represents an advance in accuracy, establishing a scalable route to solving inverse problems of long-timescale real-world chaotic dynamics.

### [135] [Grading the Grader: Lessons from Evaluating an Agentic Data Analysis System](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24839)
*Tian Zheng,Kai-Tai Hsu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to reliably grade complex outputs from agentic data-analysis systems by building a layered human-AI grading pipeline.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Agentic data analysis systems produce complex, multi-modal outputs (code, numbers, text), making it hard to automatically and fairly compare them with ground truth without confusing true errors with grading artifacts. Current grading methods for LLMs are mainly for single-turn, simple responses and do not fit this richer setting. The authors want to understand and improve how well automated graders can evaluate such systems.

Method: They use LAMBDA, a multi-agent data-analysis system, on 153 numerical question-answering tasks from the DSGym QRData benchmark. They design a three-layer grading cascade: (1) a strict regex-based grader that checks extracted answers against ground truth, (2) an LLM-based lenient grader that can interpret approximate or formatted answers more flexibly, and (3) human inspection on selected snippets. They also introduce a keyword-anchored answer-extraction pipeline to improve the strict grader, and an iterative "nudge" mechanism that re-prompts the grader to obtain valid outputs, sometimes with and sometimes without re-injecting the original question, to study its effect.

Result: Both automated graders (strict and lenient) show 100% observed precision (no false positives among 70 checked cases). The lenient LLM grader reaches 97% recall relative to human labels. The keyword-anchored extraction boosts the strict grader's recall by 60 percentage points over a simple last-number heuristic, and the lenient grader does not depend on any specific parser. The iterative nudge mechanism increases successful grading runs from 36% to 97% and raises the share of answers passing the lenient grader from 16% to 46%. Re-injecting the original question during nudging provides no extra benefit, indicating the main effect is a template cue for answers. They also find that the metadata field "variable type" correlates most consistently with how the grading pipeline behaves and with observed grades.

Conclusion: A layered human-AI grading cascade can reliably evaluate complex outputs from data-analysis agents, achieving high precision and recall while mitigating different failure modes of strict and lenient graders. Careful answer-extraction design and iterative nudging dramatically improve grading robustness, and re-injecting the original question is unnecessary in this setup. Task metadata, especially variable type, plays a notable role in grading dynamics, suggesting future grading systems should be metadata-aware.

Abstract: Agentic data analysis systems produce rich outputs, including code, numerical results, and verbal diagnostics. This makes them more challenging to evaluate than single-turn LLM responses. It is therefore necessary to distinguish genuine disagreement between an agent's output and a ground-truth answer from grading artifacts. We investigate how reliably automated graders assess such a system and what strategies improve grading quality by applying LAMBDA, a multi-agent data-analysis system, on 153 numerical QRData tasks from DSGym. We develop and evaluate a three-layer human-AI grading cascade: strict regex matching, LLM-based lenient grading, and snippet-based human inspection, which combines non-GenAI and GenAI strategies with different failure profiles. Both automated graders achieve 100% observed precision (0/70 false positives). The lenient grader's recall is 97% against human labels. A keyword-anchored extraction pipeline raises the strict grader's recall by 60 percentage points over a last-number heuristic; the lenient grader is architecturally parser-independent. An iterative nudge mechanism raises grading run success from 36% to 97% and lenient-pass rates from 16% to 46%; comparing nudging with and without original-question re-injection shows that re-injection offers no benefit, confirming the nudge as an answer template cue. We further observe in this case study that variable type is the task metadata field most consistently associated with grading pipeline dynamics and observed outcome grades.

### [136] [Matching Tasks to Objectives: Fine-Tuning and Prompt-Tuning Strategies for Encoder-Decoder Pre-trained Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24841)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prompt-based learning is dominant, but the influence of different pre-training objectives and their alignment with downstream tasks—especially for commonsense knowledge retrieval and completion—is underexplored. Existing methods often use a single or mismatched objective across stages, which can limit performance, particularly in few-shot setups.

Method: Systematically investigate diverse pre-training objectives for encoder-decoder models and introduce the MTO (Match Task to Objective) framework. MTO provides automated ways to (1) infer which training objective best matches a target task, (2) prepare unlabeled task-related data for unsupervised adaptation using that objective, and (3) design fine-tuning templates whose formats are consistent with the chosen pre-training/adaptation objectives. The approach is also extended to prompt-tuning, with guidelines for constructing and optimizing soft prompts aligned to those objectives.

Result: When training and fine-tuning objectives are carefully matched to the task via MTO, encoder-decoder PLMs achieve over 120% relative performance gains versus conventional few-shot methods, and they surpass strong baselines even with full training data. The same alignment principles also yield substantial improvements for prompt-tuning methods.

Conclusion: Aligning objectives across pre-training, adaptation, fine-tuning, and prompt-tuning is critical for maximizing the performance of encoder-decoder PLMs. The proposed MTO framework and associated template/prompt design strategies provide a principled, automated way to choose and exploit the right objectives for a given task, leading to large gains in both few-shot and standard data regimes.

This study explores the impact of diverse pre-training objectives on the performance of encoder-decoder pre-trained language models across generation and question answering tasks, with a focus on commonsense knowledge retrieval and completion. We highlight the benefits of incorporating multiple objectives during both pre-training and fine-tuning stages. We introduce the Match Task to Objective (MTO) framework and methods for determining the appropriate objective for a given task. This framework offers automated methods to prepare task-related data for adaptation through unsupervised training, based on the identified objective. In the fine-tuning stage, we design novel templates that align with the objectives of the pre-training and adaptation stages. When aligned with task requirements, these strategies can achieve a performance gain of over 120\% compared to conventional methods in few-shot settings. They significantly outperform related works in few-shot settings and exceed the baseline even in full-dataset scenarios. Furthermore, we extend this approach to include prompt-tuning methodologies, providing guidance for more effective soft prompt engineering and optimization. Our strategies significantly enhance prompt-tuning performance as well. These insights hold substantial value, precisely guiding the selection and optimization of models customized for specific tasks. Code is available at https://github.com/puraminy/MTO/

</details>


### [137] [World Models in Pieces: Structural Certification for General Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24842)
*Yikai Lu,Yifei Wu,Xinyu Lu,Tongxin Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a new way to certify which parts of a general agent’s internal world model are reliable, instead of requiring it to perform well everywhere.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In complex “big-world” environments, a single agent cannot be universally competent at all possible situations, so classical worst-case guarantees become meaningless: they fail to distinguish critical, high-impact failures from benign ones. There is a need for a framework that can provide meaningful, local reliability guarantees about an agent’s internal model, especially for high-stakes, long-horizon planning.

Method: The authors formalize the limitation of universal capability by proving that general agents cannot be universally competent, which undermines standard worst-case analysis. They then propose structural certification, a transition-local framework that links bounded goal-conditioned performance to guarantees on individual entries of the agent’s internal world model. Constructively, they design algorithms that select or filter specific state transitions via deep compositional goals, and they prove that when an agent performs well on these goals, its learned world model is structurally accurate on those transitions with provable error bounds.

Result: They derive an error bound of order O(1/n) + O(δ) for the accuracy of the agent’s structural world model on certified transitions, where n typically relates to data or sample size and δ to a confidence or slack parameter. They also show that this bound is tight in the small-δ regime, and that such a regime is guaranteed to exist under their certification framework. Thus they obtain mathematically grounded, localized guarantees on model reliability.

Conclusion: The work shows that universal guarantees for general agents are impossible in big-world settings and that worst-case analysis is therefore uninformative. Instead, structural certification can provide precise, local guarantees on which transitions in the agent’s world model are trustworthy. This enables more reliable and certifiable deployment of general agents by identifying exactly where their long-horizon planning can be trusted, rather than demanding uniform performance across the entire environment.

Abstract: In the big-world regime, agents cannot be universally capable and their ability is inevitably specialized across a world model in pieces. Consequently, standard uniform guarantees fail to distinguish between the understanding of critical bottlenecks and irrelevant failures. We first formalize this limitation by proving that general agents are not universal, rendering standard worst-case analysis uninformative. To overcome this, we introduce structural certification, a transition-local framework that maps bounded goal-conditioned performance to entry-wise guarantees on the agent's internal world model. Our main contribution is constructive. We provide algorithms that filter specific transitions using deep compositional goals and prove that a general agent on these goals has a structural world model with a $\mathcal{O}(1/n) + \mathcal{O}(δ)$ error bound. Conversely, this bound is tight in the small-$δ$ regime, whose existence is explicitly guaranteed by our certification. These results enable the certifiable deployment of general agents by localizing the specific transitions where long-horizon planning is reliable.

</details>


### [138] [OpenThoughts-Agent: Data Recipes for Agentic Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.24855)
*Negin Raoof,Richard Zhuang,Marianna Nezhurina,Etash Guha,Atula Tejaswi,Ryan Marten,Charlie F. Ruan,Tyler Griggs,Alexander Glenn Shaw,Hritik Bansal,E. Kelly Buchanan,Artem Gazizov,Reinhard Heckel,Chinmay Hegde,Sankalp Jajee,Daanish Khazi,Emmanouil Koukoumidis,Xiangyi Li,Hange Liu,Shlok Natarajan,Harsh Raj,Nicholas Roberts,Ethan Shen,Nishad Singhi,Michael Siu,Ashima Suvarna,Hanwen Xing,Patrick Yubeaton,Robert Zhang,Leon Liangyu Chen,Xiaokun Chen,Steven Dillmann,Saadia Gabriel,Xunyi Jiang,Anurag Kashyap,Boxuan Li,Yein Park,Minh Pham,Sujay Sanghavi,Lin Shi,Ke Sun,Yixin Wang,Zhiwei Xu,Erica Zhang,Siyan Zhao,Wanjia Zhao,Jenia Jitsev,Alex Dimakis,Benjamin Feuer,Ludwig Schmidt*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: OpenThoughts-Agent introduces a fully open data curation pipeline and dataset for training broadly capable agentic language models, showing consistent gains over prior open efforts across multiple benchmarks and scales.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Public efforts on training data for agentic language models (models that act, tool-use, multi-step tasks) are fragmented and often optimized for a single benchmark, which limits generalization and makes it unclear how to systematically build training data that supports broad agentic capabilities. There is a need for an open, well-analyzed pipeline and dataset that reveal what data characteristics matter and that can serve as a foundation for community research.

Method: The authors design a multi-stage, fully open data curation pipeline for agent training, sourcing and filtering tasks to ensure diversity and quality. They run over 100 controlled ablation experiments varying components of this pipeline (e.g., task sources, diversity, filtering strategies) to quantify their contribution to downstream performance. Using the best-performing configuration, they construct a 100K-example training set and fine-tune the Qwen3-32B model, then benchmark it across seven different agentic evaluation suites and compare against existing open agentic datasets under compute-controlled conditions.

Result: A Qwen3-32B model fine-tuned on the 100K-example OT-Agent dataset achieves 44.8% average accuracy across seven agentic benchmarks, surpassing the strongest prior open-data agentic model, Nemotron-Terminal-32B, by 3.9 percentage points (40.9% → 44.8%). Ablation studies show that task source selection and diversity are key drivers of performance. Scaling analyses demonstrate that their curated data yields better performance than alternative open datasets at every dataset size considered, under matched compute budgets.

Conclusion: Carefully curated, diverse training data—constructed via a transparent, open pipeline—substantially improves the generalization of agentic language models across heterogeneous benchmarks. The OT-Agent pipeline and released datasets exhibit favorable scaling properties and outperform existing open alternatives at all tested scales. By open-sourcing the pipeline, datasets, experimental logs, and resulting models, the project provides a reproducible foundation and set of empirical insights for the community to build better agentic training regimes in the future.

Abstract: Agentic language models dramatically expand the applications of AI yet little is publicly known about how to curate training data for broadly capable agents. Existing open efforts such as SWE-Smith, SERA, and Nemotron-Terminal typically target a single benchmark, leaving open the question of how to train models that generalize across diverse agentic tasks. The OpenThoughts-Agent (OT-Agent) project addresses this gap with a fully open data curation pipeline for training agentic models. We conduct more than 100 controlled ablation experiments to systematically investigate each stage of the pipeline, yielding insights on the importance of task sources and diversity. We then assemble a training set of 100K examples from our pipeline and fine-tune Qwen3-32B on this dataset, which yields an average accuracy of 44.8% across seven agentic benchmarks and a 3.9 percentage point improvement over the strongest existing open data agentic model (Nemotron-Terminal-32B, 40.9%). Moreover, our training data exhibits strong scaling properties, outperforming alternative open datasets at every training set size in compute-controlled comparisons. We publicly release our training sets, data pipeline, experimental data, and models at openthoughts.ai to support future open research on agentic model training.

</details>
